THE RED PAGE.
And Victor Daley states, by request, his own
impression from the book concerning which critics’
impressions were collected in last issue —
Some time ago I published in, say, the Island
of Barataria a book of verses. I imagined that
when I received the first copy 1 would take it to
bed with me and sleep with it in my arms. I also
thought that as a recruit in the Army of Authors
I would be immensely proud of my tine new uni-
form of blue and gold. Moreover, I was firmly
convinced that in some mysterious way the publi-
cation of the book would make an epoch in my
life. None of these things happened. I opened
the pages of the first copy without even a flutter
of the heart. And then, with curiously mingled
feelings of pleasure and disappointment I read the
volume through from cover to cover. “Yes,” I
thought, “it is good, but not half so good as I
might have made it. ” Then I closed the book and
memory carried me back to the time when my first
contribution appeared in real type in a real paper.
Any reader of this page who, before he learnt to
swim, has waded breast-deep into the sea will re-
memember the queer catch of the breath and queer
sensation in the pit of the stomach he felt at the
time. This is, as nearly as possible, the feeling I
had when I read—in a fresh damp copy of th<
paper, you may be sure—a set of verses, which 1
had written out of my very own head, printed in
the columns of the — , a suburban journal
with a circulation of quite 300. I would have
broken a window—a large plate-glass window for
choice —if I could have paid for it, so exuberant
was my delight. And when I went back to my
boarding-house for breakfast I felt that a great
gulf had opened between me—a writer for the
press whose writings were printed, and no mistake
about the matter—and the common herd of fellow-
boarders, ' mere clerks who worked slavishly at
desks for- their living. It amuses me now to
recollect that in those irresponsible golden days of
youth I was really more miserable than I have
ever been in after years. The misery was, of
course, mostly artificial. I felt it to be my duty
to mourn a lot over such large abstract subjects as
Life and Fate and the Hereafter. I remember,
too, at that time coming across a book in which
Miirger’s sigh—“Ah ces beaux jours quand j’etais
si malheureux ” was quoted, and actually sighing
in sympathy. And I was 20 years old at the time
Speaking in all seriousness, however, I believe
that the thoughts of youth are nearer to the grave
than the thoughts of middle age.
* * * «
The Book did not affect me in this way. The
critics were all very kind with the exception of one
malignant worm who referred to it as if it had
been a Rhyming Medical Almanac. I never was
so sure of anything as I was that that critic was a
haughty, besotted person who had read the volume
upside down. But the others were too com-
plimentary. Yet their reviews, though they
pleased me, did not make my pulse beat
quicker than usual. The postman caine for
days with paper after paper, each containing
laudatory notices of my volume. I read them,
and then, like a child who sits listening to fairy-
stories, I said, sotto voce, “Tell me another.” Yet,
somehow, it seemed to me that the stories were all
old stories. I had surely heard them before. Fol-
lowing up this train of thought, I discovered that
I had been for years telling the same stories to
myself. That was why they seemed old. On the
other hand, I knew they were too flamboyant. I
knew also that my verses were but a small Ruth’s
sheaf from the golden harvest of Boaz I had in-
tended to reap. Besides, even the most apprecia-
tive of my critics did not, amongst all the rest of
their extracts, quote from the volume the passages
that I, myself, liked best. This was another dis.
appointment, yet, at the same time, a proof of my
thesis, previously stated, re the Ineptitude oi
Language. I had meant in these passages to make
the reader one with me in thought and feeling, and
cause him to see certain things as I saw them.
The attempt was a partial failure, so far as the
critics were concerned. They would insist
upon using their own eyes and thinking with their
own brains. Then there was the haunting fear
that some ill-conditioned critic would discover the
tendon Achilles in the book, and transfix it with
a Paris-arrow.
* * * *
These were, however, merely surface-thoughts.
The grave condemnatory cogitation that worked
under them was this—“ You have gained your
measure of fame by fraudulent representation.
The other fellow who has gone over the hills and
far away, and disappeared in the sunset, is the
real person who deserves all the credit you have
taken to yourself. He wrote those belauded
verses.” To this accusation the present Ego could
make no satisfactory reply. But the present Ego
solaced itself by saying vehemently that it would
produce something better than had ever been pro-
duced by the fellow who disappeared in the sun-
set. He had carried away on his shoulders a large
swag made up of unrecallable dreams and
visions, but he had not taken the world—
the rolling, opalescent, tragic, comic, glori-
ous world—with him, for all that. The
harvest of Boaz was still waiting for the sickle.
Then I began to feel sorry for that poor devil of a
Troubadour who had departed so early and could
not wait for his laurels. To some people this may
seem far-fetched language, but any man above the
intellectual rank of a suburban councillor will
understand it and know it to be, applying it to
himself in Iris own degree, the most threadbare
kind of truth.
• » * *
Laurels, like bunches of spinach, should be
gathered while they are green. When Beckford
sat up three days and nights drinking black coffee
and writing “ Vathek ” the fame that came to him
was intoxicating because almost immediate. So,
too, with Byron. Smaller people must wait longer,
and when their fame comes to them they say—
-41 You should have come earlier. Have you brought
any money with you ? ”
■ Vieille Moustache ” writes—
Red Page of 30th July points out that ", . . a
reader may command the sense-impressions of a dozen
observers, and may receive in the result a truer and fuller
impression than any single observer received.” This is so
obvious that it seems strange people should affect incre-
dulity when they read that -Jules Verne, that traveller of
travellers, has been, all his life of 70 years, a stay-at-
home. But then Verne is blessed with the imagination
which observes through another’s description, and, haply,
far more accurately and minutely than the authority used
as a stimulant. Alphonse Karr, in his “ Round the World
in My Garden,” exemplifies this eloquently. Who has not
conjured up the mental spectacle of sun-blessed isles,
and drowsy seas, and the opal skies of Southern latitudes,
at mere sight of a bunch of bananas or a cluster of cocoa-
nuts ? What gave Gibbon his virile power of description
but this faculty for seeing with the eyes of Tacitus and
such others who observed the things they wrote about
not more accurately than he who read of them, the long
centuries afterwards.
And I can quote an Australian case. Of all men I
ever met whom the power of imagination enabled to see
beyond their physical ken, Deniehy was most remark-
able. Here was a man who had lived all his days in Aus-
tralia, yet who held the topographical information of a
guide-book regarding all the great cities of Europe. He
could take you to Vienna, Berlin, Dresden, Milan, Flor-
ence, and describe with the minute particularity
of the observant traveller, the “ sights ” of
each. When he spoke of the old cathedrals, their
histories, the movements from which they emerged, and
the traditions of which they remain the evidence, it
was hard to believe that he had never personally looked
upon the things he touched with such familiar fondness.
He could tell you where the great altar-pieces of old
masters could be found. He had the history of old-time
art at his fingers’-end—knew not only the great masters,
but the lesser art workers to whom, perhaps, the greater
were much indebted. I remember a talk when Deniehy
and Father Bleasdale discussed church art. The latter
mentioned Albert Durer’s “ Feast of the Rosary ” as
having been lost. “Not a bit of it,” cried Deniehy
eagerly ; “it is in Prague, much faded, but still
exhibiting Durer’s mastery of coloring.” “I know
Prague,” said Bleasdale, “but never heard of this.” “I
also know Prague,” said Deniehy, with twinkling eyes;
and thereupon he began to talk of its palaces and
churches, its university—the first great public school
founded in Germany—and even its strange winding
streets and terraces and old taverns and quaint markets
and shops, with the voluble ease of the “ oldest inhabi-
tant.” Bleasdale made a gesture of amazement. “Pass
the bottle, Deniehy,” said he. “If I didn't know you to
be a good Catholic, I should take you to be the devil.”
Deniehy laughed. “There have been those, Father,”
said he, “ who contended that it was quite possible to be
both.” “ Get thee behind me ! ” quoth Bleasdale.
Deniehy had certainly exceptional talents. He was an
a hriirable public speaker ; indeed, he possessed many of
the gifts of a great orator. His literary style was strong,
yet graceful—not perhaps so terse and epigrammatic as
that in favor now, but full of the lights and colors of a
picture. His contributions to the press were marked by
a breadth of thought and freedom from the tempting
sophistrips of prejudice in those days remarkable in news-
paper literature.

C.W.D. : The latest vernacular for swag-carry-
ing on the Darling is “ Wearing the Jubilee robe.”
She-Oak : James Bon wick, at p. 122 of his
‘Discovery and Settlement of Port Phillip * (Ib-lliourHe,
1856) refers to the obsolete sticking-plasters— “ To
accommodate the ravenous appetites of their cus-
tomers some publicans kept a cask of herrings on their
counter. In these primitive days sticking plasters were
in vogue. . . . The plasters were certain money-
orders whioh shepherds and slmarers brought down from
tile country and placed in the *afe custody of their friends
of the tap, in whose liar they were stuck up until the
amount was said to he drunk out. When the poor intoxi-
cated simpletons had exhausted their exchequer, their
bodies were removed to some dungeon-looking out-
building denominated the dead house, whence they were
afterwards precipitated into the street to make room for-
another ‘sticking-plaster.’ ”

ing not to waste good money on Le Queux’s “Scribes
and Pharisees, a story of literary London,” is addressed.

and grieves. ’Tis a fake—slipshod dust-and-ashes.
In Memo : Tennyson did not write, “ I hold it
truth with him who sings.” He wrote “I held it truth,”
eto. [He did ; which shows what a snare quotation-dic-
tionaries are: sinoe three consulted—Grocott’s, Web-
ster's, Wood's—give “I hold" with an air of absolute
assurance. One, Webster’s, indexes “I held ” ; but that
doesn't count against text. The wide-spread error en-
dorses the sentiment, anyway: Tennyson might have
kept on holding.]
The difference between Millais and Rossetti is
briefly this that in Rossetti we have contentment with
an ideal attained, in Millais a striving and reaching forth
to a larger world beyond. Rossetti was happy on the
placid bosom of an enchanted lake, Millais sailed forth on
the broad ocean of humanity.— The Outlook.
“Battler” writes from Longreach (Q.) that
“ doing a perish ” is only applied to the state of recovery
after “being on the booze,” and never to being hungry.
Talgalget : I have heard brumby used by blacks
on the Baloone, Paroo, and Bulloo rivers—sounded “ba-
roombie’’—and on one station in particular to distinguish
the unbroken from broken horse.
Melb. : The word-clipping habit grows. Heard
recently: “Hullo, Lizer! Goin’ to the ’op to-ni’?”
“ Over ’t ’Wood ? Ye.” “ Ri-buok. See yer corner
Hod’ and Rei’ ’bout eight.” Meaning the corner of
Hoddle and Reilly Streets, Collingwood.
Chris: Brennan sends a piece of verse untitled.
The pangs that guard the gates of joy,
the cruel sword that will be hist,
how distant seem'd they to the boy—
tohite flashes in the rosy mist !
Ah, not where tender play was screen'd
in the light heart of leafy mirth
of that obdurate might we ween'd
that shakes the sure repose of earth :
and sudden , 'tivixt a sun A sun,
the veil of dreaming is withdrawn ;
lo, our disrupt dominion
& mountains solemn in the dawn —
hard paths that chase the day spring?'s white
& gloomsrthat hold the nether heat
—0 strange, the world newborn from night !
0 dread, the life before our feet!
The Ports of Ships.
Behind him lay the gray Azores,
Behind the gates of Hercules ;
Before him not the ghost of shores
Before him only shoreless seas.
The good mate said : “ Now must we pray,
For lo ! the very stars are gone.
Brave Adm’ral, speak—what shall I say?”
“Why, say, ‘ Sail on ! Sail on ! and on ! ’ ”
“My men grow mutinous day by day :
My men grow ghastly, wan, and weak. ”
The stout mate thought of home ; a spray
Of salt wave washed his swarthy cheek.
“What shall I say, brave say,
If we sight nought but seas at dawn ? ”
“Why, you shall say, at break of day,
‘Sail on ! Sail on ! Sail on ! and on ! ’”
They sailed, and sailed, as winds might blow
Until at last the blanched mate said :
“ Why, now not even God would know
Should I and all my men fall dead.
These very winds forget their way,
For God from these dread seas is gone.
Now speak, brave Admiral; speak, and say —
He said : “ Sail on ! sail on ! and on ! ”
They sailed ! They sailed ! Then spake the mate :
“ This mad sea shows its teeth to-night;
He curls his lip, he lies in wait
With lifted teeth, as if to bite !
Brave Admiral, say but one good Word, —
What shall we do when hope is gone ? ”
The words leaped as a leaping sword :
“ Sail on ! sail on ! sail on ! and on ! ”
C. H. Miller.

Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins
to go you will twitch and show
signs of premature weakness.
Following are some of the symp-
toms :
Do your faoil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down ?
Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back?
Have you pains in head ?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms: —
Do your eyes twitch ?
Do your hands tremble?
Do you feel shaky ?
Can you walk without halting?
Are you melancholy?
Do you have ringing in the ears?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary?
Does your head swim?
Nervous Debility.
This affliction destroys ambi-
tion, organic strength, energy,
and hope. Following are some
of the symptoms :
Do you feel weak?
Have you cold feet?
Have you backache ?
Do you shun society ?
Are you losing flesh ?
Do you sleep poorly ?
Are you low-spirited?
Are your eyes sunken?
Do you have hot flushes?
Is your memory impaired?
Have you no manly vigor?
Have you no vital energy?
Do you have sick headaches?
Is there nausea after eating?
Do your hands or feet sweat?
Has the brightness left your eyes?
Do you feel unfit to marry?

OBJECTS :
This Company is being formed for the purpose of carrying on the business of Consultation Promoters.
As is well-known, the Tasmanian Government have lately passed an Act legalising Consultations on the condi-
tions that the sum of 110,000 shall be deposited with the Colonial Treasurer, as security, and that the drawing of
each Consultation shall be under the supervision-of Government officials.
This Company will rigidly comply with these conditions, thereby giving to the general public who may patronise
the Consultations promoted by the' Company, an absolute confidence in the integrity and fair dealing of the pro-
The estimated profits of the Company will be based on the usual 10 per cent, deductions on all amounts received
for Consultations promoted. . ~ „ . , ~ .
Of the amount subscribed the sum of £lO,OOO will be deposited with the Tasmanian Government, and balance
(less brokerage and flotation expenses) will be placed to the credit of the Company.
This Company will be considered formed, and will be registered as soon as 50,000 Shares have been applied for.
Operations will be conunfenced immediately after formation. Consultations will be opened on all the principal
events throughout the year.
Early application for Shares is necessary, as the Share List is rapidly filling-.
Applicants for Shares are informed that they are at liberty to apply for any number of Shares, but the Directors
will not allot less than TWO to any person.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Recognition.
Ho you remember, love, the day we met?
And how each gazed into the other’s eyes
One breathless moment, thrilled with strange
surprise ?
Nor you nor T forget!
I saw then, love, what I see now; the shine
Of gray-blue eyes, dark-seeming in the shade
Of silken lashes ; eyes whose glory made
The whole sweet face divine 1
But I saw more ! I knew you ! and my.soul
Leaped forth to greet the lost love found on.
more—
The friend and comrade of the days of yore,
In old-world joy and dole.
Wlu't- (.bough I know not in what years or
climes!
To know that one has dreamed, and know o
whom,
While yet no other rays the void illume,
Who has not waked oft-times ?
Perchance, long centuries since, I watched yoi
die;
Whispered wild, passionate words until I knew
You heard no longer ; kissed, clasped close, as you
Breathed a last faint good-bye.
Then, when speech failed us, eye sought eye !
Then heart
To heart in that last long gaze said farewell!
Until the light failed and the darkness fell,
And we were swept apart.
And when our eyes met next, behold ! that
same,
Same look of deathless love was there! unchanged,
From the far worlds through which the soul had
ranged
Each to the other came.
Have we not proved it, love ? Time and again
Some new page of the heart has been turned o’er,
In mystic cypher writ; no need to pore,
Or line by line explain !
A glance sufficed—we knew / How otherwise ?
We, who had felt like throbs of joy and woe,
Had shared like thoughts together long ago,
Like hopes and fears, smiles, sighs ?
Only because deep buried lies the root
In the dim past, this love of ours has grown
So great, has such perennial freshness known,
Brought forth such perfect fruit.
Tas.
W. H. DAWSON.
Ballade of the Waratah.
Some vaunt the lilies of fair France,
And some the Roman’s royal rose ;
Around them twines the old romance
That from each bygone year o’erflows ;
But to my heart lies ever close
The emblem of mine own countree,
An emblem generous fate bestows—
The waratah’s the flower for me.
What though it may not grace the dance,
Too sturdy bred for Romeos,
More fit to deck the blade of lance
In battle when the trumpet blows ;
It hath no charm for maids or beaux,
This sun-fed blossom bold and free,
Ensign of land ne’er smirched by foes—
The waratah’s the flower for me.
Life’s softer spirits may, perchance,
On heartsease sweet their choice repose,
And violets give countenance
To old-world chants or love’s sweet gloze ;
Let others laud them. Here we chose
Strong stock from off a nobler tree.
Give me the bloom our sail outthrows—
The waratah’s the flower for me.
Envoy :
Like that rich heart with promise glows
The federal star of destiny,
When here an Austral nation shows,
Its flower the waratah shall be.
N.S.W.
F. W. HULME.
Melb. Coroner Candler’s recent refusal to
accept the oath of a boy on the “ tell-a-lie-go-to-
hell ” basis, recalls old Coroner Youl’s grim remark
upon a similar occasion: “Swear the lad—he
knows more than we do ! ”
“S.S. ” : Unusual in the South Melbourne
municipal elections. For the first time the female
elector has been enthusiastically canvassed, and
there was quite a feminine rush to the poll. Im-
pertinent inquiries elicit the fact that the women
voted for the candidate who supplied the most
elegant conveyances.

The safest course for a fugitive criminal is
to lodge with a policeman. The burgling Colling-
wood (Melb.) night-watchman, sentenced to over
seven years on different charges, was shown, at
his trial, to have been carrying on a regular system
of robbery from the shops and houses he was paid
to protect; yet, all the time, he was a member of
a police cricket-club, and among the property
seized on his arrest was a bat bearing a silver pre-
sentation shield setting forth his virtues as a
cricketer and a man !

The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications ivill be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

applying to MS.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 17, 1898.
The Financial Amendment.
This House earnestly invites further inquiry into, and a
more thorough consideration of, the financial clauses, re-
garding as evils to be avoided, if- possible, excessive
burdens of taxation, the prolonged system of book-keep-
ing, uncertainty as to the amount of surplus to be divided,
and uncertainty as to the method of distributing it among
the States.— -Last clause of Premier Reid’s proposed Fede-
ral amendments.
Up to the time of writing, New South
Wales Parliament had not even got fairly
started to consider the proposed Federal
amendments, and Australia in general is still
waiting. Until Premier Reid’s amendments,
and all the other amendments on Reid’s
amendments, are considered, and New South
Wales Parliament finally comes to the sur-
face of an ocean of debate with some kind of
definite proposal in its hand, the rest of Aus-
tralia can do nothing but wait. Meanwhile,
the above clause represents, apparently, all
that Premier Reid has to suggest as regards
the principal difficulty. The financial arrange-
ment contained in the Convention Bill, ac-
cording to Reid and the other infuriated
denouncers of that scheme, is wholly bad,
and requires to be cast into outer darkness,
there to wander in obloquy along with the
Bkaddon Blot for evermore. Premier Reid
at the Convention utterly failed to suggest
any new scheme in lieu of the one contained
in the Bill. In the campaign that followed
he first denounced the financial scheme with-
out suggesting anything to take its place ;
then he proposed that it should be amended
by the Commonwealth taking over, or partly
taking over, the debts and railways; and
then he dropped this idea and again became
a hazy denouncer who objected to the scheme
on hand, but had no substitute to offer. And
now his principal “amendment” consists
practically in the acknowledgment that he
objects to the present financial arrangement,
that he wants something else, and that he
can’t tell what that something else is. In
other words, Premier Reid’s principal finan-
cial amendment is an appeal to other people
to tell him what his principal amendment is.
* * * *
The proposed excision of the Braddon
clause doesn’t cast any light on the matter,
for things will be exactly the same when
that clause goes out as they are now
with it in. All that the Braddon clause
specifies is that three-fourths or any larger
amount of the Federal customs and excise
revenue—whether the same be large, small
or middle-sized—shall either be returned to
the States, so that they may use it to pay
interest on their public debts, or applied
directly by the Commonwealth in payment
of interest. The said three-fourths may be
15s. per annum if a Federal Foreign-trade
Ministry cuts down the customs and excise
to £l, or it may be nothing at all, or it may
be £5,000,000 or more ; the fiscal question
is left absolutely open. But, seeing that the
interest bill of the five federating provinces
runs to over £5,700,000 per annum, it natu-
rally follows that, unless the customs and
excise revenue is run up to over £7,500,000,
at least three-fourths of it mast be required
to pay interest, whether the fact is specified
in the Bill or not. The Commonwealth will
work on the Braddon" clause whether the
Braddon clause is kept in or left out, and
its excision doesn’t help the situation in the
least.
* * * *
Therefore, the real financial amendments
which Premier Reid proposes are all em-
bodied in the clause above quoted. And
that clause boils down to a handful of wildly
insane propositions. In the first place, the
New South Wales Wriggler objects to the
uncertainty as to the amount of Federal sur-
plus to be divided among the States, and
wants that surplus defined. And he knows,
unless he is an ass of the first magnitude,
that it can’t be defined. In order to define
the surplus it is necessary to know (1) what
the tariff will be ; (2) what the other
sources of revenue will be ; (3) how much
they will produce per head of the population ;
(4) how the population will increase ; (5)
how the national wealth will increase ; (6)
what the expenses of the Federal Govern-
ment will be in peace time ; (7) whether
there is going to be war to swallow up the
surplus, and when, and for how long, and
what it will cost, and what damage will be
done, and whether this CQuntry will be
licked and have to pay a big indemnity, or
w in and collect one ; (8) whether there will
be drought, pestilence, blockade, or other
disasters ; (9) also, what kind of Government
there will be in Australia for ever and ever,
amen ? When these and a lot more things ate
figured out it will be possible, within
certain limits, to get rid of the aggravating
uncertainty as to the amount of the surplus.
As regards the method of distribution it can
only be distributed according to the contri-
butions of each State ; or according to the
population of each State ; or according to
each State’s necessities ; or according to area
—which would be sheer madness ; or accord-
ing to the amount each State can grab—
which would be civil war.
* * * *
If the surplus is to be distributed accord-
ing to the contributions of each State, an
elaborate book - keeping system is neces-
sary to ascertain what these respective
contributions are. And as Premier Reid
specifically objects to the book-keeping idea
it is obvious that he is against this system.
This leaves only two alternatives—to divide
the surplus according to population (an idea
which Premier Reid carefully, avoids advo-
cating), or according to each State’s neces-
sities (which he doesn’t advocate either).
At. the Convention the same G. H.
Reid was against both these ideas. The
plain and simple device—the only one
which is compatible with the Federal
idea—of letting the Commonwealth take
over the State debts and railways and educa-
tion systems, which would leave it with no
surplus to divide, was advocated by Premier
Reid for a day or two during his recent anti-
Federal campaign, but has now been aban-
doned. Without it there is so very little
Federation left that it is just worth having,
and not much more. And, without it, the
financial problem keeps coming back to the
old spot and knocking up against the old
dead wall. Which is that all the pro-
vinces depend either largely or wholly on
their customs and excise revenue to pay
interest on their debts ; and that if they
hand over that revenue to the Federal
Government, and yet retain their debts,
they must get back a large sum of money
annually or else go broke- Also, that if the
poorest province is to get enough, and yet
the richest province not get too much, and
each one is only to get what it is fairly en-
titled to, and the amount each one is en-
titled to is to be accurately ascertained, and
yet there is to be no elaborate book-keeping,
and the country is to acquire the gift of pro-
phecy, as Premier Reid demands, and know
in advance what the surplus will be, the situ-
ation becomes one that is calculated to blast
the intellect of Solomon and blight the
seven wise men of Greece.
• * » •
The present situation, therefore, can be
compressed into these four propositions.
Premier Reid insists that he is the man who
is in charge of the Federal movement in New
South Wales. He alleges that the financial
scheme fixed up by the Convention is wholly
unsatisfactory. He had nothing better to
propose at the Convention, and he has
nothing at all to pi’opose now. Which is a
pretty abject situation for a leader and a
financial authority to be in. Also he “earn-
estly invites ” somebody else to find a way
of doing something that is impossible. As
matters stand, four provinces, including New
South Wales, gave a majority for the Bill as
it stands, only the Reid Government allowed
the New South Wales majority to be over-
ruled by Act of Parliament. Now it says
that certain amendments are necessary
to make the Bill acceptable to New
South Wales, and the least it can do is
to state what these amendments are.
When it says, in effect, as regards the most
difficult question of all, that it doesn't really
know what amendment it wants, the most
reasonable inference is either that it is only
making delays for the purpose of wrecking
Federation, or that George Reid is merely
trying to make useless and purposeless ink-
marks on the Bill, so that he may claim
afterwards that it is his Bill —and not
Barton’s. The 50 elected delegates struggled
with the financial trouble in a hundred
aspects, and, after sizing up the difficulties of
it, arrived at the best compromise they were
capable of, and the people accepted that
compromise. And the one fat incubus
who says that the scheme evolved by the
people’s delegates is all wrong should at
least be able to explain where it is wrong
and how it may be put right.
The Case for the Bookmaker.
Taking a number of anti-tote letters received
at this office—mostly, no doubt, written or
suggested by the booky, and all of them uncon-
vincing—The Bulletin will quote from two.
William Mason wrote on May 8 lamenting
the intense respectability of racing at Adelaide
as compared with meetings at Melbourne and
Sydney :
“ The arrangements were perfect,” said William, “ but
it struck me there was something wanting. All the
people seemed to be afraid to speak to each other—there
seemed a stiffness about everything, and the smiling faces
were conspicuous by their absence.”
Which means that William Mason, on a visit
from Westralia at the time, was struck by the
strange lack of howling and blasphemy in the
place where these noises used to be. Also he
missed the smiling countenance of the bookie
who skins the lamb, and of the juggins who
drowns his losses in drink. He saw only a
crowd taking its afternoon’s amusement for
just what it was worth—and its stiffness made
him sad. Now, The Bulletin is always sad-
dened by the “stiffness” of the noisy race-
course habituh who, having dropped all his
coin into the hooky’s bag, wants to borrow a
bit from his friends. That’s the sort of “stiff-
ness ” one has to contend with at Sydney and
Melbourne.
* » * *
An abusive person wrote from Adelaide—
one can almost see his hook nose and awful
diamond ring sticking out between the lines of
the letter—on June 16 :
Re your advocacy for tlie totalisator in preference to
the bookmaker at race-meetings, I am sure you don’t
understand how this huge and heartless swindle (the tote,
not Ikey Mo) is worked ; if you did you would never
(abuse and ungrammatical drivel). In Victoria and N.S.
Wales, where the bookmaker is, and the tote is not, I
contend the public have some chance to win, for the
reason that the owners of the horses, the trainers, and
the jockeys are combined to beat the books, if they can ;
the public are in with them, and they have a chance to
win. In Adelaide there are no books, the owners, the
trainers, and the jockeys are against the public, and the
public lose their money every time. At times you
chronicle the big dividends the tote pays, but who gets
these big divs. ? Not the public, but the jockeys, owners,
and trainers. When a big div. is paid it is a certainty
that the horse has heen dead for months, and the public
have been losing money on him, &0., &c.
Impudent nonsense of this sort gets a credulous
hearing from an occasional honest, uninformed
opponent of the tote, otherwise it wouldn’t call
for contradiction. To anyone conversant with
racing speculation the idea of the public being
“in with” the owner, trainer, &c., “to beat
the book ” is as ridiculous as the idea that a
tote, in place of the book, can make any differ-
ence to stable tactics, except to make them
more honest. Owmers and trainers can’t com-
mission the machine to lay against their nags
when “not on the job”; therefore, it stands
to reason that the stable is never in conspiracy
with the tote to bleed the public, whereas the
book has always got a dead ’un or two “ in the
bag.” Abolish the bookmaker, and there is an
end to the “milking” practices. Of all the
money put into the tote never a penny is paid
to a cronk owner as the reward of .hiscronk-
ness. The tote does not invite fraud, whereas
the books live to a considerable extent by con-
spiring with small owners, trainers, and
jockeys to rob the public.
• * * •
As for the statement that big dividends are
invariably drawn by artful stables and not by
the public, there is not a tittle of truth in it.
When a horse is fancied by the stable, the news
leaks out (gushes out, as a rule), and the
horse starts more-or less of a favorite with
the tote, as it does with the books where
there is no tote. When the animal is
not fancied by the stable, it starts at an
outside price wdth the tote, or with the books.
But observe the difference in the result. The
victory of an outsider at Melbourne or Sydney
means that a few stray backers win a little
money, the odds being 10 or 15 to 1, and the
books win heaps of money. The outsider’s
victory at Adelaide means that the same few
stray backers win the whole pool, less the
tote’s modest commission. The odds in this
case are 30, 50, 100, or 200 to 1, instead of
Ikey Mo’s paltry “ tens.” The tote’s odds on
an average are much higher than the books’
odds ; the ordinary punter follows his whim or
his “information,” whether he bets with the bag
or with the machine, and consequently he must
stand to benefit by substitution of the tote for
the books. This apart from all other considera-
tions which make the downfall of Ikey Mo a
thing to be devoutly wished. Big bookmakers
may suit the convenience of a few big gambling
owners, and smaller bookmakers may be useful
to the scoundrelly representatives of “ cronk ”
sport, but all bookmakers, in comparison with
the tote, are bad for the public which finds the
money to support horse-racing.
* * » *
It is an utter waste of time for Ikey Mo and
his friends to pester The Bulletin with ficti-
tious arguments and brazen lies directed
against the tote. Solid arguments and sound
truths would be acceptable if they came along,
but thusfar every angry objection to the totali-
sator has .only served to show the weakness of
the bookmaking cause.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Old Bill and the New One.
When the Reid Government submits its
Federal amendments to the people of New
South Wales—if they ever get so far as that—
then quite a lot of beautiful complications con-
nected with the case will begin to be apparent.
In the first place, it is promised that there is to
be no minimum this time ; the straight Demo-
cratic principle that the majority decides is to be
adhered to. That is to say that when Reid’s
Bill is before the public Reid proposes to enact
that a bare majority shall be sufficient to
carry it even if that majority only in-
cludes 10,000 votes; but when the Bill
drafted under Barton’s leadership was before
the country the same Reid helped to
enact that 79,999 votes and a 70,000
majority should not carry it. This shows,
among other things, what a difference there is
when it is a question of Reid being the Father
of the Commonwealth as compared with the
other case when it was a question of preventing
Barton being the Father ! At the same time,
it must be remembered that the Reid Ministry
disclaims all responsibility for the 80,000 mini-
mum. That minimum was proposed by a
private member; the Reid Government ac-
cepted it, and didn’t rally up its party-majority
against it; and, according to the explanation
tendered by one member of the Reid Cabinet,
it was bound to take this course, and to
stand by in a state of subdued approval
while Federation was carefully wrecked, out of
regard for the rights of private members. Yet
the Reid Ministry promises that this time there
will be no minimum, so it appears that if a
private member interferes now, when Reid is
posing as the Father of the Commonwealth, he
will be promptly squashed. And that gives
the lie direct to the statement that the Reid
Government had no option in the previous case;
if it can guarantee to suppress any private
member who gets in the road when G. H. Reid
is posing as the Father of the Commonwealth,
it could have done so when Barton was occu-
pying that situation only it didn’t want
to and hadn’t the honesty to say so. When
the new Bill is submitted to the people (if
it ever is submitted) with no minimum,
then it follows that if Reid’s Federal scheme
gets 500 majority it will be carried, while Bar-
ton’s Federal scheme got over 5000 majority and
wasn’t carried. Also, the Reid scheme may be
carried if it gets only 10,000 votes, while the
Barton scheme wasn’t carried with 72,000.
And these curious things are to happen because
the Reid Government passed, or connived at
the passing of, an Act stating that the Referen-
dum should be faked in this manner. And the
preamble of the Reid amendments starts by
stating that the vote of 3rd June shows that
the Federal Bill is “ not acceptable to the
electors of the colony in its present shape!”
Yet if Reid really believes that 72,000 affirma-
tive votes, and over 5000 majority in favor of
the last Bill, fairly show that it isn’t accept-
able, then he will enact, if he is an honest
politician, that his Bill must have more votes
and a bigger majority to show that it is accept-
able. And he does nothing of the kind, there-
fore the natural inference is that he either
doesn’t believe it or else he isn’t an honest poli-
tician. For that matter nobody in his senses
ever- said that G.- Reid was an honest politician.
❖
The Shylock Suppression Bill.
A Democratic barrister to The Bulletin
It is to be hoped that the bogey that usury laws limit-
ing the rate of interest have never succeeded will not
have power to prevent Australian legislatures enacting
usury laws that propose no such thing. It may be that
if 20 per cent, is fixed as the maximum limit of just in-
terest, the average rate to the needy borrower will, by
various tricks and devices, be 40 per cent, or more. If,
however, nothing at all be said about the rale of interest,
but the duty of determining whether any particular bar-
gain is a usurious one or not, be imposed on the Courts,
and they' be given a full and unfettered discretion to ad-
just, modify, or annul such bargains, we should be on the
high-road to the abolition of most usuries. Honest
and unhampered Judges, with express duties cast upon
them, can do perhaps more than legislatures to equitably
adjust the relations of man to man in private law’. The
case of “fraud” is one in point. No one can doubt the
enormous power of the Courts in checking fraud, and of
their success when it comes before them. But they would
never have gained that power had the legislature defined
what was to be considered “fraud.” The Courts them-
selves refuse to define it, for the excellent reason that
immediately they did so human ingenuity would succeed
in finding several loopholes in the definition. Everyone
knows what fraud is without definition, as everyone with
ordinary knowledge of human affairs knows what usury is,
whether an Act of Parliament fixes a rate of interest or not.
A very workable and useful usury law can be had by
giving this unfettered discretion and imposing tins duty
on the Judges, and although it runs counter to the in-
human doctrines of the Manchester School it is sincerely
to be hoped that the clause of the English Commission’s
report to this effect will find a place in every Australian
Statute Book. As a matter of fact all that is sought is an
extension of the powers already exercised by the courts
in their equitable jurisdiction in favor of reversioners
and expectant heirs -to the ordinary transactions of
ordinary men. Irish barristers have won fortune and
fame for themselves by using this Chancery machinery
against the Shylocks of Dublin, and one cannot see any
sound reason why such protection against unconscion-
able bargains cannot be had in Australia. In an import-
ant case in the House of Lords, in 1877, or thereabouts,
Lord Hatherley says :
“In ordinary cases each party to a bargain must take
care of his own interest, and it will not be presumed that
undue advantage or contrivance has been resorted to on
either side ; but, in the case of the expectant heir, or of
persons under pressure without adequate protection, and
in the case of dealing with uneducated, ignorant persons,
the burden of showing the fairness of the transaction is
thrown on the person who seeks to obtain the benefit of
the contract.”
A good usury law would simply extend the area of this
principle. In Melbourne, a few years ago, a court upset
a bank’s contract with a Chinaman’s wife on the ground
that she, being unfamiliar with the English language,
didn't realise the full meaning of what she had signed.
Why deny to a trapped necessitous borrower what was
freely accorded to this Chinese lady ? Yet that’s practi-
cally all a well-framed usury law means. Of course, it
may be urged that the law, being already so, it is useless
to legislate anew. The objectors forget that most of the
ordinary legal transactions of life come not before chan-
cery experts, but before county-court Judges and ignor-
ant justices who must see principles in a statute before
they can grasp the fact that they are applicable to the
particular case before them. To cite a well-established
principle before an ordinary justice and to support it by
an ancient chancery case in a worm-eaten book usually
means an order for the other side, with costs, and it’s


rarely worth while appealing. What is wanted is that all
courts shall administer equity in usurious contracts, and
that can only be got by a plain Anti-Usury Act.
Want of Confidence.
The alleged motion of censure which is now
filling up the time of the N.S.W. Parliament,
and wasting public money, and creating much
useless outlay of conversation and ink, and
causing the Federal question to hang up in-
definitely, is a tine example of Party Govern-
ment in its most decomposed state. To begin
with, Premier Reid proposed that the Assem-
bly should consider the advisability of recom-
mending certain alterations in the Convention
Bill. The said Reid has alleged over and over
again that Federation is not a party question,
and if it isn’t a party question then it is com-
petent for anyone to interfere in the matter
without raising party issues. When a private
member set to work to wreck Federation by
raising the late-lamented minimum to 80,000,
the Reid Government slumbered peacefully
amid the debris, and refused to interfere on
the ground that Federation was a strictly
non-party matter. A few days ago one
of its own followers proposed a radical
amendment in the Government’s new Federal
scheme, and nothing happened, for the
same good reason. Then Lyne proposed
his little amendment—which, presumably, he
had as much right to do as any other member.
The matter was a non-party one, and Lyne ex-
plained that he didn’t want his small, mild
idea to be treated as a motion of censure. All
the House had to do was to get on with the
debate on the F’ederal resolutions and the
various amendments, and try to evolve some
definite idea out of the chaos. But the N.S.W.
Premier insisted that Lyne’s amendment should
be treated as a vote of censure; therefore all
public business has to be hung up till it is
talked to death. The talk will cover every-
thing that the Government has done or left
undone since it started, and no decision will be
arrived at regarding the alleged subject of
discussion—all the row is as to whether
Reid or Lyne shall have “ hoffis.” The
province doesn’t want to discuss the barren
question of “hoffis” just now. It wants to
get on with Federation. Also, there was
nothing at all about “ hoffis” in Lyne’s amend-
ment. And if Federation is to get a decent
show it has to be considered on its merits; if
any member’s Federal proposals are to be met
with the threat that if they are carried the
'Government will resign, and there will pro-
bably be a dissolution, in the course of which
many members may lose their seats, and Free-
trade will go overboard, and all manner of
other things will happen, then it can’t be con-
sidered on its merits. Considering all the
assurances that Federation is a purely non-
party question, this raising of the old party
spectre as soon as any objection is offered t o the
Ministry’s Federal programme is a dismal pro-
ceeding. Even the biggest national interests
must needs be subordinated to the question of
“ hoffis.”

State Fire Insurance.
The S.A. State Fire Insurance scheme, now
before Parliament, is the boldest thing yet
attempted in this direction. It doesn’t quite
reach the Democratic ideal, but it certainly
gets a good distance in that direction. It pro-
vides that any district may decide to make the
fire-insurance of all buildings in the State
office compulsory—apparently the insurance of
contents is optional. When Adelaide and one-
half of the other districts have adopted the
system of compulsory insurance then the pro-
cess is to apply automatically to all the rest of
the province. Probably it would be more
economical to apply- the compulsory prin-
ciple right off, but it would seem that S.A.
Govt, didn’t feel equal to this. When
the compulsory system gets afloat the premiums
will be collected like any other taxes, and
recovered by process of law, if necessary.
There will be no heavy outlay for commissions
and advertising. One manager and three
directors will boss the show, instead of the
lamentable spectacle presented by so many
private offices of a manager and a board of five
or six directors, all living on a little business
which produces barely £20,000 a-year gross
income. There is provision for accumulating
a reserve-fund of at least £250,000, and the
State guarantees payment of all losses. Unless
the scheme gets wrecked in a stormy Legisla-
ture, it should start with every promise
of success. The mournful cry of the in-
terested “ insurance - expert ” is to the
effect that State Fire Insurance must
fail because its operations are not spread over a
sufficiently large area to get a fair average, but
the expert who lives on the present system is
bound to say things like that. He is paid for
it. Most of the insurance offices which have
come to very bad grief in Australia have done
it by spreading themselves over a large area,
and getting burnt to death in remote places
where the management couldn’t keep a constant
eye on the business; and the most prosperous of
the survivors is the one which confines its busi-
ness to a single province—just as the State
office will do. At all events, State Fire Insur-
ance is obviously coming, and then the expert
whose management has burst up so many pri-
vate institutions can be judged by results.

Only Two.
Two members, one of them Higgins and the
other Maloney, raised their lonesome voices
in Vic. Assembly against Premier Turner’s
scheme of borrowing £2,000,000 for public
works purposes and setting the old razzle-dazzle
going afresh. Higgins proposed as an amend-
ment that the works should be provided for by
taxation instead of by the old, easy, thriftless
borrow, a reform which The Bulletin has
advocated in a very solitary fashion for years
past ; but he and Maloney were extinguished
beneath the dead weight of the great loan
majority. Possibly if Higgins had been ready
with a definite plan for raising the money wit h-
out borrowing he might have found another
man to support him besides Maloney, but even
that is doubtful. Australia has grown so
accustomed to being a plaintive Lazarus offer-
ing its I O U at Bull’s back-door that it could
hardly stand the mental shock of beginning to
be honest and self-reliant at this late hour. Yet
there is nothing very attractive about the pre-
sent system that this country should be so
deeply attached to it. Victoria has borrowed in
all a trifle under ,£48,000,000, and in the last
15 years alone it has paid over £24,000,000
in interest and expenses. During its whole
borrowing career it has paid away something
like £50,000,000 in interest and expenses on
the £48,000,000 kindly advanced to it by Bull,
and the £48,000,000 is still owing, and before
it is repaid the interest on it will probably run
to £500,000,000 instead of fifty. If Victoria
had faced the situation from the first, and
started constructing its public works out of re-
venue in the early boom days, and kept at it
ever since, it would be all this £50,000,000
better off, and it would have no £1,800,000 a
year interest to grind out of its people, and no
£48,000,000 liability, and no 500 millions or
more of interest to find in the future, and it
wouldn’t have burst up in ’92. And it could
have raised all the money for all its public
works out of taxation without its burden being
nearly as heavy, even in the early days, before
the revenue from its railways and other
undertakings relieved the strain, as that
which many European countries carry without
a whimper. There is plenty of time to
make a fresh start even now if the people and
the politicians of Australia had the grit and
the sense—which they haven’t. If some Aus-
tralian Government would set itself to dig out
£1,000,000 a-year from the many possible
sources of revenue that have been hitherto
neglected, and invest it in public works yield-
ing even 2 per cent., and thereby add another
£20,000 every year to the national revenue, and
set money in circulation, and find work for
some 6000 or 8000 heads of families, and sware
off borrowing for evermore, it is still possible
to bring back the golden days. After 10 years
of this policy, the revenue would be £200,000
a-year more from new public works alone. The
country would be no poorer for all this taxa-
tion ; if £1,000,000 a-year is raised in the coun-
try, and spent on works in the country, it
doesn’t make the amount of money in the coun-
try one penny the less. And in 10 years the
works w'ould make the country £10,000,000
richer, while the increased land values which
they would create would probably add another
£10,000,000t0 its total wealth. Also, the increase
of employment through this expenditure would
attract population, which means more wealth ;
and the boom thus created would be a per-
manent one—not a bubble that was bound to
burst horribly when Bull got one of his
periodical panics and the loan didn’t float.
Apparently there are only two men in Vic.
Assembly with brains and courage enough to
realise these things, and Riggins and Maloney
are the pair in question. In financial matters,
the average Australian politician is partly a
fool and mostly a cow’ard, and the Turner
Government, when it came to the pinch, has
proved to be built of much the same material
as the rest. If Australia ever wants a new
name it should be re-christened Micawber.
Barring Higgins and Maloney and The Bul-
letin, everybody seems to have the Micawber
complaint in Australia.
“The Bulletin’s” Inconsistency.
J. C. Watson, M.L.A., writes again:—
Referring to your “ Plain English ” article under the
heading of “The Bulletin’s Inconsistency,” permit me
to say that I charged The Bulletin with inconsistency,
not because it refused any longer to support myself, but
because it had turned against its own teachings. The
Bulletin of old urged, inter alia, the adoption of the
Referendum; equal voting power to all, no matter of
what station or where resident; and the abolition of
Party Government. Largely because of its advocacy these
principles are to-day believed in by a great number of the
people, “ Yours truly ” being among them. In’97 the
Labor-party advocated the inclusion of some of The Bul-
letin’s ideas in the proposed Constitution, but were
defeated. In ’9B, not foolishly insisting on the whole
hog, we yet stood firm for majority'rule in the Federa-
tion. Yet The Bulletin barracked for the men who had
been against its own ideas all along, and against myself
and other Labor candidates. I suppose our fault was
that we had taken The Bulletin doctrine as
being meant literally, whereas it was really taught only
in a Pickwickian sense. You say 1 supported Dibbs in ’92.
Wrong. In ’92 I wasn't in Parliament, but out of it went
baldheaded against Dibbs, Barton, and O’Connor (par-
ticularly the two latter) over the Broken Hill arrests.
Then you say that, though you supported me in ’97, you
couldn’t do it in’9B, because I was in bad company. I
should like to know which side could be taken without
getting into “bad company”? Just run over (in cold
blood) that list of candidates you recommended a few
days before the election ! The Labor-party couldn’t sup-
port BarwN because he not only voted for the Bill (as did
Reid), but in the Convention bowed th“ knee to the
fetish of “ State Sovereignty ” to an extent that carried
him always against majority rule. Against this, Reid
voted/or it fairly consistently, as witness the last two
divisions in Melb., 10 3 ’9B. Barton’s great influence in
the Convention, properly exercised, could probably have
secured a Constitution ahead of those in existence else-
where ; as it was, he helped the provincial “State-right ”
men from the smaller colonies to uphold the power of the
minority to run the Federal show. We therefore thought
there was a better chance of getting an amended Bill
from Reid than from Barton. Finally, you say you may,
in ’99, again support me, if I “ improve.” As I have no
present intention of “jumping Jim Crow,” I expect to
struggle along with The Bulletin’s opposition until it re-
sumes its wonted place as a leader of the forward party.
Yet, according to the printed report of his
speech,thepointonwhich Mr. Watson, M.L.A.,
charged The Bulletin with inconsistency, was
that a year or two ago it urged the people to
voteforWATSON, M.L. A., whilethis timeiturged
the people not to vote for Watson, M.L.A.
For the rest, The Bulletin certainly did—and
does—support the Referendum, equal voting
power, and the abolition of Party Government,
as Watson, M.L.A., alleges. Likewise, it
supports Federation, interprovincial Freelrade,
an elective Upper House with a rrfanhood
suffrage basis, payment of both Houses
of Parliament, the abolition of all property
qualifications for members of either House, the
joint sitting as a prompt and easy means of de-
ciding deadlocks between the two Houses (this
is wanted in addition to the Referendum, which
is too slow and costly to be applied to every
trivial deadlock), and many other things. Well,
the Commonwealth Rill offered Federation, in-
terprovincial Freelrade, a Senate elected by
manhood-suffrage (no property-qualification for
senators or electors as in Victoria or Tasmania,
or government of the people by life nominees
as in N.S. W..), payment of members of both
Houses, the abolition of the plural vote, the
joint sitting, and many other Democratic re-
forms. And, though it didn’t make every man’s
vote of exactly the same value, the discrepan-
cies were a great deal less than those now ex-
isting in N.S.YF., where the ordinary elector
has just half a vote (or a vote for one-half the
Legislature), and one man’s half-vote is worth
about 2J times as much as another’s, according
to the accident of his location. J. 0.
Watson voted against a measure which em-
bodied all these Democratic ideals, and in
favor of a continuance of the old state of
things—which includes disunion, border tariffs,
a nominee Council of rich fossils, unequal
voting power, a continuation of Party Govern-
ment, no Referendum, and no provision for the
remedy of deadlocks. Of course, if Mr. Wat-
son’s vote for the old status quo , including
Party Government, no Referendum, and a
shamefully unequal distribution of voting
power, proves that he is a supporter of the
Referendum, and equal voting power, and non-
Party Government; and if his support of
Premier Reid, who says he is going to preserve
the nominee Council, proves that he is against
the nominee Council; and if his bitter opposi-
tion of Barton and O’Connor, who both de-
clared for an elective Council with a manhood
suffrage basis, shows that he favors manhood
suffrage—then there is nothing more to be said.
More about Women.
“The Baron” thus comments on “ Creeve
Roe’s ” recent sex-whoop :
Editor Bulletin. —“ In the Spring’ the young man's
fancy lightly turns to thoughts of love” —and surely
“Creeve Roe” is young and the approach of Spring, the
stir of something fresh and vital in the air, inspired the
reflections on women (or more accurately on woman in
the abstract) recorded in Tiie Bulletin of Sept. 3.
But the quality of the young man’s thinking will
depend upon his mental attitude towards the subject of
thought. And “C.R.’s” mental attitude, though too
common, is neither healthy nor pleasing. What ex-
aggerated absurdity is this anent Woman —by which he
means always the physical sex in woman —as “ the most
interesting fact of existence to man”? What kind of
erotically-diseased men are those who find in woman
“ the goal of all ambition ” ? “C.R.” himself appears to
be so dazzled and excited by a morbid sexuality that he
fails to realise the difference between recognising the
just attraction of sex in woman and attributing to it an
all-absorbing interest and importance. In a large-minded
and clean-thinking society the fleshly school of painting
and the Burne-Jones methods are neither excluded nor
over-estimated.
“Modern woman refuses to be idealised.” True in
part. But when men (and women, too) fail to discern in
woman more than “ the beauties of her body ” when
“ the delicate aroma of her body,” and her other physical
characteristics, become of dominant interest, then all
‘true and enduring Love will cease to be.
The introduction of the sex element into literature, as
it is now being handled, does not offend because it con-
ceives woman as a Being with desires and passions, but
because it discloses a tendency to see in her nothing else.
There is a mawkish and fevered desire to accentuate the
sexual in life—to write, as does “C.R.,”as though the
physical aspects of woman and of Love were of primary
interest. To think thus, to act on this hypothesis, is to
miss all that is best and richest in the relation of the
sexes. It is the craving of this neurotic and distorted
sexuality for gratification, it-knows not how, that leads
to the unnatural and diseased pruriency of the times as
evidenced in too many ways.-
Let there be no misunderstanding. The physical bear-
ing of sex-love is recognised and appropriately valued
among the most highly developed and thoughtful men
and women, but it is also seen to attain its best only
when it is the last and fullest expression of a pre-existent
inward and spiritual unity.
It is true that sex-love, with all that it involves, has
been too long under taboo in the realms of literature and
education—we have lost the balance and dignity of the
ancients in our conception of the sex-relationship. This
is largely due to the fact that men have learnt to divorce
the sexual act from love, apd so have lost sight of its
original spiritual meaning; it has'becotne inevitably a
coarse and shameful thing. And as a means of merely
animal gratification it is this, just in proportion to its
innate and original holiness when subordinated to its
proper place and consecrated to the service of a largely
and nobly-conceived companionship of the sexes.
No one with a sense of the true proportion in the com-
plex elements of life wishes to eliminate sex from it, or
from Art. The drama of human life must, have its Helens
and Brunhilds, its Guinevkres and Isolts, but the real
value of these types does not lie in their exhibition of
woman as “ the full-blooded female of her species with
appetites and passions and throbbing veins and a
secret hankering after wickedness.” In many cases
woman is all this, no doubt, but she is some-
thing more, and even in the lower stages of
development (where in the bulk she is now) she
feels dimly that she is more than this. And that is why
she resents and wearies at the typical passionate love-
poem with its one-sided appreciation of her personality
and over-emphasis of her bodily beauties.
If these thoughts are not new, they need at least to be
re-stated over and over again lest such unmixed
animalism as breathes through “Mostly About Women”
should infect our literature and hinder—for nothing we
believe will altogether arrest—the growth of saner and
cleanlier views of the sex element in life.

N.S.W. Ministry has put up its derelict
Sydney Smith, ex-Minister for Pumpkins, to
oppose Barton in Maoleay electorate, and
although Smith’s chances are hardly worth
mentioning the position is interesting. The
reasons why Barton should be in the Assembly
are that he is a big man, an enthusiastic and
able Federationist, and that he can deal
fairly with Federation because be hasn’t to be
considering his sordid portfolio all the time;
and the Government can’t. It daren’t even
let Lyne’s Federal proposals be debated on
their merits, hut had to bang the party
drum and blow the shoddy trumpet in order to
wipe him out, for fear his proposals should be
accepted instead of Reid’s, and thereby Reid
would lose prestige and his job and his salary
be endangered. There ought to he at least one
big Federalist in N.S.W. Assembly who isn’t
hampered by the fear of losing his port-
folio. And S. Smith isn’t a big man, or a
brainy man, or even a sincere Fed.erationist.
His mission in life is to look at the
suspected vegetable through a magnifying-
glass—not that he thereby does any good to the
vegetable, but because it keeps him from fall-
ing over things and breaking them. S. Smith,
dreaming over the dropsical tomatc, is harm-
less ; to that extent S. Smith is a i agricultural
authority. And that is all one can say for
S. Smith.

what a shriek would have gone up from an

of the wounded dervishes slaughtered on the
field by the Soudanese allies. As it is, the

the Soudanese were sent “ to clear the way for
the British ” after the Khalifa’s forces had been
driven back, and—
This they did by slaughtering all the wounded der-
vishes who lay on the ground, in order to prevent them

attacking the British troops.
The Soudanese regiments are officered by
Britons, so it cannot be assumed that the nig-
gers were acting entirely “ on their own.” And
the fact that such precautions must be taken to
preserve the British regiments from injury at
the hands of the damaged Arabs scattered on
the field suggests the idea that particular care
is being taken of Atkins in the Soudan. Do
the black allies go ahead sweeping the track
and laying down carpets for “our army ” ?

The N.S. W. Totalisator Bill, introduced by Mr.
Pilcher in the Leg. Council, contains the
usual needless apology. The club which uses
the tote is to deduct anything not exceeding 10
per cent., and one-fourth of this deduction is
to go to the State and be applied towards the
support of charitable institutions. The Trea-
sury won’t openly touch the soiled loot—it
doesn’t mind having drink as its second largest
source of revenue, but it shudders at the idea
of taking the tote revenue, so that money is to
be gathered up with the tongs and thrown
out to the beggar at the gate. The fact that
the tote revenue is to go in charity is
supposed to be some excuse for the intro-
duction of the machine, though Heaven
only knows why any such excuse should
be required when the awful bookmaker
is allowed to exist without giving per cent,
of his plunder to charity by way of moral
whitewash. And the disguise is very poor, as
well as wholly needless. If the State allows
.£50,000 or ,£lOO,OOO a year to be raised for
charities per medium of the tote, because it is
too beastly pious to seize the money openly, it
will thereby be enabled to reduce the charitable
vote, and the result will be just about the
same as if it publicly grabbed the coin
in the first place. The chief advantages
of the circuitous arrangement are that it will’
enable the charitable vote to he kept down,
whereupon the Government can say that its
jollv-and-fat policy has reduced the demand for
charity. Also, if any obtrusive member asks
why the Government, with the tote revenue
added to its resources, can’t do with a little less
borrowing, the Govt, can impale him with its
scornful eye and state that the lion, person
doesn’t know what he is talking about, and
that the Government gets no tote revenue.

The cause of Labor Federation in Australia
seems to have fallen on evil days. It was first
definitely brought into existence in a still-birth
sort of fashion in Ballarat at the close of the
maritime strike. It was revived again at Vic.
Eight-Hours’ Day conference, and postponed to
Adelaide this month. Adelaide now has post-
poned it some more, despite vigorous protests
from Vic. Trades Hall delegate Billson, wiio
roundly accused Labor of insincerity in the
matter. The secret of the indifference in the
matter (a vital matter, Loo, seeing that the em-
ployers are federated) lies in the fact that trades-
federation is opposed to the interests of trades-
union-officialism. This has killed splendid
schemes up to date in England, and the fear of
their own extinction makes the average officials _
block it here.
Vic. Amending Land Act embodies Coffin’s
suggestion of registering the homestead. The
property must not exceed £lOOO in value, and
holder must be able to pay all debts, without,
aid of homestead, which then, on registration,
is protected from all future debts, except for
Govt, or municipal claims, wages, liability for
buildings subsequently erected, and fencing.
Should holder become insolvent within a year,
registration is rescinded ; and should he die
within that time the homestead vests in the
executor, and becomes liable for debts in the
ordinary course. But if death takes place more
than a year after registration, the homestead
vests in the widow till she die or re-marry. A
possible flaw in the Bill is that when the pro-
perty becomes above £lOOO in value a judgment
creditor or assignee in case of insolvency can
sell it, and all above the £lOOO can be applied
to payment of debts. Also, it holds out induce-
ment not to improve beyond £lOOO in value.
By falsely representing to a money-lending
client that he had fixed up a £250 mortgage on
said money-lender’s behalf, a Tasmanian lawyer
obtained a cheque for the amount and applied
it to his own use. The money-lender called for
and was paid £7 10s., being interest for the
first six months then due, Subsequently it
transpired that no mortgage had ever been
arranged, and the lawyer was lately charged
with larceny as a bailee. The case was heard
a few weeks ago, and the Judge directed the
jury that, if they believed that at the moment
the defendant got the cheque from his client he
was acting bond-fide, even though subsequently
he might have fallen into a dishonest course of
action, they could not convict him. Verdict of
not guilty, and the accused was discharged.
He is now pursuing the even tenor of his pro-
fessional way.
