Anything so long expected as “La
Poupee ” is bound to disappoint somebody, some-
where. This scribe owns to ten minutes of “ that
sinking feeling ” as the opening scene unfolded it-
self at Her Majesty’s on Saturday night. This
scene is laid in a Monastery, and for that reason
one expects some fun, not to say giddiness, in the
cloister cells. Instead, there are unexpected re-
marks about hunger and poverty, sage advice, and
several other elements you are not prepared to
meet in a Monastery. But enter La Poupee in
scene two ! and a sparkle rises suddenly, and from
then to the end the opera goes with a whizz and a
click quite worthy of its namesake. A funny,
flimsy shred of a story strings things together.
This shred winds itself round the marriage of one
Launcelot, a monk-to-be or unfinished friar, with
a life-sized doll that isn’t a doll at all, but is Dolly
Vane impersonating a doll that was made in her
image. And the reason why Launcelot weds
a doll instead of a woman is all because of a
Moneyed Uncle who will give him dollars if
he can produce a wife. And just there the real
doll’s arm gets broken, and the doll-maker’s
daughter dresses up as La Poupee, and squeaks
and creaks and makes mechanical conversation
when the button is pressed, and sings in a cracked
doll-voice “My Darling, I love you,” and finally
marries her purchaser, and tells him how she loves
him, and how she isn’t a doll at all. So he reckons
the Monastery well lost for her sake, and the cur-
tain falls just as the thinness of the play is begin-
ning to show itself. Considering the weakness of
the Monastery’s victualling department, Launce-
lot is probably well out of it.
* * * *
The whole piece hangs on the shoulders of
Dorothy Vane, and she rises up like an Atlas and
supports it without a falter. There isn’t an epithet
from A to Z too good for her impersonation.
Every movement of the doll is lifelike, so to speak.
The stiffened fingers, and automatic jerks, and
mechanical walks, and the honeymoon conver-
sation, and the swear, and the cracked songs, are
utterly doll-like, and yet subtly invested with her
own humor. Charles Kenningham, as the worthy
Launcelot, acts up to every freak of his doll bride.
His smooth round face is just the sort of innocent
full-moon countenance a baby might see when it
looked into a drop of water. And his voice and
dancing and vivacity work well for him. George
Laun, as Hilarious, the toymaker, makes all the
fuji possible out of his part, which isn’t laid on the
extra broad lines of other roles of his. Carrie
Moore gets in a clever little sketch of a boy
apprentice. P. B. Bathurst is out of sorts as
lather Maxime. Howard Vernon and George
De Lara do some giddy old boy business in cut-
away coats and with white bows on their calves,
and Mrs. Bracy and Miss Frances Adler and
others play their little parts without a murmur.
Several catchy songs are scattered through the
piece, but the music isn’t startling. Mr. Bathurst
has a fine song in the first scene with a nicely
harmonised accompaniment, and the Doll’s song,
‘lean dance and I can sing,” is a droll bit of
music, but you feel as if you had heard the other
poems before. Nor is the dialogue cuttingly witty.
In short, as hinted previously, the piece depends
entirely on the doll, and if her joints wouldn’t
work, and the squeak wasn’t forthcoming, there
would be very little left of “ La Poupee.”
** * *
The lady-gymnast who undresses herself
on the trapeze, at London Alhambra, is perhaps
more thrilling, but certainly not as humorous, as
Wamwratta, the wire-walker of yore. Wain-
wratta—he came out to Australia with Tessero’s
variety co. started his wire-walking performance
m swaggah evening dress. He was the typical
Johnnie at all points—small snub nose, large curly
moustache, hair parted down the centre, and very
self-satisfied smile. Having smoked his cigarette,
he would divest himself of his garments one by
one, and, when there were only the shirt and
pants left, he would whip off the pants and
crouch down upon the wire, clutching his
shirt-tails, as though to hide his naked-
ness. How the women gasped—and en-
joyed it; and how the men enjoyed it a great
deal more than the women, and lied about
it afterwards, saying it was highly objection-
able. There were no nets stretched under
aerial performers in those days—anyway, Wuin-
wratta took all risks—and the folks on the ground
floor gazed upwards without interruption, so to
speak. Wainwratta in his shirt went through a
dumb show of cold in the limbs and alarm at his
own temerity, and then —off went the shirt, and
the man, fully clad in pink fleshings and narrow,
velvet trunks, was as proper as proper could be!
Such “turns” are sermons on the Indecency of
Clothes. A nigger with only a cloth round his
waist is not a more shocking sight than a gymnast
m trunks, but if the nigger, after appearing in
trousers, starts to disclose his legs to the public—
well, he’s simply an offence against modesty.
* # ifc *
“ The Fatal Card,” at Sydney Theatre
Royal, is a thrill that doesn’t depend on anythin"
m particular for its effects. Ranchers, Cow-Boys”
“ Home Sweet Home,” bringing tears to the eyes
of a Colorado drinking-den, a lynching that ia
turned aside by the right arm of one brave Eng-
lishman, a proposal in the water, and a card that
is torn in two, are the mild excitements that goon
at the theatre for the next 12 nights. After
which another big London success, “Sport-
ing Life,” will be presented. Bland Holt
is cast in a character he has played so
often that you don’t know now which is
Bland and which is the character, the English
johnny with a stammer and a stare and an icy
cheek that gets colder as the dangers deepen.
Walter Baker, as Gerald Austin, sacrifices his
beauty nobly to a red beard, but is compensated
with an heroic part that touches the heart-strings
of the gods and makes them quiver till they yell.
Albert Norman and Mr. Cosgrove work elaborate
patterns of villainy over the play. Mrs. Bland
Holt plays the pretty spoilt English girl with
vivacity, and 26 other people deepen complica-
tions, and competently assist in the final unravel-
ling of all the knots that have [to be tied in an
Adelphi success.
** * *
The temporary indisposition of Ada Reeve
was about the most interesting item in the pro-
gramme at Melb. Opera last week. Miss Ada en-
joyed bad health for two nights, but she probably
took the floor again last Monday. At any rate,
Monday’s advt. of the Rickards show gave as much
space to Ada’s name as it gave to Peggy’s Pryde’s,
and this seemed calculated to make the dainty
little invalid feel much better. Peggy, in her cos-
ter-gal characters, promises to go strong for some
time, but her tendency to disguise herself in a
flaxen wig and sing painful songs re waxdolls and
Rule Britannia must be promptly suppressed, or it
will rub a lot of gilt off the reputation she brought
from Sydney.
** * *
The rival “star" ladies at Melb. Opera
House are said to not exactly adore one another.
The lady who had been on the programme before
the other lady arrived was determined not to have
her dear little nose put out of joint by the new-
comer, and the splendid reception which that first
lady received on the night of the second lady’s
debut was an earnest of the “healthy rivalry”
that has gone on increasing ever since. Opera
House audiences get the full benefit of the com-
petition. Each lady is bound to do all she knows,
and how the little ’un does work !
** * *
Nothing fresh happened in the Melbourne
show-world last Saturday, except a thunderstorm,
which was bad for business at most places. “ The
Harbor Lights” didn’t leave the Princess’s in
accordance with previous arrangements, but it
will certainly go this week. Then six perform-
ances of “Prisoner of Zenda,” introducing G’eeil
Ward and Miss Hughes as changes in the cast.
“ Under the Red Robe ” follows the revival.
** * #
At the Royal they are getting fair returns
from “Round the World in 80 Days,” and the
special pasteboard engine continues to vomit fire-
works from the funnel with much vigor. Also
the tribe of Choctaw Indians are dropping dead
for all they are worth the moment that the plump
Erenchinan (who seems to grow more plump but
less natural as the season progresses) takes aim at
their chieftain. The unanimity of the Tribe’s
decease is now something wonderful. The war-
riors don’t even get off a final yell of defiance.
They simply drop, whereupon several troops
rush from round the corner with fixed bayonets
and complete the horror of the scene by pre-
paring to jab any corpse that threatens mis-
chief. “Round the World,” as The Bulle-
tin has previously remarked, is full of cheer-
ful incident. The principals get very few oppor-
tunities for acting, but they are mostly equal
to the occasion when called upon, and the smile of
Miss Lily Titheradge, like the prayer of the
righteous, availeth much. Also, the audience is
deeply moved when the Hinglish tourist shakes
hands with the ’Murkan citizen as a token that
blood is thicker than boarding-house soup.
** * *
The Victorian Operatic Society has com-
menced to break the news that it will give some
amateur performances of “The Bohemian Girl”
at Melb. Bijou, commencing on Caulfield Cup
night. The V.O.S.’s effort will be in aid of St.
Vincent’s Hospital.
** * *
Prof. Marshall Hall, the warlike musical
bard who prays for his foes with thumb unto his
nose, will conduct his excellent orchestra at Melb.
Town Hall on the afternoon of next Saturday but
one (24th). It will be a Wagnerian occasion ; also
it will be an opportunity for any person who
loathes holy cant, and admires a good musi-
cian, to roll up and express his feelings towards the
man whom the drivelsome Argus delighted to
dishonor. Meanwhile, Phil. Newbury (whose
popular concerts seem as sounding brass and
howling madness to the bard aforesaid) still con-
trives to gather the multitude together on Satur-
day nights. Some of the new artists that he has
discovered—notably Rodda Perry, from Bendigo,
and a small boy violinist—are rich in raw material.
The material is very, very raw in Rodda’s case,
but the young man’s name can’t be improved any-
way. It is worthy of the finest vocalism on earth,
and it has the particular merit of being reversible.
Why not Perry Rodda ?
*# * *
Melb. University Council has decided that
Marshall-Hall will have to retire from the man-
agement of the University Conservatorium when
he relinquishes the Ormond Professorship on his
year’s leave of absence. Neither will he lie per-
mitted to act as lion, conductor of orchestral con-
certs during the “holiday,” although the Council
leaves him at liberty to direct Melb. Liedertafel.
If Marshall-Hall decides to stay in Melbourne under
these conditions he may be expected to devote the
whole boiling of his enthusiasm towards making
that Liedertafel hum.
“ Poverty Point ” see page 25,
Some Art and Some Artists.
If one could only say always pleasant thing’s about
charming people what a delightful world it would
be ! We should all admire each other, and with
sparkling eyes, full of the light of enthusiastic
friendship, shake hands effusively and tell each
other what very fine and clever fellows we are,
when properly understood. But nobody under-
stands any of us properly. No other fact of
existence is so pathetic and so mournful. I have
before me now the catalogue of the nineteenth
annual exhibition of the Art Society of New South
Wales, and would give anything I could atiord to
be able to candidly praise the pictures that
are exhibited in the rooms at 70 Pitt-street. But
a mendacity though never so well meant is of no
use to anybody. The show is not a thing to take
too seriously. The artists whose works hang on
the walls have evidently the best of intentions,
but there is, in almost every case, an impass-
able gulf between idea and execution. This state-
ment applies to the artists who have an idea.
There are not multitudes of them.
* * * *
To come to dry details. The picture by Charles
Hunt, numbered 77, is, as far as I could see, the
only one in the exhibition that contains a repro-
duction of natural sunlight shining on the face of
a wood in the evening. One feels warm by merely
looking at it. And the picture No. 2, by Aline
Cusack, is, I think, the sole painting in the
Gallery which truly represents liquid translucent
moving water. It has about it an atmosphere of
great gusty sea-laughter, and liberal breeze and
salty spray that is sea-spray and not crumpled
tinfoil. The curious thing in connection with this
picture and a few others is that they are the works
of comparative amateurs, and are indescribably
more artistic than the ambitious canvases of the
professional persons. In one of his books Richard
Jefferies says that a man may do a thing so often
that at last he is not able to do it at all. As
applied to painting, this remark means that an
artist, unless he is a first-flight man, is sooner or
later strangled by his own technique. Mr. W.
Lister-Lister, President of the Society, is a case
in point. His picture (229), entitled “ Entrance
to Port Jackson from South Reef,” is an excellent
illustration of the way in which a man with a pre-
sumably seeing eye in his head will, out of appar-
ently pure artistic cussedness, place upon his
canvas lights that—so far as I can make out —
never were on sea or land, or anywhere else.
• * * ♦
Mr. A. J. Hanson is another wag of the same
school. Nature is, seemingly, to these artists a
very crude colorist who does not know her own
business. It is her way to make her shadows dark
with a tone in them given by the object upon
which they are thrown. She certainly has her
purple shadows, but they are in the middle dis-
tance and beyond. Nine Australian artists out of
ten, however, at all cost of some effect lay on
purple even to the delirium of most dismal indigo.
And they lay it on anywhere. They have, for
instance, discovered the Purple Trees that grow
nowhere else but in the Sydney studios. Some of
the less audacious of these artistic brigands leave
out the purple, and paint their trees and shadows
a blue-gray, subtly suggestive of the shaven jaw
of a,n actor. But in no instance I have seen do
their shadows swim. And that is just the quality
of a shadow in the middle day. Most of the pic-
tures to which I refer are representations of scenes
painted in strong sunlight. I am, therefore,
within the limits of my argument. This English
language of ours is so deficient in color-terms that
one is at a loss how to express the dominant note
in Australian pictures. There is, consequently,
no other way out of the dilemma except to resort
to simile. The hue of a three-quarters-ripe dam-
son plum is the nearest comparison I have
at hand to describe the aforesaid dominant note.
In No. 24, “In Open Melody that Never Ends,”
Hanson’s waves do actually break, after a fashion,
and his shadows are natural. But what is to be
said of 193, “ Bell Creek, Wellington.” by Arthur
Collingridge? The drawing is unexceptionable, but
how can a shadow of.a tree have the same tone on
land as it has in water—more especially when it is
thrown upon another shadow? Color on color,
they say, is bad heraldry, but shadow upon
shadow, made rigidly distinct and separate, is
even worse. Eor some mysterious reason, our
Australian artists will not see that all lights and
shades and colors in nature melt into each other.
Every shadow has its penumbra. Your highly-
cultured artists will tell you that everything in
nature, as seen by the properly educated artistic
eye, has three shadows, or rather the shadow has
its own edge and then a fainter and a yet fainter
edge. But what is the use of talking? Australian
artists would continue laying on .their purples
even if Moses and the Lrophets came to re-
monstrate with them. Purpleism is a congenital
disease with them, and most of their pictures bear
the inevitable petit bleu birth-mark.
* * * *
After making the foregoing unpleasant remarks
it is pleasing to be able to say with a clear con-
science that Hanson’s Picture of a Young Lady
{l68 —“ Maydo”) is one of the best things of its
kind in the Exhibition. There is in the eyes of
that handsome young woman a limpid light and
vital sparkle one seldom sees in painted portraits.
But the airiest and most delicate thing of this sort
in the Exhibition is Mary Stoddard's picture of
Miss Burdekin. It has a tender grace and charm
which are curiously foreign in that gallery.
* * * *
The large pictures are all unsatisfying. Gordon
Coutts’ canvas entitled “The Cloud that comes
to all ” would have been a better picture if it had
either a sunnier or a darker background. And Mr.
Van der Velden’s picture, “Disillusioned,” for
which he asks J!l050, is about the sort of thing
that should delight the soul of Bland Holt. The
young wife, with her baby in a cradle behind her,
is sitting silent and gazing with desolate eyes into
the Past. Her look and attitude would have told
her story in quite sufficient fashion without the
melodramatic addition of the scattered cards and
broken jug in the left foreground. Why should
not an artist know that there is more potency in
suggestiveness than in hammered-out statement?
The very breath of the nostrils of all art is sug-
gestiveness. Take as a fine sample of this quality
Mahoney’s picture, “Peaceful Evening” (No.
40), for which he asks fifty-two guineas. The
treatment of the misty moon, and the dim silver-
grey scene upon which the white horse in
the foreground gazes contemplatively, are fine
to the limits of present praise. One can
almost imagine what that white horse is thinking
about. I his is emphatically a picture for artists
by an artist.
* * • •
Julian Ashton’s wreck of the “ Hereward ” is a
fine thing in its way—sombre and bleak and cold
wuh a sky breaking to rain. The ability of the
artist is unmistakable, but I do not think that an
artist has any need to make one shiver. No. 70,
“The Three Sisters,” a Blue Mountain theme, by
Mr. A. Henry Fullwood, with its swimming dia-
phanous mists rising from the gorge below the
peaks, is a picture painted by a painter. The rocks
in the foreground are simply admirable. His paint-
lng (154), “Reflections,’’is in another, and perhaps
more interesting, key than the aforementioned.
The figures in the background are, however, out
of drawing] to an inexcusable extent. The very
odd thing in this connection is that Mr. Fullwood
should not have seen this for himself. But even
the best painters are not infallible. It is only
critics who are so constructed. Ralph A. Bay-
ley s picture (136), “In a Yorkshire Fishing Vil-
lage, a Misty Morning,” with its delicate greys and
soft atmosphere, is, though small, a fine example
of what a man can do with his tools when he
knows how to use them. Mr. Bayley, lam told, is
a young English artist who has come to Australia
in quest of health. In the meantime, he paints
portraits that are portraits, and not chromo-
lithographic insults to the eye. As “Consider
the Lilies,” by Mr. Moore-Jones, is one of the
largest canvases in the show, I suppose it should be
mentioned. Very well: to me it is simply awful,
laken altogether, the Exhibition is depressing,
there is no trace, or even promise, of strong,
original work in it. It would be delightful to me
to speak differently. But that would belying.
And there are other people on the staff of this dis-
solute journal who can do all the lying that is
necessary.

. Adelaide has got hold of the tired-feeling
virus. The Holy City had the national Austra-
lian malady in a particularly epidemic and viru-
lent form, the other week. Then rain fell ; where-
upon it was observed that a quantity of fine dust,
like powdered sulphur, had been precipitated.
And immediately Adelaide was attacked by a
tremendous energy. It is suggested that the
powder is the pollen of the wattle and the other
treasures of the spring poet.
Q. Premier Byrnes’s swift shut-down on
the Workman’s Lien Bill was a big relief to the
much-embarrassed “native-born.” He wants to
shed himself of his colleagues, but he doesn’t
know how to do so without relinquishing the
helm. His action with this Bill has naturally
raised the antagonistic chorus—“ What’s the use
of taking a Bill into committee if M’s.P. are not
to be permitted to amend it?” And there is no
satisfactory answer to the plaint.
Cable tells of the death of the wife of Hori
Grey, from whom the great proconsul had been
estranged for 40 years, but to whom he was recon-
ciled a year or two back. The reconciliation was
brought about by Lord Stamford, who is related
to Grey. The story goes that when Stamford
suggested to Grey that he should meet his wife
the old man did not encourage the idea, but a por-
trait of the lady was hung in his room, and he was
often observed to regard it long and wistfully.
Eventually Grey consented to meet his wifi- and
the reconciliation was brought about. The de-
ceased lady was a daughter of Admiral Spencer,
and her marriage to Grey took place GO years ago.
Premier George Reid cursing in N.S.W.
Assembly at the Federation Bill:
Mr. Wise s explanation is that the Dominion was ar-
ranged in three different parts, and an equal number of
senators given to each. But the fact is this, and tbf
effect of that explanation is that six independent pro-
vinces before the Union have only 33 senators in the
Union, and two other independent provinces before the
L nion have 48. If that makes a similar case to that which
exists in this Federation, perhaps my hon. friends will
allow't to go in. (Laughter and cheers.) To emphasise
the Canadian illustration, I will say that two provinces of
a population of 3,600,(100 souls have 48 senators, and six
other provinces with a population of 1,200,000 have 33
senators. Now, in our case two provinces with a popula-
tion of 2J millions have 12 senators, and three States with
a population of 700,000 have 18. '(Laughter.) Now how
that can be said to be a similar adjustment to the
Canadian system requires a considerable amount of ex-
planation. All I can say is, if it is the same thing, give it
to us, and there will be no more trouble about it.
And yet George Reid voted for tbis arrangement
m the Convention, and, according to his list of
amendments, lie is willing, even now, to let it
stand, and is still in favor of it. Same old George!
A great and clumsy Australian romancer
has arisen in England. This gentleman (a French-
man, De Rougemont) claims to have found quite a
number of vast sea-monsters in Torres Straits—
where the British-India steamers and multitudes
of other craft pass through regularly without drop-
ping on any of his sea-serpents and other horrors.
Ihe boss monster was a devil-fish which reached
up and yanked the pearl-fisher out of his boat.
Also he dropped on a race of giants in the same
locality. He was marooned for two and a-half
years on a sand-bank (where he presumably lived
on raw sand), and was afterwards for 30 years
chief of a cannibal tribe in northern Wes-
tralia. He was located, apparently, not very
far from the scene of Kimberley gold rush, in a
locality where the naked and penniless nig is con-
stantly harried and moved on by the W.A.
trooper, and lie solemnly swears that‘the wretched
darkies thereabouts use “pots and pans of virgin
gold.” De Rougemont’s tribe was a great mili-
tary organisation, not in the least like the
scarce and badly-scared Australian aboriginal, but
more resembling the Zulus. While pervading
these parts he found a demented white man, who
proved to be the explorer Gibson (Gibson died in
quite a different part of Australia in 72), and
rescued two white women who were captives
among the natives. De Rougemont, having evi-
dently a wild idea that Northern W.A, is a per-
fectly unexplored land, and not knowing how
the local cannibal is looked after and care-
fully run in to be flogged for his own good
in the lock-up at Roeburne, goes into strange
gyrations about the botany and geography
of the country, and, so far, Britain seems to take
him rather seriously. De Rougemont is an even
more primitive story-teller than Calvert. He
claims to know the place where the native digs
out the great slabs of virgin gold, which his pots
and pans are made of, so possibly he has in view
something in the mine-flotation way. The Bul-
letin sees it recorded that Prof. Crookes (the
illustrious radiometer-man) was somewhat cautious
in introducing M. de Rougemont as a lecturer to
the Royal Society. The pallid Crookes isn’t an
enthusiast in fish-stories.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
N.S.W. has begun its new financial year with
good prospects. The revenue for July was £837,750, an
increase of £17,118 as compared with July 1897. For
August the N.S.W. receipts were £097,158, which is
£83,684 more than the amount received in August 1897.
Therefore the two months show an increase of £100,802 as
compared with the corresponding two months of last year.
And the expenditure for same period (£1.413,477) is
£13,429 less than that for July and August, 1897 ; also, up
to date, the State income is £121,431 ahead of the out-
goings. Which would be a splendid result if it wasn’t
arrived at by shunting more and more of the public ex-
penditure into loan-account. Under any decently honest
system of government the increased revenue would
be a reason for reducing loan expenditure, and
paying more of the national expenses out of revenue,
but the policy of the great wriggler isn’t built
that way. During the two months £99,155 was added to
the public debt, and the loan expenditure was £303,537 —
which is £65,471 more than the loan expenditure for July
and Aug., ’97. The loan expenditure helps to make a
kind of temporary artificial prosperity, and the artificial
prosperity helps to keep up the revenue, and the increase
of revenue is held as a proof of the justifiableness of more
loan expenditure—and so the game goes on.
* * * *
Queensland revenue for August, 1898, was
£288,664, an increase of £28,410 as compared with the
figures for Aug., ’97. But Queensland's alleged boom is an
even worse lie and swindle than that of N.S.W. Last
year the kanaka province made a burst of prosperity by
borrowing and spending £1,220,000, which, in proportion
to population, was twice as large a loan expenditure as
that of N.S.W., and nearly approached the worst doings
of the old Parkes days in Sydney or the Gillies riot in
Melbourne. This year it proposes to make an even bigger
burst of prosperit.y by borrowing and spending £1,536,090,
equal to about £3 ss. per inhabitant, or £l6 ss. for every
family of five. A certain amount of affluence has been
created by this wild scattering of borrowed cash, and
that borrowed affluence naturally makes the revenue look
up a little. Queensland has already, in proportion to
population, the Biggest Debt on Earth ; it owes £72 per
inhabitant, while Victoria, at the other end of the Aus-
tralasian list, owes £4O. And therein lies the whole story
of Queensland’s present little boom, and of the great
burst that will follow.
* * • •
Westralia’s August revenue was .£221,045 —a
fall of £25,428 as compared with Aug., ’97. For July and
Aug. the revenue is £42,372 less than that of July' and
Aug., ’97. If the drop continues at the present rate, the
yeaFs revenue will be fully £250,000 behind that of ’97,
and it will take much hard sacking of the miserable public

increase of £5944 as compared with Aug., ’97. Eight)

The August revenue in Victoria (£534,234)
shows an increase of £34,433 as compared with August,
1897 ; and for July and Aug. the increase, as compared
with the corresponding months of last year, is £39,605.
Premier Turner reckoned that the revenue for the year
ending June, ’99, would be £20,775 better than last year’s
total, and, unless a slump sets in, he has already realised
his expected increase nearly twice over. September
starts, too, with the death of a rich man named Smith,
whose estate panned-out for £24,627 probate-duty.
* * * ♦
The National Bank of Maoriland is steadily re-
covering from the awful episode that struck it in 1885,
when £lOO,OOO of its capital disappeared, and the other
awful episode in 1891, when holders gave £2 11s. 3d. per
share to have the scrip taken off their hands, and it was
found necessary to write off £150,000 out of the remain-
ing £250,000 paid-up capital, and make a call of £150,000.
The profits for the year ended 31st March, 1898, are
£25,538, equal to t'9 2s. sd. per cent, per annum on the
shareholders’ funds, which is enormously above the pre-
sent average of banking profits. A div. of 5 per cent,
and 1 per cent, bonus absorb £15,000 ; £lO,OOO is added
to reserve, making it £40,000 ; and the other £538, with
previous balance, leaves £4195 at credit of profit and loss.
The profits of the N.B. of M.L., since it got over that un-
lucky episode in ’9l, have been as follows :
March, 1892 .. £19,335 March, 1896 .. £18,090
„ 1893 .. 10,344 „ 1897 .. 21,461
„ 1894 .. 16,997 „ 1898 .. 25,538
„ 1895 .. 16,431
Out of this £134,196, representing seven years’ profits,
the National, as already mentioned, has created a £40,000
reserve, so it has at least done something to repair the
£250,000 loss sustained by the shareholders in ’B5 and ’9l,
though it has still a good way to go before these disasters
are made good. The National has a large and steadily
increasing business. Its reserve of £431,087 cash and
£100,355 in State securities equals a shade under 20 per
cent, of all liabilities to the public—which is reasonably
good, though not by any means above the average. Alto-
gether, however, the institution is doing remarkably well.
Other things being equal, the difference between
the bank which makes large profits and the one which
doesn’t is very simple. Assuming that the assets are
perfectly sound in each case, the way to make a large
percentage of profit is to run a large business on a small
paid-up capital. As a case in point, the National Bank of
M.L. trades on £290,000 shareholders’ funds against nearly
£2,700,009 belonging to the public, and makeslarge profits.
As an example of the other kind of thing, the Royal Bank
(Melb.) trades on £150,000 of its own money and only
£320,000 belonging to the public, and its profits are com-
paratively small. In case of a great slump in values, the
advantage, so far as the safety of depositors is concerned,
is all with the class to which the Royal belongs, as it has
over four times as large a margin ; but as dividend-earn-
Ing concerns, the Royal of Melbourne, and concerns like
It, have their disadvantages.

The Union Bank of Australia issues (very late)
Its balance-sheet for the half-year ended 2Sth February,
1898. So far as profits are concerned, it is the best half-
year, with one exception, that the bank has experienced
since August, 1893. Some recent profits of the Union are
as follows :
February,’93 .. £69,609 February,’96 .. £37,996
August, ’93 .. 60,295 August, ’96 .. 58,837
February, ’94 .. 44,132 February, ’97 .. 37,578
August,’94 .. 44,676 August,’97 .. 37,436
February, *95 .. 37,172 February,’9B .. 47,696
August, ’95 .. 36,063
The profit equals £3 16s. 4d. per cent, per annum on the
shareholders’ funds (paid-up capital, £1,500,000 ; reserve,
£750,000; contimrent account, £250,000), which is rather
good as the average of bank earnings go nowadays. The
usual dividend at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum
absorbs £37,500 ; another £lO,OOO is written off bank pre-
mises and other property ; and the remaining £196, with
previous balance, leaves £24,699 at credit of profit and loss
account. In a general way the Union looks prosperous. Cash
and liquid securities (£4,923,456) are £1,009,000 less than
they were six months ago, and the advances have in-
creased to about the same amount. The reserve of cash
and securities is still solid enough for all purposes, equal-
ling over 27 per cent, on all liabilities to the public, and
the fact that there is a million less money festering to no
good purpose in the cellar probably accounts for the large
increase in the earnings. Huge cash reserves have a
sensational aspect in the balance-sheet, but they' tend to
make business eminently unprofitable.

The once great Victoria Insurance Co. (Mel-
bourne) is now only the ghost, of its old self, and its
balance-sheet for the year ended 30th June, 1898, is
especially solemn reading. The last six years’ record of
this co. reads as follows :
Profit. Dividend Paid.
June, 1893 .. £34,084 44} per cent. .. £22,468
„ 1894 .. 24,400 „ „ .. 22,468
„ 1895 .. 20,741 401 „ .. 20,221
„ 1896 .. 17,058 352 » .. 17,974
» 1897 .. 18,010 „ „ M 17,974
Loss.
June, 1898 ... 23,336 Nil.
Profits .. £90,957 Dividends .. £101,105
This shows that the co. has paid away (10,148 more than
its earnings during six years. And in addition to this there
is a further loss of £20,000 through depreciation of in-
vestments, so during the past six years the Victoria has
gone back over £30,000. The fact that a larg'e amount in
the way of depreciation had to be provided for was
tacitly admitted for sometime back, and, despite this fact,
the management in 1896 and 1897 paid away practi-
cally' the whole of its profits in enormous dividends,
and made no visible effort whatever to meet the difficulty.
And, though the great Melbourne fire could not be defi-
nitely' foreseen, such disasters are of periodical occur-
rence, and when a co. with a large business and compara-
tively 7 small funds blews all its earnings in 35 per cent,
dividends during good seasons it is practically inviting
disaster. The Victoria Insurance co. is now a compara-
tively small concern with £50,178 paid-up capital, £25,000
reserve, and £11,838 re-insurance fund. The income from
interest and rents has dropped from £5804 in 1893 to
£3614, and the premium-income remains much the same.
Evidently' the co. wants a lot less 35 per cent, dividend
and a lot more persistent effor,tto build up a solid reserve.
Another three years like the last would leave compara-
tively little of the old Victoria standing.

The Victoria General Insurance and Guarantee
Co. (Melbourne), which is a species of baby relative to the
other Victoria, also presents a depressing balance-sheet
for the year ended 30th June, ’9B. Owing to the-great
Melb. fire, the year’s operations show a loss of £5Oll, and
an utterly unjustifiable 10 per cent, dividend absorbs
£2OOO, so that the co. ends the year £7Oll worse off than
it was at the beginning. The last six years’ record of this
co. is also rather interesting :
Profits. Dividends.
June, 1893 .. £6283 .. 15 per cent. = £3OOO
„ 1894 .. 6477 .. 20 „ = 4000
„ 1595 .. 5629 ..20 „ = 4000
„ 1896 .. 4957 ..25 „ = 5000
„ 1897 .. 3689 ..20 „ = 4000
Loss.
„ 1898 .. 5011 ..10 „ = 2000
Profit, £22,054 Dividends, £22,000
For three successive years the Victoria G.I. and G. Co.
has paid away more, in 10, 20 and 25 per cent, dividends,
than its entire earnings. It is a very small affair at the
best—with £20,000 paid-up capital, £9OOO reserves, and
£1494 profit and loss balance ; and if the management
continues to howl after big dividends as urgently as it has
been doing the co. is never likely to be much bigger.
Moreover, though the co. is, on paper, £54 further ahead
than it was six years ago, the probabilities are that
it is really about £4OOO behind. A memo in the balance-
sheet states that the mortgages are put in at their old
paper-value, and that losses are allowed for in the contin-
gency account, so presumably the contingency reserve of
£4OOO is now only a paper item which has been swallowed
up by losses not yet written off. The policy of keeping a
paper-reserve on one sideagainst paper-assetson the other,
instead of writing them both off, is mere stupid inflation.
The premium-income has decreased from £12,702 in 1893
(which wasn’t much) to £10,758 in 1898, and in the same
period the interest from investments has dropped from
£1854 to £1054. As the interest is only £3 Is. per cent,
per annum on the actual investments, it certainly looks
as if some of them wanted writing down very badly in-
deed, and as if the whole of the £4OOO contingency fund
would be wanted to meet the loss. Even if that £4OOO
was all written off, the investments would yield less than
31 per cent, per annum, and that certainly wouldn’t look as
if they hafl been written down any too low. Altogether,
the small Victoria looks about as cheerless this year as the
big one, and the bloated-dividend complaint seems to be
eating away the foundations of both of them.
* « * *
The old Victoria Life Insurance Co. goes on
dying slowly and respectably. It takes no new risks
nowadays, but merely sits on its pile and watches the old
assurers pegging out. When the last one pegs there
will probably be a big sum for the shareholders to divide.
Last year assurances to the amount of £12,340 fell in, and
after paying them and allotting a 12. j per cent,
dividend (£5000) to shareholders the funds are only'
about £3500 less than they were at the begin-
ning of the year. Probably in course of time the
Victoria will have only one policy-holder left and
will have £lOO,OOO or so to divide among its share-
holders when he dies. And as likely as not that last
insurer will hang on all b.y himself for 20 years, and
refuse to take his surrender value or to get out on any
terms, and the office will have to be kept open for the
sole purpose of collecting the lone veteran’s premium
once a year, and the last days of the Victoria Life Co. will
be disfigured with much cursing.

Balmain (Sydney) New Ferry co. publishes a
rather remarkable balance-sheet. It sets forth that the
revenue for the half-year to 30th June, 1898, was £6097, as
compared with £6391 for previous half-year, and the trading
profit, after paying all working expenses and writing off
£5BB for depreciation, is £B9O, against £1306 for previous
half. No div. was paid for the six months ended 31st
Dec., ’97, owing to the disastrous fire that struck the co.’s
fleet; which leaves £2196 accumulated profit for the year.
The fire-loss absorbs £2004 of this, so the net profit for
the year is £192. Applying the insurance reserve
(£120) towards covering the fire loss, and including
previous profit and loss balance of £1512, the
amount at credit of profit and loss is £lB2l, and a half-
year’s div. at 10 per cent, per annum reduces this to
£1145. So far everything is perfectly plain. The re-
markable feature is that while the balance-sheet says that
£451 has been written off the value of the co’s steamers
it appears from the same sheet that £1144 has really
been added on to their paper-value—at least they now
stand in the balance-sheet at £15,530, whereas six months
ago they only represented £14,386. Of course it is pos-
sible that they have been so greatly improved as to be
worth the extra value put on them, but no allusion to
this very extensive writing-up is made in the report. And
any extensive writing-up of properties (the value of one
vessel rises at a jump from £2984 to £3839) is always more
or less —well, worthy of note.
m * * *
The Life Assurance Act Amending Bill now
before Yic. Upper House proposes to make the policy un-
assailable on score of misstatement of age, and so avoid
the lamentable spectacle of the widow bill-of-saleing her
furniture to fight a wealthy co. The Bill proposes to
make all present policies unchallengeable on that score,
and for the future the co’s will, of course, take care of
themselves. Big handicap to have to prove a dead per-
son’s age, as the cadaver frequently carries the knowledge
with him. Bill also makes policy up to £lOOO unseizable
by legal process ; and, on insolvency within two years of
policy’s inception, insolvent can appoint agent, who can
purchase it for not more than amount of premiums paid,
and hold it for benefit of family ; in case of death within
two years of inception, should estate be insolvent without
the £lOOO, policy would only be liable for the premiums
paid, and the balance of the thousand be available for the
family. The Bill is being a good deal bucketed by the
House of Landlords, who don’t, or won’t, know the in-
surance-agent’s pleasing little practice of verbally modify-
ing the written contract and leaving the insurer to stand
by the letter thereof.

Broken Hill mine-managers, who, sprinkled
with two or three thousand a-year men, constitute a very
high and mighty body, prepared themselves to give the
N.S.W. Govt. Metallurgist (Mr. Taylor) a Klondyke recep-
tion. Reason was the publication in all the dailies of a
par. that, at their request, Mr. Taylor was going up to
consult with them as to refractory-ore treatment ! And,
when you come to think that these men have been speci-
ally' trained, and have been living with this problem,
working with it, and eating with it, and sleeping with it
for years past, it was a bit “steep.” Of course they
denied at once that they had made any representations
on the subject.
* * * *
Since last week’s meeting of Great Cobar share-
holders it has leaked out that the value of the mine’s
production for the half-year was £127,441, that the chair-
man estimated the cost of production at £68,000, thus
showing a profit for the six months of £59,441. Copper
produced, 1723 tons—so the value of the bullion would be
£73 19s. 4d. per ton. That would bring the producing
cost to £39 9s. 3d. a ton. It is impossible to get at the
exact figures without the tributors’ assistance, and they'
think it suits their business to keep those figures quiet.
But as the Cobar ore is probably the simplest blast-
furnacing proposition in Australasia—not excepting Mt.
Lyell—it is unreasonable to assume that their cost of
production is the greatest, such as this estimate would
make it. As one well-known metallurgist put it :“ I
don’t call it smelting—it is simply ‘melting.’” Since
these amounts were earned the output from the mine has
much increased, as shown by' the bigger weekly sales of
copper. No doubt the mine is now working at a total
profit of £150,000 a year, which is about the total amount
it paid in upwards of 20 years, when the grade of the
ore was much higher, but when nothing was got for its
gold, when it was handicapped with heavy road carriage,
and worked the old reverberatory system. Changed
conditions have had a marvellous effect on this mine, and
probably will have on some others.

The Garden Gully United (Bendigo) has now
paid 17s. 6d. per share, all within six weeks, from the new
reef at 1440 ft., and the shares are something like 18s.
paid-up. The last dividend from the old reef was
paid on 2nd Aug., ’94, and then a common opinion was
that the grand old Garden Gully' mine was “ done.” But
the Bendigo speculator comes next to Job in the matter
of patience and dogged perseverance, and he pegged
away. A 4loz. reef and over 3000 oz. of retorted gold for
a fortnight’s work are a good reward for these four years
of ill-luck.
• • * *
Though the most.-talked-of Bendigo mines are
sweating away at 3000 ft. or so, there are scores of pro-
mising ones which have not chiselled out the gold beyond
400 ft. y'et. The other day the Prince of Wales United re-
solved to get away' down a bit faster; and, to help it on,
it has put up a new battery. What the claims yielded in
the very early' days is not known ; in the next few years
the profit was ' 80,000 ; and in modern times, under the
present co.—which had to be helped on by Govt, prospect-
ing-aid—£3l,ooo has been paid in dividends in about four
years, while three times as much has been disbursed in
other directions. And all from the stuff above the 300 ft.
level.

Dear Bulletin.—l wish to join the Society for
Being rather Tired of Myself and my Critics. 1 didn’t
start the talk—l merely chipped in, in refutation of the
plain ignorance and the fancy lies of the Chillagoe
damsters. Just this in final answer, and I put on a
muzzle. “ Old Miner ” says he sampled Mt. Garnet for
average of 5 per cent, before it was taken up, and pre-
sumably, therefore, his 5 per cent, average was from sur-
face. Also, he must have done the sampling 15 years ago
—because for nearly that time the mine has been a free-
hold. I state again that the 104 ft. of lode-width disclosed
by the Ml. Garnet tunnel averages 10 per cent, copper
and 20oz. silver. The S.M. Herald reporter, writing a
few days ago, puts the copper contents at from 12
to 15 per cent. I did not say that there were
20,000 tons of ore in sight on the Girofla lode. I
said there were 22,000 tons in sight in the Giro-
fla mine, and I now say it again, and, in spite of
“ R. 8.” saying it, it is true. Also Jack and Stewart and
other big men say the Garnet lode will go down to great
depths—not to the depth of the father of lies, because he
has no depth and he writes to The Bulletin as “Old
Miner.” “ Briton ” attacks iny statement re Keelbottom.
I have seen a prospectus, and what I meant by stating
that the property was not necessarily floated was merely
in allusion to the fact that a London “ registration ” does
not cover “flotation” as it does in Melbourne. I have
not seen the Keelbottom, and I’m ready to bet that
“ Briton ” hasn’t seen Chillagoe, but reputable men have
told me what I have written you of its elusive richness,
and I know several who dropped money over the mine in
past capitalisations, and who state that it was given a fair
run and will not pay. Elijah’s mantle has not fallen on
me and my own clothes are good enough. As the secre-
tary of the 5.8.1t.T., R.B. says, let the Chillagoe field talk,
and talk it can. Fifteenthly, and in conclusion, I am
“ terribly anxious to prop up the Chillagoe boom.” The
field is the biggest copper-silver country in Australia, and
boom it shall—Randolph Bedford.

If Father Long's sensational announcement at
Kanowna concerning the much-doubted finding of the
“ Sickle ” nugget had been looked at in cold print there
would not have been much of a stampede out that way.
Instead of telling the crowd where it was found, all he
could say—or, anyway, all he did say—was that he
thought it came from a place (Lake Gwynne) about 4£
miles out. But the locality is so unpromising, and there
is so little local evidence in support of the find, that 24
hours after the crowd had swarmed over the gully there
were only half-a-dozen forlorn men making any pretence
at work. And the wily Father had pledged the diggers
beforehand not to ask him any more questions about the
b usiness.
* • * •
“Parley”: The Sacred Slug of Kanowna has
never looked like anything but the work of a fictionist.
There has been too much mystery, too much theatrical
flam, too much rumor, and too little evidence. Tub Bul-
letin now publishes more tangible statements. “It was
(said to have been) found by two men from Cassilis (Gipps-
land), named Brodie and Millaine.” That is candid, and
ought to bring a response. Having only the evidence of
newspaper telegrams, I doubt the Sacred Slug, and
I fear that Brodie and Millaine are myths. “The
big slug is now at Melb. Mint.” Rats 1 The big
slug weighs nearly a cwt. It was deposited with
a bank. That bank would ship it to Melb. And—why
didn’t it get a cheap and legitimate advt. by exhibiting
it, as was done with former big finds, in some jeweller’s
window? Why did the bank not hold a little levee with
special invitations to the reporters ? Simply because no
Melb. bank has received the Sacred Slug. The banks are
the only ones in the gold-escort business. “ Brodie and
Millaine ” would not employ any others as agents—and
they would not themselves lug about a clumsy cwt. of
misshapen gold. Firstly, they would have to smuggle it
out of Westralia, or else reveal it to the customs officers,
and thereby disclose their secret. Secondly, they would
fear the almost absolute certainty of having their secret
discovered on the steamer. Thirdly, there is the great
suspicion that broods in the West, arid that has separated
so many otherwise unsuspicious partners. One rich blow
was discovered by a band of good friends. After the find
they separated into distinct camps, cooking their own
food for fear of poison. This has again and again
happened Out West, and “ Brodie and Millaine ”
would probably get under the influence. Would they be
likely to cart their clumsy treasure about, only their’ two
selves knowing the secret ? Then, again, 1 have not seen
anywhere that Father Long actually said he saw the pre-
cious slug. The finders’ joint yarn’, told with much cir-
cumstance and seriousness, would quite possibly mislead
the priest. But, again, has he ever said that his own eyes
have seen the Sacred Nugget ? Lastly, why the necessity
for secresy ? Once deposited with the Union Bank, the
credit of the institution is good enough, even if a £5OOO
nugget were stolen from it. If it is not in any bank, it is
probably the delusion of sick men. If it were in a bank,
it would not be lying there idle. Escorts of parcels of
gold worth much more than this are common enough all
over Australia, including the West. My verdict is : no
nugget. Moreover, was there an application for a re-
ward claim—and if not, why not ?

General Mining
The new air compressor at the Long Tunnel
mine, Walhalla, Victoria, is giving great satisfaction, and
has made a continuous run, night and day, of over
18 months, without once stopping. Manager Thomson
speaks in the highest terms of it. The plant is a 350
I.H.P. cross compound double stage air compressor, with
Corliss type air and steam valves, and reflects great credit
on the Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South Mel-
bourne, who manufactured and erected it. This firm has
lately purchased the plant and stock of the old-established
Langlands Foundry co., now in liquidation, and, as it
has all the patterns, can supply duplicate parts of any
machines made by Langlands.*

Society
Australian art has not yet reached the Age
of Consent. At Sydney Society of Artists’ ex-
hibition there were 19 fruit and flower pretties,
and only two studies from the nude.

The pious Chow doesn’t hide his light
under a bushel. A .Rockhampton Chinaman
afhxes following notice to his shop: —“Being a
Christian, I wish to notify to general public that
I intend to close my establishment on Sundays,
and I respectfully invite their attendance on
Saturday evenings, thus conducing to respectful
observance of Lord’s l)ay. ngned—Jimmy Bung
Lee.' ’

Sydney boarding-house keepers are just
now called on just before meal-times by gents in
tjuest of board and lodging, who, having just
arrived by boat, have left luggage on wharf till
they have found suitable apartments. Of course
when terms have been agreed upon the new
lodger has lunch, dinner or tea, according to hour
and then departs to get luggage— not to return !
* • * »
The other day a Sydney woman told
Judge Simpson that she discovered her husband’s
lapse from virtuebylisteningtohimtalk in his sleep.
His tones were plaintive and he was earnestly
pleading to “dear Mrs. Blank” to let him
go home. This, apparently, Mrs. Blank was
unwilling to do; for, later on, it was alleged
that she took him away altogether, and the
Court smashed up the marriage. Men usually
call the women they hug by some pet-name, the
more they hug them the more absurd the name.
But this one was like Tristram Shandy’s dad,
who, even in his most delirious moments, never
failed to call the lady Mrs. Shandy.
A moral, husbands, here from life :
Remember, when inclined to rake,
The man who would deceive his wife
Must be extremely wide-awake 1
* * • *
The number of cottages in connection with
the N.S.W. Govt, poor asylums is limited and the
demand for them is keen, as life therein is much
easier than in the main buildings. A month or
two ago, a female inmate died, and, as is ous-
tomary, the surviving husband was instructed to
prepare for transfer to the men’s asylum at the
end of the week. Loth to lose his comfortable
quarters, the old fellow struck a bargain with the
aged woman who cleaned the cottages, herself an
asylum inmate, and a hasty ceremony was gone
through. The bridal couple, whose combined ages
totalled 140 years, applied for the cottage, but,
alas, the departmental head had no sense of the
romantic, and gave it to the next applicant.
• ♦ * *
At a N.Q. town recently a pretty little
Yum-yum strolled into a fashionable shop to
negotiate for a tailor-made costume. The tailor-
man, after much laborious dumb-show, got the
little lady to understand that she would have to
be measured, whereupon Yum-yurn, with a sudden
"sweep, unrolled about three fathoms of cummer-
bund, plucked deftly at a fastening or two, and,
with a single wriggle, stood before the paralysed
Snip clad in nothing but a smile of seraphic inno-
• cenoe!
The “little brown woman” Jap. lydy is per-
sistently making her way down Queensland coast
—she. is now well established in Townsville. Her
English, of course, is rudimentary, but she gets
along all right with a silvery little laugh that
adequately fills the conversational gaps. Her
linguistic education is apparently imparted on a
rigidly utilitarian plan, as she appears to under-
stand nothing of what is said to her, but simply
waits for the interviewer to pause and then chip
in—“ Me on’y sixeteen ; sem’teen neshee mont’.”
Then, next chance she gets: “ On’y one wick
here ; nichee place. You nichee gem’man: plen-
dee monee, wha’ ?—ha, ha ! ”
“ Glory ” : “ Scotty the Wrinkler ” prefers
men to women nurses ; most soldiers don’t. I was
on ship-board with a crowd of the Army Hospital
corps. One day I went on the forecastle-head,
the jib vyas lying unfurled on deck, and two of the
corps were lying in it. The sail formed a screen
from deck to stay, and I, unobserved, heard this
pleasant confab : “ You fellahs at the front will
have a show to make a bit. By the time they get
down to us, at the base, they’ll be skinned.”
“ Most of ’em’s stony—there’ll be very little for us,
if you ask me.” “Get out; they all drew a
month’s pay the day before they left, and lots of
’em have got watches and rings, and things. You
coves will be all right—l wish we were going to
the front.’’ A soldier told me that he was badly
wounded in the thigh at Majuba, and one of these
ghouls “ went through him,” and left him to bleed
to death—one of his own company found him un-
conscious, and brought him in. The stories that
soldiers who’ve seen active service tell of the male
army nurse would give a black snake the night-
mare. Besides, the ordinarily-constituted male
will get well just for the sake of trying to mash
his nurse—if she’s under 50.

Dear Bulletin. —l do not wish to discuss
“ Breaker’s ” neatly attired nymph of the hospital,
but by same post as the B. came the accompany-
ing letter, and the subject of it was mentioned in
my irritating comment on female attendants. I
named a friend as a victim of their barbarity.
I do not say they are all so, because, like
“Breaker,” 1 was refitted in Mooroopna Hospital,
where the nurses were perfect. My old chum
says of the N.S. W. asylum to which he was sent
I am not in hospital, but turned out in the yard like
an old horse in a paddock to get better or die! On my
arrival here the doctor ordered medicine for me, and told
me to go in the yard from 6 a.m. till 6p m. They take
your flannels from you, and give you all sorts of old
clothes to wear, covered with lice. Language fails me to
give you a description of the place—looo men—4oo in
hospital and 100 in the yard like out-door patients. If I
were able to travel I would not remain one night. There
is only one doctor to all the inmates.
All this is from a manly man who, I assure you,
does not know how to lie. We thought he would
get better attention there than here, or he would
never have been allowed to go. Even in a “6x
8 ” tent I could make him more comfortable than
he is, and we shall do so ; but we must have the
matter of the cold yard and filth seen into. His
dog is here in charge of a friend.— Scotty the
Wrinkler.

ing into a big grievance,
and the Church must
look to it.

Regard the “girls’ curriculum” fathered by-
Brisbane (Q.) Girls’ Grammar-School trustees, for
the competitive examinations for “ the Queen’s
Scholarship ” :
Inorganic chemistry, physics, geology, physiology, zoo-
logy and botany ; English, French, German,’ Latin, arith-
metic, algebra, geometry, history of Europe, ancient his-
tory, Greek, plane trigonometry, and mechanics.
Hey for the sweet girl graduate ! Not one art
subject not even drawing and painting. No
plastic art, such as sculpture and moulding. No
poetry (thank Heaven !). No music. Even his-
tory is to be ancient and European. Australian
history is, apparently, in the egg. What sort of a
“ She who must be obeyed ” is in incubation in
Alienalia ?
* * * *
Brisbane has run its course of dengue-
fever and beri-beri, and has partly climbed the
steeps of influenza, only to descend into the mala-
rial valley of measles, diphtheria, pneumonia, and
tuberculosis. Every second person you meet in
Brisbane has either had measles, or feels them
coining on ; every person whom you don't meet is
abed with them. Influenza has now stretched out
suckers Westward, and almost every family in
Roma district has at least one member low with it.
* * * *
Ail enthusiastic dog-lover angrily protests
against’the recent slaying by two Sydney subur-
ban constables of a tyke which prevented them
from investigating the condition of his master,
lying apparently insensible against a tree. “ Con-
temptible butchery of a faithful animal ! ” and so
on. The Bulletin dunno. The “ faithful
mimal ” has occasionally been known to make a
light repast off his defunct idol, and present case
recalls another instance in which a N.S.W. police-
trooper was severely censured for having shot the
guardian-dog—until it was discovered that said
guardian’s affection for hi§ master was so strong
as to have induced him to assimilate portions of
its object. The canine-fidelity legend has numer-
ous large holes in it.

During the hearing of an alleged criminal-
assault case in Launceston (Tas.) Supreme Court
the other day, the foreman of the jury asked that
the court might be cleared ; but the Judge replied
that he had no power to order such a thing.
Counsel Clarke then suggested that his Honor
might follow the example of the Irish Judge, who,
upon having it pointed out to him that several
women had not complied with a request to leave,
observed, loudly, “ Oh, it doesn’t matter !—all the
decent women are gone ! ” (Fact. Baron Penne-
father). Judge Dodds, however, declined the
responsibility of saying that anybody was not
“ respectable”—and nobody left the court. When
the question of the indecency of Havelock Ellis’s
“Sexual Inversion ’’-treatise was before a London
police-court, the magistrate plainly told the
stylish women present that to remain would be
gross indecency on their part, at any rate—but not
a petticoat budged.
Bargo ” : Coming home late, one cold
night recently, over Pyrmont bridge, Sydney,
found a man sound asleep ; legs dangling over the
water, hands clasping the bridge-railing. Awak-
ened him, and said he: “Yer the seventh
idiot that’s roused me ternight! Can’t
yer see I’m right over the stack of
this little steamer aside the bridge
that’s got half-steam up, and
I’m as warm as toast ? . . .
Fall in? Well, if I do fall in
it’s none of your business,
anyhow ! ” And before I
had recovered from my as-
tonishment he was snoring
again.

“Pak-Mar” : The pro-
sperity of a nation depends
upon the number of its crimi-
nals. Thus—the more crimi-
nals, the more police; the
more police, the greater the
contentment of the cooks ; the
more contented the cooks, the
better the cooking, and, through
it, the health of the nation. Health
is. both wealth and the cause of wealth.
Therefore, the more criminals, the better for
the nation.
“ Cos. ” : More re pre-natal influence.
Woman of writer’s acquaintance had a
violent altercation with a Chow fish-hawker
re the payment of his little bill. Some
months later she gave birth to a boy who
bore unmistakeable likeness to a China-
man. Sister of same woman living on
a selection was so much worried by
Hindoo hawkers that three of her chil-
dren were dark enough to elicit nasty
remarks from the neighbors. This sort
of pre-natal influence is very common in
Australia, especially around the Kanaka
districts.

He music in neuralgia and nerve-depres-
sion. In Germany the music-cure is greatly in
vogue. They say a special hospital has been es-
tablished at Munich for the systematic application
of music m these disorders. It is held that the
vibrations of. the tympanum, setup by music, pro-
duce oscillatory movements in the nerves and so
on to the brain, which is soothed by this form of
massage or vibration treatment. Music and mass-
age are thus convertible terms, and Marshall-Hall
is only a professional masseur, after all. No
wonder he was drawn to a man of like calling—to
wit, Mr. Peters, the masseur—and no marvel that
he immortalises him in the following :
To Mr. Alfred Peters, Masseur.
Some say dead Lazarus was raised,
And others sny he shammed ;
But, Peters, when you brought me to
I was both dead and damned !
It has been found that the note C major produces
vibrations which neutralise the disordered vibra-
tions in the affected nerve more readily than any
other note. Hence tunes in which C major pre-
dominates have been recommended as most effi-
cacious in the treatment of neuralgia. It has
been hinted that the experiment should not be
tried on individuals having no ear for music, lest
an induced current of rapid percussions and vibra-
tions tuned to D major should eventuate and lay
low the well-meaning experimenter.
* * * •
The Mitre, organ of the Vic. High Angli-
cans, makes editorial allusion to the Marshall-Hall
controversy, its last words being :
One point seems to be plain. So far as the public at
large have stirred in the matter, they have been aroused,
not by the indecency, but by the profanity of the Pro-
fessor’s speech and verses. The charge of indecency is
one that could scarcely have been pressed, had it stood
alone. We are unable to discover in those “poems”
specially referred to sufficient ground for declaring their
writer unworthy of his high post. But it is not the first
time that the real charge against a man is not that for
which he is condemned. Neither the Argus nor the
University Council can proceed against the Professor for
atheism. It is undesirable that they should. Yet we
recognise that forbearance has its limits, although they
are difficult to determine. The atheist who becomes
blatant and generally offensive must be checked somehow
or other. And that is what has happened in this in-
stance.
Marshall-Hall’s poems are not indecent. But they
are atheistical. The authorities could not con-
demn him for atheism. Therefore they did right
to condemn him for indecency, of which he* was
not guilty, and to say he was indecent though the
statement was not true. This is the verdict of a
religious paper.

One of the many curious pieces of country
scenery which the Agricultural show brought to
Melbourne this year attracted much attention in
Collins-street. It consisted of two very round-
shouldered sons of the plough and furrow, with
earth-soiled moleskins, shabby tweed “ slop ”
coats, slouch hats, and the very latest style of
crook, willy-hoy walking sticks !

LOVE’S LOST HEAVEN.
Why so you seek me with those awful eyes,
In search of Love that withered in its birth?
You cannot in your Hell claim Paradise,
Nor seek for smiles where smiles are all a dearth.
I loved you onoe, but now that love is dead—
Night fills my heart where late devotion shone;
Flee from my sight with bowed and shaking head :
I tell you all is over—Love has gone!
HUGE R. M'CRAR.

* * * *
The other morning a Q. Govt, employd of
20 years’ standing was entering his office when he
took a fit and died. The day’s pay was refused to
his necessitous widow on the ground that deceased
had not signed the attendance-book !
• * * *
At Gladstone Creek, N.Q., recently, a
woman was found with a broken jaw, a crushed
nose, and other injuries, under circumstances that
pointed to outrage and attempted murder. For-
tunately, however, for some man or men in the
vicinity, she recovered sufficiently to explain that
while driving some horses away from a tub of
water, she was kicked in the face. As the horses
were quiet draught animals, nobody would have
suspected the truth but for the victim’s own evi-
dence, and another “ horrible male outrage ”
would have been trumpeted from one end of Aus-
tralia to the other. As it was, “a posse of
police ” went post-haste to the spot.
* * * *
“ The destitute sick of Sydney who apply
for Govt, medical aid,” remarks a Bulletin cor-
respondent, “have a hard time before they get it.
The waiting accommodation for the men is a
vault below the Board of Health’s handsome
structure, and there patients in all stages of suffer-
ing have sometimes to wait shivering for hours
before they are disposed of.”
'* » * *
William * Scott, ex-lunatic, at Mackay
(N.Q.) t’other day, told a butcher’s-shop crowd
that he was going to cut his throat. Couldn’t
appropriate a butcher’s knife, so went off, re-
turned with a razor, and almost cut off his head to
a full house. It is commonly said that the man
who is going to suicide never tells anyone that he
intends doing it. Wrong, in the writer’s experi-
ence. In every one of the cases of suicide per-
sonally known to this scribe, the felo-de-sc-cr
always told of his intentions beforehand ; and, in
many of the suicides of which writer has read, the
preliminary tattle was a prominent feature.
Seems that a dramatic prologue is a fad of suicide-
maniacs, as well as an emotional epilogue in the
form of a letter to the coroner, of which letter
egotism and histrionic display are the principal
features.

A Croydon district publican successfully
cyanided himself, on a recent Sunday. Mark
Bung’s lethal draught and his chosen day. On

exploding a plug of gelignite, laid on his tongue.
And he had a second capped plug handy, in case
the first missed its mission.

jrjils in Melb. is to work crosses, altar-cloths, &c.,
for Catholic and other tabernacles. Jewish tolera-
tion of Christianity is rather rough on the True
Faith, and it makes a Believer feel small.
Melb. police have suddenly awakened to
the fact that the sojourner in the local Botanical
Gardens in search of Sabbath calm is frequently
disturbed by sounds of gory combat arid the yet ®
of frenzied barrackers. Two offending yputh >
who had probably quarrelled over the scientin
name of some shrub, were recently hauled before
the Court, whose verdict was <£3 or 14 days.
The P.M. also remarked that their conduct was
disgraceful, as the place was set apart for reerea-
tion. And yet the two battered louts and then
backers thought they were having recreation.
One half of the world can’t recreate itself without
knocking the other half’s nose endways.

* * * *
The parson’s watchful eye ! “I stood out-
side one of the biggest hotels in Brisbane on Sun-
day last,” groans the Rev. D. Frank el lon ; I saw
beer taken out and placed at a corner ; I next saw
a policeman go to the corner and then come back
wiping his mouth.” The evidence is not complete.
A Sydney publican not long ago having a giudge
against a, bobby placed an empty pewter on the
outside window-sill on Sunday afternoon as a
slight amusement for onlooking friends. Bob iy,
believing it was meant in good part as of old,
surreptitiously grabbed the vessel, and a second
later, like him of Brisbane, went off wiping 11s
mouth. But the expert crowd knew that his
mouth watered because there was no beer where
the beer should be. Rev. Frackelton cries aloud :
“You can hang a man on circumstantial evidence ;
am I not justified in saying that policeman drank
that beer?” No.

Rochester, about the tiredest of Vic. up-
country towns, has been running its legs off lately.
The earthquake that struck it and roused it to
this activity was an order from a certain steamship
co. for 2000 “ old hens age not more particularly
specified. Spring chicken isn’t going to be off
on that line for some time to come.

a’Beckett is a prominent

The First Australian Horse bids fair to be
as big a curse to the N.S.W. country M.P. as the
local wants in the way of roads and bridges: In
almost every one-horse township warlike residents
are holding meetings, and demanding to know
why the local member has not arranged for a
corps of the “ real Mackays ” to be formed
there. This has apparently impressed said local
member, as 25 of him rolled up in a deputation to
War Minister Brunker the other day, with the
result that “Old Sunday ” has promised, if Reid
agrees, to increase the strength of the regiment
1 o 1000.

The folly of fighting for heavy damages
after defendant has offered a reasonable com pro-
mise, was instanced in the action of Mrs. VVu-
liams widow of Dr. Williams, against a Melbourne
chem’ist. Dr. Williams, a notorious morphia-
maniac and cocainist, was supplied by the chem-
ist’s assistant with atropine in mistake for morphia,
The negligence was admitted. Dr. Williams'
injected a dose of one of the drugs into his arm
and died—from atropine poisoning, as his widow
alleged The chemist offered her =£4oo in settle-
ment of the claim. She preferred to go for £3OOO
damages. The medical evidence was not clear as
to the cause of the drug-soddened doctor’s death,
the symptoms being consistent with cocaine poison-
ing The jury found for defendant with costs,
and the comely lady, who had eyed the jury with
fascinating expectation as she sat in court, went
sadly home to think about it.

It is now possible to communicate by tele-
phone from one end of Europe to the other. So
far however, distant telephonic communication is
quite in its infancy throughout Australia.

“ C.R.” : Was in the bar of a big Melb.
hotel two or three weeks ago when three gentle-
men and two ladies walked down the passage and
out into Bourke-street. One of the gentlemen
was a straw-hatted, well-setup* youngish man who
looked as if he washed himself regularly with soap
at sixpence a cake. “Do yon know who that is,?
said the barman to me. I shook, my head.
“That’s the Duke of Manchester—don’t look much
like a duke, does he?” I replied that my
acquaintance with dukes was not sufficiently ex-
tensive to enable me to spot one at sight. Just
then a magnificent figure clad in a dark uniform
marched up the passage in the direction of
the stairs. “Who is that noble-looking poten-
tate?” I enquired. “Ishe an emperor in dis-
guise’” The barman answered with a chuckle,
“ That’s just what he is, and what we call him,
too. And you should just see him spread his tail
when we tell him that he walks like a prince.
He’s the hall-porter ! ” ;

THE OLD MAID’S LAMENT.
Who calls me fairest of the fair?
Who cleans my bike with neatest care?
And in my bloomers mends the tear?
Who tucks me into bed at night?
And at my call blows out the light ?
Then does the round to see all’s right?
Who brings me, at the break of day,
My tea and toast upon a tray,
Delighted by my side to stay ?
Who from the depths of passion deep
Will summon tear-drops if I weep?
Who treads on tiptoe when I sleep?
Who cleans my shoes with polish rare ?
And brings to me an easy chair ?
Then lays my slippers ready there?
Who brings my bike, and when I start,
Will beg a kiss, or, ere we part,
Who hugs me tightly to his heart ?
Who? Nobody!
E.J.W.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 13, IS9B.
Mr Dear Myee—
This brief, bright week’s glitter began in earnest
at Randwick on Saturday. The races that run in
the spring bloom many tones brighter than races
at any other time of year, and this year every blue
is turned turquoise, and every green has rubbed
against a pistachio-nut, and the pink that couldn’t
go red at a moment’s notice is out of the world
entirely. To put the fashions briefly, as fore-
shadowed by Race modes, muslin lawn-cleaners in
every hue, lifted occasionally to show fiery silk
petticoats, lead by yards of draggled tail. These
are topped with hats that could lose themselves in
the heavens of Holman Hunt, or with earthier
creations of rosy straw and ruddy feathers. Cream
and dust-colored voiles made over silk, and chased
with true-lovers’ knots in narrow ribbon and nar-
rower lace, or steel sequins, follow at a brief inter-
val and every woman who didn’t wear one of
these on Saturday wore the other.
With the exception of some twelve or twenty.
A replica of Lady Rupert Clarke couldn’t be ex-
cepted. She was like nothing outside a fairy-tale
A princess robe of declamatory green fitted like a
skin as far as her knees. There it ended, briefly
alarmingly. And a skimpy white skirt took up
the parable, and insinuated that an accident
had happened. From what the skirt said, I
inferred that the gown came from Paris and
the remainder of it had got lost on the’way.
Bulrushes m cream ribbon waved up and
down what there was of this dress, and two
huge pearl earrings were kept in countenance
by ropes of pearls round the neck. A pink
hat and peach-colored feathers were the only indi-
cations that it wasn’t the frock of a mermaid out
on a holiday.
By the way, Mrs. Prendergast’s ball the night,
before wasn’t altogether kind to the pink hat
people. IS o ! A peach-hat was never meant for
the day after a ball. The blush that isn’t cast
into sallowness by a glow overhead must have
its pink-and-white decisively defined. In fact,
black hair and colorless skin are the only legiti-
mate adjuncts for rosy head-gear.
. Another peach-blossom was Mrs. W. B. Mitchell
in rose brocade, with an apron skirt and a black
chiffon sash. The billows of pink that waved
from her hand were more like a fallen sunlit cloud
than an earthy sunshade. The fat parasol has
driven out the thin one this year.
The Melbourne bride was in a simple muslin,
-^* SS Madden wore a conspicuous green
gla.ce frilled to the knees, with a shaped train like
a lizard’s tail, and the usual embroidered design.
Cupid, who doesn’t usually trouble much
about this sort of thing, is having a big
finger in frocks this year. The trail of the
true-lover s-knot is . embroidered, or stamped
or ruched, or braided on to every gown
worth owing for. The worst of it is these things
don t last. Love in society will be as dead next
month as the flowers of last year’s spring—sartori-
alfy speaking, of course.
,! n the vice-regal box, the prettiest dress was
Miss Dolly Brand’s cream voile, its.revers outlined
with ruchings of bright cherry ribbon. Lady
Hampden and Lady Gormanston were well dressed
m a way you wouldn’t notice, and the aide in a
red and white striped shirt is going to have an in-
fluence on male modes shortly.
I presume “Trelawny of the Wells” inspired
the innumerable ’6O skirts and “gardener’s-
daughter ” hats, which latter, I may say, shouldn’t
be encouraged by anyone whose profile won’t bear
silhouetting. The woman whose nose was lost
when she turned her head side-face, ought to have
known better,
I heard ghastly rumors of broken trysts on
Saturday, 1’ ive balls and four race-ineetings taxed
Sydney milliners beyond their possibilities. It
iuVi'n H la * suburbs didn’t get their race-frocks
till 10 that night. All dresses over seven guineas
were delii vered in time, but under seven the heart-
burning began.
At night, the froth of the course found its way
to La Poupee” ; long before the curtain lifted
every step was taken, and a double row of standers
encircled the back of the d.c. “La Poupee ” '
Let me say, quickly, that Dolly Vane is the doll
°F a b aby s dream and a grown-up’s most capri-
cious fancy, all in one. But why isn’t the doll
more like Dolly ? The doll that was.really a doll
didn t deceive anybody, but the house gazed for
several minutes at Dolly Vane before her flesh and
blood asserted itself. And now I understand
those jerky movements of hers in other
operas. She used to sit down with a snap
and get up with a click, and had a habit of making
her eyes stare vacantly. These tricks are La
loupees. Ido hope there will be no “sympa-
thetic imitation of Miss Doll’s queer ways. Just
fancy if society took to staring vacantly—at you
among other people. Hence, horrible thought!
The frocks are radiant harmonies that melt
without mischief into each other’s tints. I don’t
say that this is the best-dressed opera ever pre-
sented, but lots of lovely clothes walk in and out
particularly in the wedding-scene. Dolly Vane’s
doll’s-frock is a truly toy-like conception. Every
woman was aged six once more as she gazed at the
green silk petticoat, embroidered in stiff little up-
running sprays of flowers, with a pink-draped
over-skirt, and a white hat with pink and white
feathers (ten-and-six at the Civet Cat, I remem-
ber, and sixpence extra for the box).
• Ugo Biondi, at the Tivoli, is the latest craze.
A short, sleek, black-haired man, with a straight
nose and nonchalant manner, he comes out in his
little black satin trousers, looks all round the
house, says he can’t speak English, appears to
search hard for a word, and winds up with a care-
tul enunciation of “ awfully obliged. ” That
brings down the house before he has done a thing,
-then the little games begin. His play “ Dis-
covered is acted by a husband, a wife, a lover
and an old servant. You could about swear they
are all on the stage together. The lover gets
under the table, the husband walks in through the
door, the wife screams out in the other room, the
old servant enters drowsily to discover what the
noise is, and a wild confusion follows, which it is
almost impossible to believe is created by one
man, Ugo Biondi. J

I he course of true love, or otherwise, runs no
smoother now than heretofore. One hears of a
Jacob of to-day who waited for his Rachael seven
years His bride-elect’s feverish headache on the
eve of the wedding he mentally put down to ex-
citement, but when the mother was fastening up her
child s white frock on the morning of the marriage
the measles were out on the poor girl’s shoulders T ’
Then there s that local heroine, who always
swore she wouldn’t be married in May because a
girl friend, who had been rash enough to do so was
tossed by a cow on the first day of the honeymoon
got her nose all skinned and the sweetest hat in
her trousseau torn to smithereens.
~ “Accordingly,' our superstitious friend named
the day in April. The happy man travelled hun-
dreds and hundreds of miles to find his Precious
laid low with influenza. All April she was more
or less ill, and he couldn’t stay away from his
business all May, waiting for auspicious June, so
the y just had to take the risks of the ominous
month and eat their wedding-cake with cold chills
running down their backs.
