GOULI-GOULI.
Ifc is an open secret that the N.S.W.
Govt, party relies on beating Barton for the
blasting’s and Macleay seat, in N.S.W. Assembly
by raising the miserable sectarian cry. What
earthly connection there can reasonably be be-
tween the loathsome squabbles of long ago and
the great question of a United Australian nation
it is hard to say. But certain it is that the
Yellow Pup is now being worked for all it is
worth in the hope that thereby a political cypher
like ojdney Smith may be the means of exclud-
ing from the House the man who, above all
others, should be there at the present momentous
time.
The daring and once-beautiful Empress of
Austria, stabbed to the heart with a file, last
week, by an Italian anarchist, was a sister of
Bavaria s mad King Ludwig. Her last years
were embittered by the tragic death of her son
who got mixed up with the inevitable woman’;
also, she had found out, in the course of along
experience, that in her husband Francis Joseph
there was more of the Francis than of the Joseph.
Present writer, as a child, well remembers the
assassinated Empress hunting with the hounds in
the Lnglish Midlands, in the late 70’s. The Em-
press rented Cotteshrooke Hall, a house belonging
to a country squire, who has since attained
notoriety in the London courts. She was a mag-
nificent rider, and a grand judge of a horse, but
her hunters never looked smart. They were
selected rather for strength than for style. She
introduced a new feature into the hunting-field—
that of carrying a fan as well as a whip. The
i ytchley horse-women used to smile at it, and
the fan newer became fashionable for Dianas
though an Empress used it. The sight of a tall
handsome woman vigorously fanning herself while
m full flight across a ploughed field after a yelping
pack sticks in the memory.
The eternal feminine looms large in M L
politics at present. The country is convulsed over
a demand for the admission of women reporters
to the Parliamentary Press Gallery. By a majority
of over two to one the mere men who now occupy
the gallery have resolved to beg the Speaker to
keep the fair ones to the Ladies’ Gallery, where
our of their number have for some time been at
work as recording angels.” Reviewing the
situation, a Liberal bard chortles :
Ungallant scribes ! who will not squeeze
Four girls in, more or less,
Denying thus—excuse the wheeze—
The freedom of the press!
And, in reply, an Opposition minstrel snortles:—
Rats ! Let those Peris at the gate
Of Heaven stand, disconsolate—
They shan’t invade our gallery 1
Think of the freedom we should lose
Cerulean yarn, and smoke and booze—
(And something from our salary !)

Worried to Death.
Not long since, a business man of this city died
from worry. The strain on his brain and constant
anxiety brought on numerous complaints, which
ultimately led to his death. Nor is an occurrence
of this kind so uncommon as people suppose.
Business men are very often martyrs to constipa-
tion and kindred ills, and, though an absolute
and speedy remedy is to be found in Bile Beans
for Liliousness, every one does not appear to
recognise that fact. What is the direct cause of
constipation is hard to say. It is mostly the
effect of disobedience to the laws of nature,
irregularity of habit, and is often caused by an
overstrain on various organs of the system.
Constipation may also be caused by eating indi-
gestible foods, or eating too much or too fast.
Insufficient liver action, also, is often the cause,
and the reason for this is that it is one of the
duties of the liver to furnish for purposes of diges-
tion a fluid called bile. An insufficient quantity
of this fluid interferes with the digestion, and
constipation is among the results. In curing con-
stipation two things are necessary—first, to do
away with the existing trouble, and then to pre-
vent further occurrences of the same ailment from
the same cause. The first may be effectually
bi ought about by taking Bile Beans as directed.
After that, strict regularity must be observed.
Don t let business worries interfere with regularity,
as it is an extremely dangerous practice.*

A swagman admitted recently to Bendigo
Hospital handed the matron 2s. 4d. as all the
money he had in the world. He died two days
afterwards, and .£897 in securities was found in his
possession.

Another “oldest inhabitant” has gone
over Mrs. Brid ret Blake, at Moree. She
arrived in N.S.W. in 1820, and had a clear
remembrance of “ old Macquarie.” She lived at
Singleton for 66 years, and was 96 at her death.

evangelist preacher in Scotland be-

“Jack” Want and Donaldson, the new
member for Tumut (N.S. W.), were discussing a
certain old Tumut elector, a champion at rounding
up voters to the poll. “What has become of
him?” asked King Cabbage - tree. “Dead,”
answered Donaldson. “ Poor old chap ! ” said
Jack, sorrowfully. “ We’ll never see his like
again. On Judgment-day, if they put a Heavenly
electors’-roll in his hand, and let him loose in that
cemetery, the angel Gabriel will be a fool to him.”
John A. Wallace, mining millionaire, de-
fendant in the Melb. family money dispute, was
30 years ago known in Vic. N.E. district as “ Six
Stars Wallace,” being proprietor of a “ Star
Hotel” in Beechworth, Rutherglen, Chiltern,
Yackatidandah, Bright, and Stanley. At latter
place he also ran a general store and butcher’s
shop, and would often give a newly-arrived digger
a complete mining rig-out on tick. Being a good
judge of human nature, too, he nearly always got
paid.
John Matthew Smith, an old-time Melb.
solicitor, recently deceased, left by will .£246,277,
on which probate-duty to the amount of £21,627
has just been paid. Tiie testator was the Tulking-
horne of Melbourne, much “quiet business ” pass-
ing through his hands.
The demise of Rev. Coles Child, who died
the other day at Petersham, Sydney, aged 81,
releases the State from an aid-to religion pension.
He was one of the C.E. band who, on the aboli-
tion of State aid to the churches, claimed and
drew a pension to t>e end. He was ordained by
Bishop Tyrell in 1849.

Coghlan to the Auditor-Generalship—the present
Auditor-General is nearly 80 years old —is, it is

Edward Flood, whose estate is now before
N.S.W. Equity Court, was, from a money stand-
point, the staunchest of the late Henry Parkes’
friends. But for an accidental omission to sign a
document, Parkes’ last years would have been
free from pecuniary trouble. Flood and Parkes,
though dissimilar in most things, reciprocated
each other’s goodwill.
Kitchener, “Sirdar” of the Soudan, has
had a younger brother in Melbourne for the last
18 months, and yet only a handful of the Naicest
knew it. He is a pallid youth, not in the least
ferocious-looking, but he nevertheless became en-
gaged to the daughter of one of the biggest soft-
goods houses in Flinders-lane, ere starting on a
trip to Ceylon the other day.

“ Melb.” : Judge Hartley Williams keeps
on Pleasant Sunday Afternooning with talk about
crimes, prisoners, gaols, floggings, solitaries, and
what not, and indeed there is a gruesome appetite
in your Sabbath Afternooner, who must either
enjoy his Antoinette Sterling or his Hartley
Bilious. If Williams would but head a crusade
in favor of jury-sentences he might be trenchant,
but this would mean a lessening of his own im-
portance.
Cap’n Hancock, for over a-third of a
century grand panjandrum of Moonta (S. A.) cop-
per mines, has handed ever the reins to his son,
who won’t find the work very fresh to him. The
old gentleman, who, for some years past, has
worried more over the jigging of silver-lead sul-
phides than over copper, retires in ill-health to the
S.A. Mountains —locally “the hills.’: A good,
old-fashioned Cornish mining man was Cap’n
Hancock, nightly at class-meetin’ and at prayer ;
wherefore during his long reign Methodism
flourished exceedingly at “Munta.”

Of the men sentenced in
N.S.W. for ructions in connec-
tion with the last shearers’
strike, only two now remain
inside gaol, a batch of five
having had substantial chips
taken off etheir sn fences during
the last few months. The still-
imprisoned pair—Bonnor and
Murphy—don’t seem to have
been any worse than the rest
of them ; and a petition is
coming along from the back
country asking for their libera-
tion, too. The grounds relied
upon are that the sentences
were passed at a time of great
public excitement, and that
since then the class to which
they belonged have by their
conduct shown that they now
look another way for the re-
dress of grievances.
Queensland Under-Secre-
tary for Mines Sellheim has
been hardened and weather-
beaten by years of rough work
on the first Q. goldfields. He
swore off white bread years
ago, and hasn’t touched a
crumb since. About the only
thing which has not become
acclimatised about P.F.S. is
bis German accent. He used
to call geologist' Skertehley
“ Shikertchley.” In the early
days of the Palmer, when .Sell-
heim was warden, he used
often to sleep on thousands of
ounces of gold for weeks,
awaiting the escort, but he
was known as a dead shot and
slept with one eye on the
dust. Once the escort-horses
badly needed shoeing, and
horse-nails were so scarce that
Sellheim could only get what
were needed by offering 2s. fid.
apiece for them. Some months
later there was not a storekeeper on the held
who would sell a pound of tea or flour unless the
purchasers’took also a pound of horse-nails !
Noteworthy that no Victorian Judge, dead
or alive, has any descendant at the Bar of that
province, while in N.S. W. the names of Stephen,
Wise, Manning, Windeyer, Innes, Foster, Owen,
and Simpson are much in evidence. In Victoria,

Court Bench, and County Court Judge Wrixon
left a son who is now too beastly rich to practise,
but no Vic. Jud' e has left a practising barrister
behind him.
Recently Cchled that R. Elliot Smith,
M.D., Ch.M., of Sydney, has been awarded a
valuable scholarship'by the British Medical As-
sociation for researches into diseased conditions of
the brain. Dr. Smith is a graduate of the Sydney
University—the first locally-trained man to pro-
ceed to the M.D. degree of Sydney. Most Sydney
medicals stop at M.B. He was always strong on
brain-conditions : gained the Sydney gold medal
for his thesis on the brain of the platypus, and has
since won kudos for similar theses in Ireland and
London. The scholarship for advanced research
which he held from Sydney University expired m
May last, so that the new success comes at a good
time for him.

One night as old Jack Robertson (then.
N.S.W. Premier) was riding home to CloveTly
after the Assembly had risen, a footpad under-
took to stick him up on Rose Bay road. Old Jack
knocked him over with his hunting-crop. Speak-
ing of the matter next day he said that “it
was hard upon the poor devil. If he had gone
through me,” said the old warrior, “he would
have got but half-a-sovereign for ms pains, and!
that wouldn’t have hurt me. I borrowed it from,
Tom Garrett just before I started for home.”
Messrs. W. B. and J. B. Dailey, sons of
the late “ Plain Bill ” Dailey, Q.C., have passed!
for the Bar in England and will be admitted when
they have eaten the prescribed dinners. They
have lately been on a visit to Sydney. Mr.
Dailey’s third son, Mr. C. B. Dailey, thinks of
entering the Imperial army. Mr. Charles Martin,,
a stalwart son of the late N.S.W. Chief Justice,
has also qualified for the English Bar and may
shortly return to Australia to, as he puts, it,,
“ personally conduct criminals to the gallows.”

Aboriginalities.
Waipin : Pig yarn, just as I heard it.
“ Yis, Barney is a great pig, God bless him. ’Tis
herself thinks a dale of him. Foive year ago I
caught him in the bush, a bit of a bonnuv as big
as me fisht. Herself was like a mother to him,
an’ he grew at a great rate, wid no ind of atin’
an’ wid tushks on him loike an elephant. Well,
two year back I was in town to the Cup, an’ a
lump of a shwagger comes up to the dure, an’ sees
herself alone. After some yarning, says he —‘ I
want a shakedown for the night.’ ‘Go else-
where,’ says herself; but the divil a move was an
him, an’ by and by in he shteps, not seein’ Barney
in front af the fire. ‘ Sool him, Barney,’ says her-
Belf, an’ Barney goes for him, an’ me blagyard
bolts up the laddher on to the sthack. An’ there
he had to shtop, wid Barney shepherding him,
till meself comes home. ‘ Come down ! ’ says I.
‘ls it for a woild boar to ait me?’ says he.
So up I goes an’ gives him the father ov a
batin . Thin, whin 1 let aff him, he looks around
haf-croyin’, an’, says he, ‘ I’m betune a woild
hairy-toothed mahn an’ his pig, an’, be jabers ! I’ll
chance the blanky pig.’ An’ he meks a lep, an’
aff wid Barney at his heels. There’s his ould
dhrum in the shed beyant, and here’s Barney to
welkim him back whin he comes for it.”

“ Leoro ” tells of “ a bad three minutes ” :
I was working at the Surprise mine, at Charters Towers,
and had just returned from a six months' spell, having
been down to Melbourne to get married. The first night
I got back I was over at the camp of a great pal of mine.
George Cooper, when a man came rushing up with a
white face and staring eyes to say there had been a
fall of stone in the Morning Star, and that help was
wanted as there were two men buried. George and I
jumped up to make for the mine, when the chap who had
brought the news said a 70ft. rope was wanted. George
said to me, “ There’s a rope in the 60ft. level of the Sur-
prise ; ” so we two set off as hard as we could lick for
the mine, which was about a quarter of a mile away.
When we reached it, as there were no ladders in, Jack
swung himself onto the rope and I lowered him, and, as
bad luck would have it, knocked the peg of the windlass
down the shaft. It didn't take long to get the rope on
top, and then I set to work to haul George up. He was
a big man, but I never doubted I could do now what
I had often done before. However, when I had
got him up about 30ft. I began to feel the strain
something awful. During the next ten feet all
the strength seemed to go out of me. I couldn’t
understand it at first, and then it flashed on me what was
. the matter—l had been away on a long spell without
doing a tap of work, and had got out of condition. By
THE BONE TRADE IN THE COUNTRY.
"’Elio, Go’anna, still ’ere? Whachyer waitin’ for with all the bags? ”
“ Ole Dwyer down at the store’s yivin’ a ha’penny a pound more for bones.
So I’m jest stoppin’ till ’is moke pegs out; he ain’t got
far ter go—and there’s a reg’lar gold-mine in ’im !”
Heaven, it gave me a turn when I saw what I waa in for
I got him up another few feet, slower and slower, cursing
and praying, and then could do no more, so just held on
and cried out in a regular agony “George, old man, I’m
done!" I'll never forget his voice as he shouted back,
“ For God’s sake, Jack, don’t 1< t me go 1” I tried again
with an awful fear upon me -the fear of killing a mate,
and got him up a few feet more. Then my head swam
and I knew I could haul no more, nor lower him either,
but must just let go and be wiped out by the windlass-
handle. I tried to shout for help, but couldn’t, and any-
way it would have been useless. Just as I was going to
let go, George kind of screamed out, “Don’t kill me, Jack!
You —— cur, stick to it! ” Then I heaved again with
my hands and arms on fire and my brain bursting, and I
don’t rightly remember anything more, but when I
dropped, after an eternity, and was falling down the
shaft, George was there to catch me. He gave me a hand
along the tunnel and left me sitting on the tip blubbering
hk e a kid while he went off with the rope to the Morning

“W.”: He cats, rabbits, and bush-liars.
In 8., 6/8/’9B, you say, “Oats are notorious
rabbit-killers in Britain, often taking’ to a wild
life in a warren in order to feed on the inhabitants
thereof.” Ditto in Australia and Maoriland.
Let me add that where rabbits are abundant the
cat becomes an epicure—kills young rabbits only
and eats nothing but the head, leaving the rest to
hawks, native-cats, and other gross feeders. As a
lad I lived in a slab-hut m a rabbit district. The
family cat when suckling kittens was a mighty
nocturnal hunter. When the kitten was old
enough to crawl, quail, rail, ground-lark, or young
rabbit was brought in every hour or two, and
always alive. Once, when puss was snoodling the
solitary survivor of a newly-dropped litter, I
brought home a nest of young rabbits just able to
run about. Thrown into her basket, they were
not killed, but adopted and suckled for several
weeks. I suspected that puss was reserving them
for nights of sport and instruction, but not so. In
due time kitten and bunnies ran about the floor
together, and the old cat brought home game as
usual: often a young rabbit as before, and the
strange thing is that none of the fosterlings were
ever killed in mistake. But when my father
heard of the phenomenon, he pronounced it a
the*lot unna *' thing,” and promptly squashed

De S. :At Broken Hill, the other day, a
young wallaby was thrown in with a cat and young
kittens. Cat took to the strange animal after
halt-an-hour s investigation, and suckled it with
her natural youngsters.

“ Flong” writes : I have been in the bush
all my life, and for last 18 months have been walk-
mg two miles to a neighbor’s place four nights in
the week. I always carry back a bottle of milk,
inn °, n dark nights, I have gone astrav from
100 to 4Qoyds.—sometimes to the right, ofte’ner to
the left—l have often found that the direction
defended upon which hand I carried the milk in.
If in the right, Igo to the right ; if in the left, I
go to the left. Even when I have a pound of
butter m the other hand, I am bound to go with
the hand that carries the milk—perhaps because
the milk is the heavier. [Suppose you were
carrying a bottle of whisky ?]

“ Nea ” : While travelling once came upon
a man in a tent who was very anxious for me to
stay to dinner. Said he would have a damper
cooked in a jiffy; pulled off his shirt, poured
some flour on it, and started to mix. Have
myself used a corner of a tent, and even a saddle-
cloth, when nothing better was to be had ; but
that shirt made me suddenly remember that I was
in a great hurry.

“ Y.A.”: Re queer
flocks. Returning to
Shanghai in a house-
boat from a shooting-
trip on Tahu Lake, met
one of the many annual
flocks of ducks, num-
bering several thou-
sands. The individual
ducklings are hatched
artificially, and start in
infancy for the city a
hundred miles away,
supporting themselves
en route on waterweeds
and tadpoles with an
occasional dole of
paddy, and camping on
the creek - banks by
night. The owner and
his family follow them
in a “sampan,” and
occasionally hurry them
up with long bamboos.
By the time the city is
reached they are plump
and ready for market,
at from 8 cents (2d.) a-
piece. The majority
are “corned,”and when
duly split up the back
' and squashed flat, re-
semble nothing so much
as a tennis-bat. Once
ventured to empty a
duck-gun into a detach-
ment, paid each
for dead and wounded,
and sickened my friends
with duck-meat for a
month.
One resalt of a phenomenally good season
in parts of “ Sturt’s desert,” which takes in a good
patch of far-western N.3.W., is such a growth of
the gorgeous Sturt pea as has not been known for
10 or 12 years. And, as Broken Hill is set in the
midst of one of the most favored patches, the
crowds of flower-gardeners have made some inter-
esting discoveries. Among the millions of the
common crimson and black blooms are rare white,
white and pink, pink and heliotrope, and other
distinct sorts, all, however, pretty much alike in
shape.
* * * .
“Old Timer”: John Horrocka’ death in
the Land of the Crocodile occurred thus : He had
punished his camel—a brute with an extra-ugly
temper -and shortly after, as Horrocks was draw-
ing a charge from his gun, the camel struck at
him with a forefoot, caught back the hammer, and
exploded the piece. The ramrod was shot through
Horrocks’jaws, pinning them together. Paralysis
set in, and he implored his men to shoot him.
Jemmy Green, his black boy, travelled night and
day to a station 150 miles away, and brought out
assistance. Horrocks was still alive, and able to
give some instructions, one being that the camel
should be shot on his grave. I had the story from
Jemmy Green himself, weeping bitterly as he told
it. Horrocks had taken Jemmy to England,
where he was presented to Queen Victoria.

They were talking of dust and dust-storms
Out West. “See that coat-of-arms up there?”
continued Simpson, pointing to the lion and
unicorn above the entrance to the post-office;
“moulded out of the dust left on the walls after
one storm. Same storm, I wanted to light my
pipe. Had a stick and bunch of Sturt pea in my
hand. Wind blowing, needed both hands. Stuck
the stick into a cloud of dust that stood bv, hung*
the dowers on it, lit my pipe. Station I worked
on, boss tried to run a garden. Never troubled
to plough. When he saw a storm coming, he’d
send me out to scatter seed over the paddock, and
then let the dust cover it. Tell you what. Saw
a man go out once in white suit. Dust-storm.
Suit chocolate-brown. Then rain. Dust so thick
not wash off. Hot sun followed, caked it on him.
Had to carve clothes off with picks and cold
chisels.

“ Face” : Recently saw a farmer, after
fire, thistles, and language had failed to shift his
jibbing plough-horse, fasten his teeth in the nag’s
top lip and pull back like a terrier. The horse
went with him all right and kept going. Then
the farmer spat out the lip and went back to the
plough-handles.

Ed. Bulletin.— Be light-haired blacks.
About 18 months ago three fair-haired, brown-
skinned aboriginals, two men and a woman, came
to Kanowna, W.A. The men were well-built
fellows, very different from the miserable local
niggers of the district; the woman, too, was a
fine specimen, and wore her long hair don© up in
a knob on the back of her neck. When asked
where they came from they pointed north-east.-
E.P.E,
* • * •
“ Tin-lip ” : Called in at a Q. selection the
other day for a drink, and saw the proprietor, who
told me, mournfully, that he had 28 children—
twins five times, triplets once. “Yes, sir,” he
said, pointing to his wife, drawing water at the
well, “she has em reg’lar onst a year, and some-
times a litter of ’em. ’Tis hard on a poor man.
Protection and black labor don’t seem to be doin’
much good to the colony; a man with a large
family don’t get a chance.”
* * • *
In Geraldton (Q.) district lately a black-
boy captured a fair-sized turkey, and, not wishing
to kill it, fastened it by the leg to a vine. In the
morning the bird had vanished, and in its place
was a carpet-snake with a yard or two of vine
down its throat. The wrath of the astonished
blaokboy was only appeased when he had des-
patched the snake. A post-mortem revealed the
turkey, which the blackboy afterwards cooked
and devoured.
» • • •
“C.H.R.”: The ingratitude of the com-
mon tramp is a distressful thing. Writer knows
a farmer (a hatter) to whom one day came a long,
lean, hungry-eyed swaggie looking for work. He
was put on, and for the first few days proved a
first-rate farm hand —docile, obliging, and even
respectful in his manner. But how he did feed !
A leg of mutton at one sitting was nothing to
him. However, the boss didn’t begrudge the
tucker so long as the man kept up his big
average of work. Soon a change came o’er the
scene. The tramp began to feel himself. He got
full of good mutton, and he began to swell; and
the more he swelled the flasher he became. He
reared up behind, and pawed out in front, and
curvetted around vvith arched neck and bulgy
eyes; and the more he pranced the less he
worked ; and one day he sat down on a log and
told the boss to wheel the blessed barrer himself;
and when the latter expostulated, the stall-fed
stranger threatened to “ pull the nose off of his
face.” Ultimately, the local bobby had to remove
him from the hut, where he was found reading
the Protestant Standard.

“ Mirracrinya ” on a phase of way-back
Queensland life :
Re the lately-passed Q. Aboriginal Protection Act. If
Meston were to trip out to the Wilson, Bulloo.and Cooper’s
Creek he would find some eye-openers. Downs, for
instance, which is better known by another name on
account of the number of mernonge.rs (men who habitu-
ally cohabit with black gins) that usually make their home
there. In nearly every instance the blacks’ camps at the
stations in the S.W. corner of Queensland are simply
huge brothels—owners, managers, and knockabout hands
all have their “ fancy ” gins, and woe betide the stranger
caught making love thereto. Meston will find that, if he
wants to improve the blacks’ morals out that w ay, it is
not the casual man who carries a gin about the country
that he will need to tackle—his trouble will be with the per-
manent hands on the cattle-stations, who look upon the gins
as their personal property. As for the half-castes, he won’t
be troubled much with them, for nearly every gin on giv-
ing birth to a half-caste turns it over to the old camp
gins to be killed. Sometimes, if there are whites about
when the half-caste is born, they prevent its destruction,
but, as a rule, nobody bothers. You see, it is a bit awk-
ward having half-caste children, with strong family like-
nesses, about a place. On Downs, there is a
large camp of blacks, but very few half-caste children,
yet every man on that station at one time lived openly
with the gins; and it is the same on other squat-
tages. Whether the Act lately passed will alter the con-
dition of the aboriginals much is a very open question.
It is perfectly certain that these big cattle-stations, with
many blacks on them, will not pay the nigs, any reason-
able wages. At present, they get no wages, with a few
exceptions ; and they are more-or-less fed by travellers.
On some holdings, the condition of the blacks is down*
right slavery. A resident of the district, camped close to
one station, saw a big stockman ride up to it jingling a
pair of handcuffs, and inquire if anything had been seen
of a strange blackfellovv, as one had run away from the
station he belonged to. He W'as told no, when, all at
once, he cried, “Why, there he is!” pointing to a six-
year-old piccaninny, playing under the verandah with the
overseer s children. Imagine bringing the darbies along
for such an infant! No wonder the station he belonged to
is considered an absolute slave-holding concern. Follow-
ing- is the only instance so far, out there, of the Aboriginal
Act being put in force. A white man from one station
took a 12-year-old half-caste lad out of the blacks’ campon
another station,with a view to better the boy’s condition
by clothing him properly and so on, but the No. 2 station-
people promptly sent a constable to bring the boy back
to the blacks’ camp. Man asked boy in constable’s pre-
sence if he wanted to return, and, on the boy replying in
the negative, defied the constable to take him away. Re-
sult : Summons tmder the Aboriginal Act, to compel sur-
render of the boy to barbarism again. Will Mr. Meston
inquire into this'/ The Act, in short, will be a dead
letter as regards squatters, though it will probably be en-
forced against any poor devil who has no influence behind
him.

“ Mirracrinya ” : Teller of blacks’-grave
yarn in Bulletin, 13/8/’9B, is a bit at sea, Facts :
Jack Kennedy got into camp late one night close
to Nocalunga Station, fossicked about in the dark
and saw a big heap of dry wood close to camp.
Used it for firewood, and in the morning found he
was camped amongst blackfellows’ graves. Blacks’
camp was handy, and when they caught sight of
him they came over and said, “ You all the same
wokkaratchie (crow)—sit down longa deadfellow.”
The name stuck, and Kennedy is still well known
in this district by it; but as for the blacks giving
him afterwards a wide berth, he is a great favorite
with them, and whenever he is camped about
here you will always find the nigs, yarning with
him. Don’t think these S.W. Queensland blacks
would care a rap about having the wood taken off
their dead brethren. They are more scared about
white people’s graves than their own. Instance :
A man fencing in the cemetery here borrowed a
nigger of mine to help him for a day, and when
the darkey came home he wolfed his supper in
such a way that I asked him if the man hadn’t
gi ven him anything to eat. “ Walya mine eat
him,” he answered, “that fellow bin put him on
deadfellow’s grave.” I found out afterwards that
the whitefellow had hung the tucker-bag on a
grave-fence, and the blackfellow considered the
tucker defiled. Must have meant it, too, for a
blackfellow is always ready for a gorge, hungry
or not.

“V.K.”: “Raspberry” appeals for a
yokeless collar for the working bullock. In parts
of Europe the bullock pulls with his head ; the
shoulders are absolutely free. A cushioned cres-
cent is the medium of leverage, and it seems to m<-
the most rational way of exploiting the animal's
strength, which lies mainly in the neck. Abe,
this arrangement is cheaper, lighter, and easier P
handle than the yoke.
“ BUSKIN"—A Sydney Street Sketch.
“Bauhinia”: lie bower-bird’s mimicry
powers. At a C.Q. survey-camp, once, was suffer-
ing from a short, hacking cough, and our camp-
keeper insisted one night that I had returned tc
camp during the day, as he had heard me cough-
ing. Next day and the day after same assertion
on C.K.’s part, and denial upon mine, and on
Saturday morning the C.K. declared “ he’d be
hanged if he’d stop by himself any longer, for the
camp was haunted.” Looked as if we were going
to lose our excellent culinary artist; but on Sun-
day morning, as we were lounging in bed an hour
or so later than usual, my cough started clear and
distinct from an adjacent clump of gidya. In
rushed cookie, blazing with excitement. “ There ;
what did I tell you ? Now tell me I’m mad! ”
Out we all bundled, and found the feathered
mimic in full cough, spluttering away fit to break
his little neck.

* * •
“ Dareel ” : The amal-
gamation of stations under
one managership has been
much practised by financial
cos. since the ’93 bank
troubles. Some eight years ago a
now-famous co. tried the plan in the
mirage section of N.S.W., and the
experiment proved too costly to be
repeated. Taking over one of several
hard-hit properties, they sent up a
manager to lick things into shape
ere clearing out the pioneer owners
of adjacent runs. The manager
started by sacking all old hands.
“ Know "the country ! Of course
they do. Know it well enough to
dodge me on hot days,” he said, when
someone doubted the wisdom of work-
ing with a new staff. With a South-
ern experience of swift-rising-and-
falling rivers, he under-estimated the
dangers of the lignum-country floods.
Said a bush-fire might reach him 40
miles back from the creeks, but for
flood-water to come along the rnts
they called “ warrambools ” —well,
he’d guarantee if it did come so far,
he could block it with one plough-
furrow. “ Fact is,” he told one of
the hands, “you old ’64-ers have
flood-water on the brain !’’ The
old hands bided their time, and
one morning when the manager had
ridden in towards the frontage and
struck a scalded plain where there
were usually only plain-turkeys, lie
saw wild swans, and, not much
alarmed at seeing water so far out
that side, hurried the stock back to-
wards the Murilla ridges—only to be
met en route by the flood spreading
out from the despised ruts ; and in
48 hours occurred possibly the heavi-
est single loss of sheep in Austral-
asia. When the big financial bugs
got on to : that run, two months
later, they saw thousands of skele-
tons piled against .fences, trees and
scrub—a fitting monument to the
folly of running big interests by ava-
rice and arrogance. * So overboard
went the amalgamation plan, and
with it the manager. How the
co. pai’tly squared the loss by buying
from the adjacent runs sheep at
nominal values and crediting the
bereft property with stock rating at
after-flood prices is a story written
in their ledgers, and in the hearts
of the poor devds who had hoped
that the results of their foresight
and experience might be credited to their over-
drafts.

Some men take a lot of killing. A miner
fell down 90ft. of winze at the Markey claim, Cas-
silis, the other day. It dees not usually need a
long winze to batter the life out of a miner, but
this one arrived at the bottom in fairly good con-
dition, ai d started to climb out again after a spell.
He got half way up, and slipped and went rattling
to the bottom once more. The battered digger
was discovered eventually and hauled out. This
happened on a Tuesday, and on the following
Sunday night the miner went to work as usiiaj.
But writer knew a faceman who was twice buried
in, once fell down a 150 ft. prospecting shaft, and
once into a bob-pit, and after all lived to be killed
by a cab-horse at Gcelona. Third? of the indignity
of being killed by a Geelong cab-horse !
* * * •
There was a fire in a marine-store shed in
Adelaide, the other day ; and among the other
wrecks wiped out was Fitzpatrick, a bottle-
gatherer—a poor beggar of whom Fortune had
made a cockshy for years. Well-educated, but
restless, Fitzpatrick became a “rusher,” and
finished up that career at Kimberley. He was
fairly lucky there ; but, coming back, thieves
cleaned him and his mate out, killed the latter,
and so battered Fitzpatrick that after the police
found him he was for 11 months in hospital.

Since 1842 there have
been 90 males and three fe-
males hanged at Melb. gaol,
and 38 males and three fe-
males are recorded as buried
within its walls. Prior to
1865 no record of such inter-
ments was kept, and as exe-
cuted persons were not in-
variably quicklimed in the
prison precincts in the early
days, exact statistics on the
subject do not exist.

An old Melb. reader of
The Bulletin, who has been
in the' habit of posting the
paper to friends in Edin-
burgh, protests vigorously
against the new parochial
postal law by which all
newspapers printed outside
of Victoria are books, subject
to the charge of Id. per 2oz.
when mailed from that “ loy-
al” province to the beloved
mother country. It seems a
monstrously unfederal thing
that the bulky Australasian
should be carried from Mel-
bourne to Great Britain for
3d., whereas to send a copy of The Bulletin by
the same post costs 4d. And if a Bulletin and
an Australasian be packed together the post-
office demands book rate on the parcel. The
“ beastly foreign ” element is reckoned to leaven
the whole lump.

The Yic. Government has decided to buy
Longstaff’s fine picture, “ A Bush Fire in Gipps-
land,” for the Melb. National Gallery. The
Govt., it seems, enquired of sundry representative
persons—Mayor M'Eacharn and others as to
whether the picture was really an “ all right ” pro-

bell-toppers.

For the first time for many years the re-
nowned English chess-player, Blackburne, has
failed in an important tourney to gain a place in
the prize-list. Originally a waiter at a-chess
resort in London, the game which attracted so
many customers seemed to Mr. B. so excessively
simple that he did not see why others should not
wait on him, and learning the moves he speedily
became a “master.” Especially a§ a blindfold
player he has stood at the head of English chess
for many years. > v-
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him.. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
How to Remain Young.
FEED THE HARD-WORKING ORGANS
OF YOUR BODY
WITH JIJST WHAT THEY REQUIRE.
Here are some important truths expressed by a
well-known professor of a New York Medical
College:—
“All Nature ever needs, or ever asks for, is a
little help. She becomes overworked—is it any
wonder sne needs help ? ”
“The heart beats night and day, and never
stops. The lungs are always working away like a
pair of bellows. The stomach is constantly oc-
cupied-; so are the kidneys and the liver. You
cannot give them a vacation, but you can help
them-immensely. Those great organs—the kid-
neys and liver—are willing to work, but they need
food, and you can give it to them.”
“ Ydu dan easily feed these organs, and you will
be. surprised how much better you will feel if you
do so. There is a discovery which is an actual
food for, the kidneys. It is known to scientists
and,-the world as Warner’s Safe Cure. By all
means try it. A little of this remarkable medicine
taken three times a day will produce a most happy
effect. Instead of being tired, fagged out, des-
pondent, and with little ambition, you will grow
bright, hopeful, and happy. Women, especially,
need a good friend of this kind, and I know of
scores of ladies who would not think of being
without this grand remedy to help them when
they specially need it.”*

MELBOURNIANA.


Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.
J. Love ; Quite probable that if a strong leader
stumped N.S.W. in the interests of Unification as against
Federation he would “return to Sydney” with a big
majority. But N.S.W. can’t unify by herself, and things
would be no further advanced. Unification suits the big
province which would boss the show, and it can be easily
converted. But start by converting the small ones
D.F.D. : Before condemning Marshall Hall for appear-
ing to cave-in, it were well to remember that it is not
impossible to count heads beforehand in Vic. University
Council debates and divisions, and that few professors of
■music in the world could afford to bear the stigma of
even an illegal dismissal on grounds some of which had
reference to immorality ... W.H.R. (Rushworth): (1)
Difficulties in the way. (2) Victoria has a bank-note cir-
culation of about £i,000,000, and if the Govt, chose to
replace this with State notes it could undoubtedly keep
that amount of legal tender paper afloat without any
trouble. But if it shoved (say) £3,000,000 of legal tender
paper on the country, the £l-note would be at a dis-
count. If you keep more legal-tender paper afloat than
the country requires it always depreciates Back

Cable news of the British-Egyptian victory
in the Soudan suggests that Sir Herbert Kitchener
might have captured the Khalifa but for giving
his army a two hours’ rest before entering
Omdurman. “ The Khalifa, with his harem,
escaped by another gate at the very moment that
the British w T ere entering the city.” Two hours
earlier they would at least have bagged the harem.
And that would have been “better than nothing
at all.”
Learn and be Wise. —Federation is in the air ;
so are the measles. We don’t wish to block Federation,
but can lay you on how to prevent your baby, your
brother, your sister, or your aunt, or even the old man,
from taking the measles. Just drop into M'LEAN
BEOS & RIGG’S Ironmongery establishments in Mel-
bourne or Sydney, buy a few tins or bottles of their
specially-prepared disinfectants, and your house is as
safe against the malady as the cause of Federation is in
the hands of the present Parliament. The reader in-
wardly resolves to buy, preferring to spend a few shillings
to having the measles. Wise man.*
A correspondent of the Outlook (Eng.) :
Whilst public opinion is still occupied with regard to
Piper Findlater, may I venture to state that he is not the
only soldier who has found in the bronze of his Victoria
Cross a medium for extracting gold? A well-known
gallant cavalry officer, who had obtained the decoration,
retired from the army and devoted himself to the study
of sewage. In the course of this interesting pursuit he
invented a fertiliser. Every bag containing this manure
was marked with the Cross, which he used as a trade-
mark. I believe it proved an excellent form of advertise-
ment !! 1

“Melb.'’: Indignation over trees being
cut down in the Exhibition Gardens, formerly
Carlton. The identity clings to familiar trees,
while the new generation quite rightfully sees no
beauty in them, for indeed a good old Australian
town-tree is as gnarly as an octogenarian swagster.
Not till the typical O’Callaghan had followed
O’Brallaghan and Shanaghan to the sinnnetry did
Archbishop Carr manage to extract the hideous
dead gum-tree alongside St. Francis’s, Melb. The
wicked vandalism that robbed the historic One-
Tree Hill, Bendigo, is another matter.
HEALTH IN THE BOTTLE.
“D.C.L.” Scotch is a delicious and finely-
blended whishy of excellent flavour, which, while
maintaining all the due stimulating properties of
the spirit has been mellowoed by age into a per-
fectly wholesome and mild beverage.*
The clocks of Ayrshire Downs (Q.) station
went astray, and the men couldn’t tell the time of
day, except by averaging the time of all the
watches, each of which worked at a different rate.
The manager started regulating the working
hours by the sun. The next man swore he'd fix
things. He boiled the watches of six shearers,
and then drenched them with kerosene. Ayrshire
Downs still works “ by the sun.”

REMARKABLE CASE, AS PUBLISHED IN
THE ADELAIDE “ADVERTISER.”
Many wonderful cures have been placed to the
credit of Bile Beans for Biliousness, and we have
now to chronicle the marvellous case of an old
lady of eighty summers, who has suffered the most
excruciating agony for the past forty years, and has
been discharged from a hospital as incurable. Mrs.
Bridget M'Arney, the lady in question, resides at
Gibson-street, Bowden, Adelaide, where a re-
porter of the Adelaide Advertiser interviewed
her a short time since. Mrs. M'Arney is a
typical daughter of the “Emerald Isle.” She has
lived at her present address for nearly nine years,
and in the district for seventeen years, and is very
widely known. “Tell you the story of my ill-
ness ? ” she said, in response to a preliminary ques-
tion. “ Certainly I will, with pleasure'.
“About forty years ago I became very ill. I
suffered from severe pains in the head, chest, and
stomach, and used to have bad fits of retching,
and always, on getting up in the morning, had a
bad taste in my mouth. I could not enjoy my
food, and at last became so bad that I had to call
in a doctor. He prescribed for me, and I con-
tinued taking his medicine, but it did me no
good. I then called in another doctor, who told
me that I was suffering from a disordered
liver, and advised me a change of climate as the
only remedy. I tried other medicines, which did

yards. The doctors also told my daughter
that I was not to be left alone, as I might be
called away from this world at any moment.”
“ To look at you now, Mrs. M‘Arney,” said the
reporter, “ no one would think you had suffered
a pain in your life ! ” “ That is just how I feel,”
replied the old lady, cheerfully, “ and it is all
through Rile Beans.” “What induced you to
try Bile Beans, after the best of doctors had
failed ? ” “ Well, I will tell you. I saw a report
in the paper of a person who had been cured by
them, and resolved to try them myself. I ob-
tained a box, and, in about a week, .my health
began to improve. I continued taking them
until I had finished the first box, and then ob-
tained two more, and, before I had finished the
third, all pain had left me, my appetite had re-
turned, and I was able to sleep well, and alto-
gether felt in splendid health.” “Did you take
any other kind of medicine during that time,
Mrs. M‘Arney?” “I took nothing whatever
but the Bile Beans, and that is why I
know they alone cured me.” “I must say
you look well enough now,” said the reporter.
" Yes, and I feel well, too 1 ” observed the old
lady, “ and, instead of dying, as I was expected to
do, I can now walk into Adelaide, a distance of
about two miles—and, after a short rest, return

Indigestion.
Indigestion is an ailment which may develop
into a most severe disease if not properly attended
to. Acute chronic indigestion is by no means a
slight affair. Attacksof indigestion in a more orless
mild form are too common to dwell upon for any
length of time. As its name implies, this disorder
is an insufficient action of the various digestive
organs, which prevents the food being properly
dissolved. In cases of this kind the effect is two-
fold : First, the waste part of the food is kept
in the stomach and becomes sour instead of
being carried off; and, secondly, the blood making,
or useful part of the food, is kept from perform-
ing its offices. The causes of indigestion are
many : Insufficient liver action, over-eating, eating
too fast, and partaking of rich and indigestible
foods, etc., etc. Constant worry or mental strain,
or lack of regularity, may also cause an attack.
The first thing necessary to cure an indisposition
of this sort is a thorough opening of the bowels
and a complete clearing out of the whole digestive
organism; then the liver should be stimulated to
action, and regularity observed in everything.
Bile Beans will do the first two of these, the
patient must attend to the third. Chronic indi-
gestion of course needs longer treatment than a
temporary attack. It is well to bear in mind
that headaches, face pimples, listlessness, and a
host of minor ailments arise from indigestion or
some other form of digestive disorder.
+
Piles.
Anyone who has ever suffered from Piles knows
well what a painful and troublesome ailment they
are. There are two kinds of Piles the Blind
and the Bleeding Piles. The former is the most
common, and also more painful. The complaint
is much more common among women than men,
and is often caused by constipation. Inflammation
is generally the first symptom, and costive-
ness always accompanies the ailment. Piles are
composed of small particles of various sizes, form-
ing singly or in bunches, and extending upwards
into the bowels. They cause excruciating pain,
and cause the sufferer to be in a constant state of
misery. The Bleeding Piles are caused by a
swelling of the hemorrhoid veins which compose
the tissue wall of the bowels. While not so pain-
ful as Blind Piles, they are more dangerous, as a
great loss of blood may result. A false delicacy
exists in mentioning this disease, which causes
much trouble. . In all cases of Piles a remedy
should be applied at once, as otherwise violent in-
flammation may ensue, and serious results follow.
Bile Beans cause the bowels to act freely, thus
softening the walls thereof and affording instant
relief. _ No remedy will act so quickly or effect a
cure with so little pain in cases of this kind as
Bile Beans.
Influenza and Colds.
Influenza is not generally understood by the
greater part of those who suffer from it. It is in
reality an aggravated form of cold, supplemented
by a general weakness of the whole system. Ordi-
nary colds, it is well-known, are caused by the
congestion of the blood, which, as a rule, is most
prominent in the head or throat. Influenza can be
traced to the same cause, and irregular or insuf-
ficient liver action is, in most cases, responsible for
both. When the liver, through any derangement
of the digestive organism, ceases to act in the
proper manner, a certain amount of bile finds its
way into the blood, and, at the same time, the
blood created from food consumed is of a de-
cidedly poor quality. In this manner, the blood
becomes congested, the circulation sluggish, and
a cold results. The circulation of the blood now
being much slower than usual, the entire system
is affected. The nervous organism becomes un-
strung, the appetite poor, and the rest disturbed.
The body is extremely susceptible to chills, and the
slightest draught causes a violent attack of cough-
ing or sneezing. Many so-called cures for influ-
enza fail to get at the root of the trouble, and only
afford temporary relief. A medicine that will
keep the liver in thorough and regular working
order, and the digestion right, will, without
doubt, prove the most effective preventive and
cure for influenza and colds. This is what Bile
BeanB for Biliousness will do, and we maintain
that no better remedy can be found for these
ailments.


POLITICAL
POINTS
Premier Kingston (S.A.) makes no s.ecret
of his belief that Australian Federation will soon
be an accomplished fact.
Joey Bell, the cultyah’d but notover-gilded
Q. “coming” politician, has just received a solid
Govt, brief in a big land case. Tomjoe is looking
after the election-expenses of the faithful.
N.S.W. National Ass is dead. It died
of the rift in its ranks caused by the Federation
controversy. Its secretary, the energetic Epps,
now directs the operations of Sydney safe-deposit.
Q. Labor-leader Glassey’s speech, announc-
ing his election as leader of the constitutional
Opposition, was the most effective he ever de-
livered. It occupied just three minutes. Good
omen.
Q. Assembly asked Premier Byrnes if he
would explain the estimates-item of Governor’s
“contingencies.” Byrnes: “No!” Why should
a hired, servant’s contingent expenses, to be paid
for by his paymasters, not be given in detail?
Copeland, M.P., in N.S.W. Assembly, the
other night, scored off Premier Reid with an
assertion that the Gladly Adipose One was only
leading the House in the sense that a fox led a
pack of hounds, which were in full cry after him.
Premier Braddon’s announcement that he
will not appoint a regular Agent-General for Tas-
mania pending the present efforts to achieve
Federation is the first real assurance given by any
Premier of his belief in the nearness of Federal
union.
Alfred Deakin in his middle age strives
hard to restrain his natural rapidity of utterance,
whereas the young A.N.A. politicians who get
into Vic. Parliament all try to imitate Deakin’s
fault. Hume Cook, Watt, and Salmon are
instances.
Q. Barrister Edwin Lilley, who has his
share of contumaciousness, is determined to con-
test Oxley electorate, thus making it a three-
cornered affair and a certainty for sitting-member
Grimes—the dreary M.L.A.' who, at an agricul-
tural banquet, gave “God Save” as a solo.
It is amusing. The Czar proposes a gene-
ral European disarmament. French newspapers
declare that the thing is impossible as long as
Great Britain controls Egypt. The German Em-
peror congratulates General Kitchener on his con-
quest of the Soudan. And the British Lion wags
his tail.

Consider what a two-edged compliment is
the playing of “God Save the Queen” at the
Governor’s appearance in Parliament. It looks as
though the band cast its cold, critical eye over
H.M.’s agent, and then, in hopeless despair of
human instruments, called on the Deity to succor
so badly-represented a sovereign.
John Haynes’ Bill for the Abolition of
Capital Punishment in N.S.W. was unlucky in
being called on a few hours after the George-street
tragedy. Members ref used to have anything to do
with it until Copeland suggested that the words
“in certain cases ” be inserted, which Haynes
accepted, and the first reading was agreed to.
Pitiful the aids which institutions for the
benefit of organised labor have to invoke. The
generally-excellent weekly organ of the S.A.
party has to promote its circulation by means of
a bicycle offer; and the Broken Hill Trades Hall
is getting its roof put on with the takings at a
series of tugs-of-war and comic-singing competi-
tions.
Big political changes loom in England.
Hardly was Gladstone cold in his grave than there
were rumors that Home Rule was to be dropped
by the Liberals. And now they forecast a Whig-
Unionist party under Rosebery and Chamberlain,
and possibly Asquith. Significant, too, that
direct Radical candidates are now being run
against Liberals and Tories at bye-elections.
The objection to mutilation as a punish-
ment is the same as that to indiscriminate hanging
—juries will refuse to convict obviously guilty men
on account of the disproportionate consequences
of conviction. Someone in S. A. proposed a similar
penalty a few years ago, but it was hurried as
unceremoniously out of the House as Bailes’s pro-
position will probably be out of Vic. Assembly.

Premier Reid boasts that all the motions
of censure (about four in four years) brought
against him have been “ ignominiously defeated.”
Well, he brought 13 motions of censure against
Dibbs in three years, and every one of them
was “ ignominiously defeated.” In those days the
reison why Dibbs couldn’t get on with public
business was that he had to stop at least once
every month and wipe up the floor with Reid.
S.A. Premier Kingston brought in an
Early Closing Bill as an Eight-Hour Day present
to unions. Kingston proposes that his Bill, if it
passes (and it ought to about scramble through),
shall be immediately operative in the metropolitan
district, and (for some inexplicable reason) optional
in the country. The idea for city and suburbs is
that shops shall close at 6 o’clock on four nights of
the week, at 9 o’clock on Fridays, and at 1 o’clock
on Saturdays.
The coldness between Premier Kingston
and his old Labor friends, which began with the
Federation question, has apparently toned down,
as the Premier had a great reception at the recent
Adelaide Labor Conference. Awhile ago the
Laborites threatened to oppose him in his W.
Adelaide constituency, a decision which, if per-
sisted in, would have hopelessly divided the
Democratic party. And, with Federation still
unsettled, there may be fresh trouble yet.
A Perth paper makes courteous remarks
about Premier Forrest:—
FORREST the FINANCIER has had seven years of
Divine Providence, and he never gave God any credit—
seven years of howling luck in which he has hopelessly
muddled things, and prostituted the Government and
ruined the country for which he professes such an ardent
affection that the mere mention of the beloved name dis-
solves the unwieldy mass of blubber into tears. . . . The
man who couldn’t keep the country straight, with all the
luck the Forrest crowd has had, is not fit to walk behind
a night-cart and make remarks about the atmosphere.
English trades-unionists have short
memories, too. Just after the dockers’ upheaval,
the Congresses were saturated with Socialism.
Then that fell into unpopularity almost as rapidly
as it had grown in favor, and resolutions were
adopted designed to shut out the Burneses and the
Manns. But now, after the Engineers’defeat and
the Welsh coalminers’ protracted agony, the Con-
gress (this year at Bristol) shouts for Socialism
again ; and quite new men seem to be leading the
shooters.

S.A. Oppositionists, since the failure of
their attempt to oust the. Kingston Govt., have
reverted to their old game of talking against
time, and many important measures, long ago
affirmed by the people, will have to be abandoned
again this year, the Early Closing and House-
hold Suffrage ideas probably included. Opposi-
tionists, by the way, are still divided as to the
rival claims to leadership of Downer and Glynn.
Maoriland Tory Opposition is now trying
to kill the Seddon Govt, by persistently stone-
walling all business, so that it may go to the
country and ask, with a loud yell of scorn, why
Seddon hasn’t done anything during the session.
Sixty-six hours of terrific jaw were gone through
on a formal motion that the House should begin
to consider the estimates, and the stonewall only
collapsed when the legs of both parties gave way
beneath them.
"THE GREAT AUSTRALIAN BIGHT."
Crowder, Westralian M.P., makes the
legal members of the Assembly snort by an
amendment proposed in the Juries Bill that if a
Judge certifies that there was no reasonable ground
for resisting an action, the attorney responsible
shall be personally liable for all taxed costs if the
defendant does not pay up within a month.
South Australia some years ago ceased the
compilation of official law-reports—partly, it is
said, through official parsimony, and partly
through some members of the bench being
afflicted with that tired feeling. But now there
is a rumpus, and people outside the courts begin
to ask why the judgments of the courts interpret-
ing the law straggle more or less haphazard
through the daily papers, to the great financial
loss of litigants.
Dear Bulletin. — Re recent pars, on Edwin
Lilley. That much-talked-of alleged Q. Democrat
wants severely roasting. He is hanging back from
the rough work of politics in the hope that—Byrnes-
like—he may drop in just in time for the feast.
He regards himself as a sure thing for the Attorney-
Generalship in the Democratic* Ministry, when-
ever it arrives, and is leaving all the uphill,
unpopular and unprofitable struggle to grafters
like Dr ike, Glassey and Powers. It will serve
him right if he is “ left.”*— Caledonia.
Apropos of Premier Turner’s Woman
Suffrage Bill, Victoria had woman suffrage to a
limited extent about 30 or 4'» years ago, and
abandoned it. In those days it was simply enac-
ted that any “ ratepayer ” had a vote at Parlia-
mentary elections, so the male and female rate-
payer stood on the same level. It was done by
pure accident, however; the hair of Vic. Parlia-
ment stood up wilh horror at the innovation, and
Attorney-General Higinbotham abolished the
female elector sometime in the early 60’s.
Premier Byrnes, in Q. Assembly the other
day, stated that “My colleague, Mr. Philp, after
the (Papuan land-grab) agreement was signed, was
informally shown it, but the matter was one that
did not attraot his particular attention ” Philn
though understood to be no longer an actual paa-
ner in Burns, Philp and co., is still connected with
those trade-bosses of British Papua ; and that such
a matter as the considerable infringement of the
firm’s virtual monopoly in the possession should
not have attracted his attention is—well, queer !
Barton’s relation to the Hastings and
Macleay constituency will be unique, if he wins
the seat, as it is certain he will do. Barton will,
by the arrangement entered into —that the retiring
member shall attend to all local affairs —hold the
seat as a qualification for taking part in Parlia-
mentary affairs, but will represent the constituency
only under the direction of ex-member Clarke.
That leaves Barton in a political state as
nearly Federal as can be conceived in a provincial
Parliament. It also leaves Clarke’s grip on the
constituency as firm as before.
The Age, which was so hopelessly left at
the post in the recent Federal fight, seems deter-
mined to be in the running now that the
question is again on the cards. The “Liberals”
are being whipped up to a full recognition
of the “ cardinal defect ” of equal representation,
and the “financial question was left in such an
unsatisfactory condition ” that the “ whole ques-
tion of Federation will need to be re-opened.”
But the numbers are up as far as Victoria is con-
cerned ; and David comes galloping up now
that “ the race is all over bar shouting.”
In 1872 the Victorian railways yielded an
annual revenue of =£2388 per mile. Now the
revenue is <£B32 per mile. The Gillies boom,
during which Central Victoria was so crowded
with useless railways that it is now difficult to
walk five miles without falling over one, is the
main cause of this great downfall. The only
visible way out is to build more lines in the empty
regions of the east and west, where there is still
room for profitable extension, so that there may
be a bigger mileage of payable railway to carry
the dead weight of Duncan’s hopelessly unprofit-
able eockspurs.
Queensland Labor members are, unfor-
tunately, rather long-winded. The Courier pon-
derously suggests that it would be well if the
Laborites would stick up the famous forty-plank
platform on a I'lackboard in a conspicuous part of
the House, and save time and jaw-labor by simply
announcing, apropos of every discussion : “ Well,
these are our sentiments ! ” The gibe is not ill-
deserved, but if the followers of Tomjoe Byrnes
would stick up a similar blackboard with nothing
at all on it, and reply in similar spirit, “ Well,
these are our sentiments ! ” the thing would be
simply complete and beautiful.

The leader is openly jeered at by five or six of his
followers. Three-fourths of the party won’t rub

son and Hoolan hate one another with enthu-
siasm ; there is war to the death between Cross
and Worker Higgs; Sim has just seceded on a
flimsy pretext; Fitzgerald lately announced in the
House that there is “no man I would sooner fol-
low than Premier Byrnes if ” ; Jackson violates
the platform by voting for private railway bills;
and the majority, who five years ago wouldn’t use
the same spittoon as a Ministerialist, now join
their foes on roads-and-bridges deputations, jun-
kets, and intrigues. The parky is “ broadening ”
—in the wrong direction.
The private - enterprise gentleman who
wants to build the North Sydney bridge, and the
other man who. wants to connect Sydney and
North Sydney by a tunnel, are both to be heard
of again in N.S.W. Parliament this session. In
the end, it is probable that one or other of these
schemes will get through, and the bridge or
tunnel will be constructed at about twice as much
as the State could build it for (promoters’ ex-
panses, company-flotation ex.’s, directors’ fees, the
plunder of first, second and third robbers, and the
fact that the co. will probably have to pay 5 per
cent, for its borrowed money, whereas the State
could get it for 3, will make all the difference).
Then, after a year or two’s experience of the
private syndicate and its ways, there will arise an
irresistible demand for State ownership, and Govt,
will buy the enterprise at £200,000 or so more than
the necessary cost.
By a majority of 32 co 24 Queensland
Assembly has fixed Tozer’s salary as Agent-
General at <£lsoo a year. In debate it was re-
vealed that at the shuffle of the cards which sent
Nelson to the Council and opened the door of the
Premiership to Byrnes, Tozer’s appointment to the
Agency-General at the advanced rate of £l5OO a
year for three years was signed and sealed. Practi-
cally the three patriots, with the connivance of each
other but on the blind side of the public, worked
the oracle thus : Tozer and Byrnes sent Nelson to
the Council on a salary for life; Nelson and
Byrnes sent Tozer on an improved salary to Lon-
don for three years, and Tozer and Nelson opened
a path for Byrnes to the Premiership, to hold as

STEEPLE...
long as he can. Lovvles and Vine naturally felt
at home while negotiating with Queensland
patriots, and will have reason to be surprised if
their New Guinea adventure be not ratified in the
Queensland Assembly by another vote of 32 to 24.
According to the Australian Pastojral-
ists’ Review, Maoriland “boasts the most respect-
able and best-educated population in all the
colonies.”
The daily press spreads itself about the
“ gloriousness ” of the recent Soudan battle, in
which were slain a trifle of 12,000 dusky fanatics,
as against 46 Britishers. Battle? Battue!
Old London story. Policeman, after vain
attempts to get a clue as to his home, etc., from a
very little strayed boy (loq.): “ Where does your
mother get her beer ? ” Youngster : “At the Blue
Bell.” The wisdom of Solomon.
A Melb. ad. for a newspaper-canvasser
brought one reply in which applicant stated that,
although he had never done any canvassing for
advts., he thought he would suit, “as he used to
obtain orders for a butcher ” ! Rump-steak by
the inch on a 12 months’ contract!

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Perfect spring weather brought together some
15,0t)0 people at, Randwick on Derby-day. Mr. “Jack"
Samuels pulled off the Hurdle Race with Mentone, and
as he started at 7 to 1 J.S., no doubt, got back some of
the money the horse has lost for him. War God, a Melb.
Cup candidate, started favorite for the Trial Stakes, but
was unable to run into a place, the winner turning up in
Blair, a horse lately well-known on the suburban courses.
Cocos, at 5 to 4, went out favorite for the A. J.C. Derby,
but the public planked it freely on Picture—as usual ex-
pecting a straight run from anything hailing from the
Oxenham stables. They were not disappointed, as Picture
cleared out by herself at the distance and won easily from
Lee-Metford. Picture is by the imported Russley, from
Queen’s Head. 4 to 1 was the best price about Oban for
the Epsom Handicap, and, as Humphrey Oxenham had
made no secret of his good chances with Alemene, the
Gozo mare was backed by the crowd down to 7 to 1.
Alemene took command into the straight, and was never
headed, winning by a length from Influenza. Mr. Oxen-
ham backed the mare for a good stake, and, as he never
lays his own horses, his win must have been a solid one.
The Spring Stakes running was a big surprise. Amberite
was favorite at 7 to 4 on, whilst Merloolas was at 4 to l.
At the utile Merloolas went to the front and held it to
the finish, beating the favorite by four lengths. The
public picked upon the correct pea in Tubal Cain 11. for
the Kensington Handicap, but W. Delaney, who had the
mount, very nearly made a mess of it, as A. Delaney, on
Fitzroy, caught him napping, and Tubal scraped home
with only a nose to the good.
* * * •
Whenever Oxenham has a horse in a big race at
Randwick, cute punters on the flat watch the balcony’ of
his house overlooking the course. If a party appears
there to watch the race it is a good thing, but should the
balcony be deserted it is best to have your bit on some-
thing else.
• * * •
In the Hurdle Race at Randwick on Saturday
Zoroaster lost his rider first time round, but kept going
and put in the next round with the leaders, jumping
beautifully ; he eventually finished with a good run in
the straight and passed the post second. In future Zoro-
aster’s owner should tie his weight on in a sack and start
him without a boy.
• * * *
Nowhere in N.S.W. has there been such a
decay of equine glories as up at the “ Oxberry "—the dis-
trict once so celebrated for its good sportsmen and game
racehorses, where old Jorrocks used to run his three-mile
heats, and where Veno romped home after hard tussles
with his compeers. The other day, the course at Claren-
don vyas as fine a bog as you’d find in Ireland. Never a
favorite got home ; the only chance of picking a winner
was to put all the starters’ names in a hat, turn round
three times, draw one, and go and back him. Knowledge
and judgment counted for nothing ; a punter had to trust
to luck —and there was none about. In the old days, the
Hawkesbury races meant a prolonged carnival for Wind-
sor and Richmond, the hotels were full-up for weeks pre-
vious, and a general spree was indulged in by the corn-
fed population. Now, however, these meetings are merely
attended by a batch of Sydney horse-owners and a squad
of hard-faced, leather-lunged “ bookies.” Even the cater-
ing was done by a Sydneyite. Horse-racing, as a sport,
in the Hawkesbury is as dead as the present writer’s good
resolutions of 12 months ago.
* * • •
• Morris’s Fairfield once more won in the high-
jump at Royal Show, Melb., with 6ft. 2in. By the way,
what became of Lubra, who knocked ’em alffive years
ago with 6ft. lOin. ? —Jin. better than Spondulix's record.

Fairfield changed hands at 200sovs. after win-
ning the high-jump at Melb. show. The beautiful chest-
nut has won £2OOO in show prizes.
* * * •
In the case of Hawkesbury Racing Clnb and
Warwick Farm, the A.J.C. has lowered the minimum of
added money to £3OO, which hardly seems fair to some of
the other clubs.
* * • •
No fewer than 181 jockeys and 120 trainers have
been granted licenses by the A.J.C., and, of the lot, how
few are proficient in their profession ! With regard to
the majority of the trainers, their system is such a rule-
of-thumb that only castiron animals are able to stand
one of their “thorough preparations.” As for the
majority of the so-called jockeys, they deem race-riding
nothing but flogging and spurring their wretched mounts
from start to finish. And yet owners are found callous
enough to entrust valuable animals to the oare of such as
these 1
» * * *
Geelong Racing Club has decided to hold its
summer meeting Feb. 1 instead of Jan. 18, the alteration
being by arrangement with “Tatt.” who undertook to
work a Geelong Cup sweep for the later date.
* * • *
“Tattersall ” is to have competition right at his
door, from a co. formed on co-operative lines. The new
co. is to he floated in 120,000 shares of ss. each, and the
intention of the promoters is to allot small shares to as
many people as possible throughout Australasia, in order
to secure a constituency of supporters. The new co will
begin in a small way—with a 5000 consultation at 10s. on
the coming Melb. Cup, and the argument is that to risk
10s. for £lOOO in a 5000 sweep is just as good a business
risk as it is to risk 10s. for £lO,OOO in a 50,000 sweep.
* * * •
Is “ Tatt.” in favor of the tote ?
* * * •
Ikey Mo must have beamed the other day when
a couple of pretty, bad mistakes were made by the new-
chums working the tote at Kanowna (W.A.) meeting.
In the Trial Stakes they paid out a £1 18s. instead of”a
£2 ss. div., some 10s. tickets being counted as pound
tickets; and in the Flying, where there was a dead-heat,
the beautifully simple plan was adopted of lumping the
tickets, dividing by two, and paying.accordingly, instead
of paying two divs. in accordance with’the numbers on the
respective horses. The club lost £6u and the public £lO
—and, of course, it was impossible to trace ticket-
holders.
• * * •
On 7th Sept, a man was brought up at Adelaide,
charged with obtaining money under false pretences by
selling No. 3 tote-tickets on the Steeplechase which he
had thumbed into No. 8. The tickets were sold to a
dealer who bought at a small discount front lucky in-
vestors reluctant to face the crush at pay-window of
totalisator. Man committed. Evidently—nay, undoubt-
edly—suggested by a recent “ Sporting Notions " par.
* • » »
Both at the Boulder and Kalgoorlie green grass
covers the racing-track all around, which seems nothing
short of a revelation there. In fact, it is said that in the
first races some horses stopped to have a feed, while the
others, not having seen grass for so long a time, shied off.
* * • *
“Teddy” O’Rurke, son of the Maoriland
Speaker, who married a semi-millionaire Maori half-
caste, is making a reputation in England as a crack polo-
player. Years ago, young O’Rorke was regarded as one
of the straightest riders to hounds in the province, and he
was also the crack amateur jock.
« * * * •
The odd thing is that English Derby-winner
Jeddah should have started such a very red-hot favorite
(5 to 2 on) for the Leger. There was nothing strange
about the defeat of the “pot.” London Referee, in
June, speaking of Jeddah’s performances before and after
the Derby, remarked that he was two different horses,
and nobody knew which of him to expect. In the Leger
race, where he was the wrong Jeddah, the “ books ” pro-
bably gambled against him. Ikey Mo makes a golden
rule of expecting an uncertain horse to get beaten every
time, whereas the punters expect the animal to run “up
to form”—best “form.”
• * • *
J. J. Miller’s “ Australian Racing Chronicle ”
for 1897-8 contains reports of nearly 1500 race-meetings,
or about 800 more than are recorded in the rival “Turf
Register.” Yet the “ Register," being an old Tory insti-
tution at double the price of the “ Chronicle," has pro-
bably a larger sale, and Miller is rapidly growing dis-
gusted with the “sports” who thusfar have not appre-
ciated his ss. half-yearly publication at its proper value.
The “Chronicle” can be obtained from Gordon and
Gotch anywhere.
• * • •
A day’s tote takings at Kanowna (W.A.) last
month, £17,000. They must back something.
The coming Port Jackson sailing season pro-
mises to be lively. Sam Williams, who built the crack
18-footer Australian, has just finished a 22-footer for Mr.
Taylor, late owner of the Latona, and, if looks go for
anything, she should prove a heeler. The Donnelly boat
built for Mr. Sam Hordern is a noble-looking craft, if any-
thing on the big side, and should do best in calm water,
but in a blow and lumpy weather writer will not be sur-
prised to see her go under to the Williams boat, which is
to be sailed by clever George Ellis.
* ♦ * *
“Alec” Sloane, stroke of the last Vic. eight
and prominent Fitzroy footballer, was sculling up the
Yarra last week when he ran into a corpse. The body was
carried to the morgue, and Sloane was notified that he
would have to give evidence at the inquest the following
Saturday afternoon. Then an awful gloom settled on the
community, and a cold chill came out on its brow, for
on the Saturday aforesaid the Fitzroys were to battle
against Essendon for the football premiership, and what
were they to do without their trusty Sloane? Business
in Fitzroy was almost at a standstill, but eventually
Minister Best, the local member, and naturally the presi-
dent of the club, was found to interview the coroner and
get the inquest a day earlier. Trouble was thus averted,
but, had the difficulty occurred half-a-dozen years back,
Melbourne would have surely been plungedinto a red
revolution, which even the unconditional surrender of the
coroner would not have sufficed to quell. A man who
could so far forget himself as to call a leading footballer
as a witness on the day of a big match would have had to
resign, or blood would have flowed in the gutters.
Coroner Candler may congratulate himself that he does
not live in the bygone golden age of football.
* • • *
The picked team sent by W.A. Lacrosse Asso-
ciation to show the goldfields men hovvit’s done, got a big
surprise. CoMgardie beat them by 2 goals to 1 ; and,
though they beat Kalgoorlie by 2 to nil, they were downed
by a combined goldfields team by 4 to nil.' The visitors,
who relied chiefly on individual efforts, were beaten by
the grand combined play of the goldfields forwards. At a
social after the big match, the visitors’ captain magnani-
mously admitted that, although they had come up hoping
to teach the local ’crossites something, they had them-
selves learnt a lot.

• • • •
The Bulletin recorded recently that Jack
Hamilton, M.L.A., winner of the Q. revolver champion-
ship for seven years running, refrained from competing.
It now learns that he did so in order to give the next-best
man a chance. Likewise the revolver with which Ser-
geant-Major Savage won the Q. championship of ’9B was
presented to him by “Jack," who swears that Savage won
because his revolver “knew” the targets, and could not
send missiles amiss.
* * * .
Young Stewart, of Sydney Grammar School,
added a few more good performances to his already long
list at the Great Public Schools sports on Saturday, when
he won the 100, 220, and 440vds., and long-jump schools’
championships. A record that will take much beating.
Q. Attorney-General (Premier) Byrnes has
sanctioned an Eight Hours’ Art Union for sth May, ’99.
There is no gambling in Q.
• » * •
The Irish broad-jumping wonder, Newburn,
eclipsed even himself, for at Mullingar he cleared the
phenomenal distance of 24ft. 6Jin., thus beating his
former record by over 6in. Such a record should be good
enough to stand for years, when it is considered the
length of time that “ leppers” have been vainly trying to
attain the 24ft.
* * * *
“ F.” : “ Stonewall Jackson,” the old-time Aus-
tralian pug., is not dead; he is very much alive. His
present occupation is kangarooing and pig-charming on
the Bogan.
* • * *
At Kalgoorlie, the other Sunday night, when
baited by a crowd the Salvationist fighting member, in
red, gave forth that any bloke what would put up a
tenner against his was welcome to knock ’im out. No
takers.
* * • *
Fitzsimmons is giving Corbett a surfeit of the
medicine the latter ladled out so plentifully in the old
days. Fitz recently put up a deposit of (5000 for a
match with Jeffries, whereupon Corbett’s manager
covered Fitz's deposit with the object of a match. Fitz’s
reply was that he was only going to tackle men he had
not beaten previously, but added that if Corbett met
and beat either Jeffries or Sharkey then he would talk to
him. Jeffries has since broken his hand, and is laid up
for repairs, whilst Sharkey is not the sort of fish
Corbett is likely to be anxious to catch. Whatever the
other pugs may be doing, thousands seem to be plentiful
with Fitz and Corbett—unless, perish the thought, the
deposits were in the form of a cheque.
Robert Alexander, who died at Milton
(Brisbane suburb, Q.) t’other day, first came to
Australia in ’4O and started squatting in New
England (N.S.W.), in partnership with Robert
Lawson. _ The two Bobs took their sheep to Bur-
nett district (Q.), and were first owners of Boon-
doomba and Proston stations. In the fifties, they
dissolved partnership, Alexander taking Proston.
In the sixties, he bought Bovinia, and went North
in ’62 to explore the Flinders country (Gulf), after-
wards trying to travel up 10,000 ewes, but
droughts and poison killed off 8000 of them.
Going under in squatting in ’75 he became P.M.
at Thargomindah in ’77, following on at Beenleigh
and Taroom. Four years ago he retired from the
Govt, service, and thereafter resided in or near
Brisbane. He married a daughter of the late 0.
Sheriff Halloran.
Gory ructions may be expected at next
meeting of Melb. Presbyterian Assembly conse-
quent upon the sale of Chalmers Church for
ill,ooo to the St. Vincent’s R.C. Hospital. In
the days of Chalmers himself, the old man used
to drop his eyes when he left the church by the
back way for fear of seeing the heretics emerg-
ing from St. Pat’s on t’other side of the
road ; and, after it was decided to shut up and
. sell the edifice, the Orange crowd waved the
tomato shirt so extensively in the Presbyterian
Assembly that an auction was tabooed for fear the
“ Cartholics ” might get hold of the fane. Pri-
vate sale, it was agreed, was the only way to
effectually prevent the desecration of the good old
building by the Scarlet Woman, and accordingly
it was sold to a duly-qualified Protestant person
(to wit Colonel Waddell, of Sydney), who took a
profit of .£lOOO, or over, on his deal and traded it
off to St. Vincent’s Hospital. Dr. Morrison, of
the Scotch College next door, who has long been
looking forward to getting the extra slice of
ground, is now blocked. When all’s said and
done, nobody, not even the Scotch or the Jews,
can beat the Pope and co. at a bargain.

27 of this issue.*
The Atacama Case.
This case, in which The Bcllettn is charged
with having libelled Mr. M. C. Cowlishaw by
publishing on March 26 last an article dealing
with the loss of the Atacama, of which Mr.
Cowlishaw was registered owner, has to-day
(Sept. 13) reached its 11th day; and stiil
continues before Chief Justice Darley and a
special jury of twelve. The plaintiff called 20
witnesses, and The Bulletin will probably call
even more, so that the trial will spin out to a
length and expense unprecedented in N.S.W.
