British aristocrats earning a humble, but
more or less honest livelihood :—Earl of Harring-
ton, small fruit-shop at Charing Cross ; Earl of
Wmchelsea, agricultural store in Long Acre;
Lord Rayleigh, milk-shop near British Museum ;
Lord Rosslyn, perfumer in North London ; Hon.
Roland Leigh, laundry in Romford-road ; Lord
Windsor, pub. at St. Fagans.
A few days after Sydney Smith, N.S.W.
Minister for Pumpkins, was rejected for
Bathurst, his friends resolved to apply a plaster
to his lacerated feelings in the shape of a testi-
monial. Accordingly, the hat was sent round,
and was kept on circuit for several weeks. Last
week, at the local School of Arts, the ex-Minister
was given a purse of sovs. The presentation was
made with loud ostentation, tempered with deep
gloom—said gloom caused, no doubt, by the
“ timber-yard ’' appearance of the hall, but more
particularly owing to the distressing fact that the
purse contained less than 50 gold pieces. Four-
teen of these came from Upper-House Webb and
connections (local storekeepers); 20 were sub-
scribed by Smith’s political opponents, and the
balance (somewhere about £l4 18s. 6d.) by the
large body of his loyal and liberal supporters.
The funereal function concluded with the late
Minister for Vegetables returning emotional
thanks for the gigantic offering.
Dear Bulletin. —Just a little room to con-
firm the views of “Herodias” in re artistic inep-
titude. As he truly says, “ the public is sane ”
—eminently so; likewise commonplace and unima-
ginative, as is proper to sanity. Grass? I should
smile. Green every time—no, sometimes brown ;
depends on climate. And isn’t the sky (look
at it, not through it) very much like an in-
verted blue enamelled saucepan? As for the
sea, that huge salt pond—what price edible fish ?
Bo on. The “looney ” artist somehow misses the
aspect of things. He hardly sees the grass for the
sunlight that floods it, infinite depths of azure are
above him, while before him gleams the sea like a
liquid sapphire. Poor, harmless crank, drunk with
the wine of beauty, besotted with riotous revelling
in the palace of truth, mad with the frenzied en-
thusiasm of the dreamer ! No wonder the sane
public sniggers as it jams its way into the music-
hall and rushes the bookstall for Marie Corelli,
while framed oleographs decorate its home. Feel-
ing hopelessly out of it, the poor artist is forced
to content himself with Shakespeare, with Bee-
thoven and with—well, say, Streeton. “ Give
us ‘ toons ’ and legs,” yells the public. Sane ?
You bet. Good old Public !— Aletes.

Vic. Justice Williams on Crown Prose-
cutors :
Good men, of clear intellect, who knew their profession
well, should be employed. There was one who was the
best Crown Prosecutor who had ever come within his ex-
perience. His calmness of mind, judicial attitude in re-
lation to prisoners, perspicacity and common-sense he
envied ; and that Crown Prosecutor would make a beau-
ideal Judge for the Criminal Court, if the Govt, appointed
a Judge solely for that Court.
The Law Commission is likely to recommend such
an appointment, but Finlayson, the man indicated,
will probably be passed over in favor of a politician.
The Co-operative Wool and Produce Co.
Ltd., are now going round wearing the following
telegram in their hat—“ We authorise you to pub-
lish ; we entirely disapprove of steps taken against
you by few buyers ; you may rely on our support:
Marquiset, Joncquel, Laine, Hercuez, Vandamme,
Lamerand, Nutte, Gaillet, Lhoest, Betz, Reichenecker,
Zeroux, Duvillier, Layeock, Vettleton.”
This telegram to the Co-operative Co. from these
French, German, and English buyers, means the
burst-up of the recent boycott. So. on 22nd inst.,
the woolgrowers’ champions will hold a sale of
3000 bales in Sydney Chamber of Commerce.*
About the only excitement the way-out
Westralian camps have in these boomless days is
the capture and almost inevitable subsequent
escape of bad niggers. Thus Mt. Margaret has
been entertained lately by Stone Soak Jimmy—a
mostly-naked gentleman with a turn for thieving.
Captured once, he escaped, but was collared along
with “ another desperado,” named Micky ; again
escaped, “though he was chained round the
neck,” but was run down a third time. “Bar
accidents,” a local man pathetically \\ rites, “we
hope to get rid of him for at least two years.”
Messrs. Plummer, Love and co. have made
a sensation in the Australian liquor trade with
Teacher’s Highland cream whisky. This whisky
they placed on the market some eight months ago,
but the demand for it soon used up the available
stock. A new stock has come to hand, and, as
more is on the way hither, Messrs. Plummer,
Love have no fear of another famine in their
choice brand. Considering the competition there
is in whiskies now, Teacher’s Highland cream
whisky has scored a remarkable success.*
Melb. Tocsin on “ Immoral Sydney ” :
Human passion seems to have thrown off all the re-
straints of religion ; divine laws and ordinances are
treated with contempt. Crime stalks unabashed and un-
molested through the city. The streets are teeming with
painted temptresses, and thronged with unpunished
criminals and impenitent felons. Commercial and politi-
cal integrity is almost unknown, and the very founts of
justice are polluted at their source. Merciful Heaven, is
this a Christian city ? Christ’s weeping over Jerusalem
could be as nothing to the anguish He would experience
if He came in the flesh to Sydney and witnessed the
failure of His religion in this mammon-worshipping,
crime-stained, and sin-polluted city—this city which is
pre-eminent among the cities of the world as the home
of juvenile prostitutes—this city in which is rampant that
class of crime which a pure heart may not conceive, nor
a chaste lip utter.
Hum ! Smellbourne is evidently jealous.
The Parkes Bursary Committee can only
offer a prize for 1898. The money is not coming
in according to anticipations. In fact, there is
little hope now that Bursary proportions will ever
be reached. The prize for ’9B will go for the most
deserving pass at the Technical College examina-
tions.

At Poverty Point.
An interesting point in theatrical law cropped up at
Ferth (W.A.) last week. Martyn Hagan and lively Lucy
Fraser, his wife, sued Rickards for their return fares to
Melb. The Rickards reply was that Mr. and Mrs. H. had
forfeited their right to the return fares by staying in
W.A., after the termination of their engagement, and
giving performances “on their own 1 ” Case dismissed.
“ J.P.” : Seeing Kennedy’s mesmeric show the other
night recalled to me Madame Sibley, the old-time mesmer-
iste. She was once performing in Twowharves, a small
town on Richmond River, and, during interval between
first and second parts, she carried round among the spec-
tators a phenomenally small infant on an ordinary
saucer. The mite was alive and gruesome to behold.
“ What do you think of it ? ” Madame asked a hardfaced
old cockie. “Well,” was the hoarse reply, “it looks to
me like a machine-made kid 1 ” Some of these river
cockies have seen a lot.
When Harry Hodgson was stage-managing at Bendigo
(Vic.) for Milne, the American tragedian, he took great
pains once to explain to the supers, the story of
Casar’s assassination. After shouting himself hoarse
with the speeches of Brutus and Antony, a hand was
held up at the back, and a voice asked,* “ Please, Mr.
’Odson, can I wear my fireman’s uniform in this ’ere
scene to-night?”
The young Duke of Manchester is a very stage-struck
person, like his father before him. The late Duke was
commonly known as “ Uncle ” among the ballets and
choruses of Great Britain and Ireland.
A highly promising dancer is the damsel (Miss Syl-
vester) who plays the boy in that pretty little ballet at
Melbourne Royal. She is said to be “well connected,”
apart from her figure, and “not more than 15 or 16,” in
which case Miss S. is every inch a daisy for her age.
Several people must have smiled when Newbury started
his first number at Melb. Town Hall “pop” concert last
Saturday. It was the air from “La Favorita,” which
Biondi’s orchestra plays under the direction of Donizetti.
“ Dar- dar-de-dar,” the sugary music goes, and the con-
ductor, with half-closed eyes and a deeply sentimental
turn of his giddy old head, endeavors to look twice as
tender as a spring chicken. Donizetti is the gem of
Biondi’s repertoire.
A recent Brisbane sight was George V. Rignold wobbling
along on a bike larding the lean earth from every pore.
Even the points of his moustache drooped.
“Paradis”: Biondi recalls to me Greville in a panto-
mime of “Jack Sheppard.” Acting Jonathan Wild, he
appeared in a multiplicity of disguises, and at last flab-
bergasted everyone by springing on the stage as con-
ductor of the orchestra.
“ Haresfoot ” : Miss Appleton graces “ Round the
World in Eighty Days,” a vivid reminder of her popular
mother, who, as Miss Jenny Bryce, was a pantomime
star, with the three Stewarts—Nellie, Docy, and Maggie.
“ A.V.G.” : My first recollection of Peggy Pryde is as a
herald in a Manchester panto., announcing in a rather
reedy, juvenile voice the various countries in a grand
procession of nations, “ France, Germany, Rooshia,” &e.,
the pronunciation of the last-named being given with
special gusto. It was about the time but perhaps it is
hardly fair to Miss Peggy to mention dates. At any rate,
she was very young then.
“Theorbo” : Exit Julius Herz to the land of eternal
melody. Julius might have made his little mark as a
German composer if he hadn’t come to Melb. some 30
odd years back. In ’72 he helped to organise Melb.
Metropolitan Licdertafel, and reigned as conductor
thereof for 20 years. In ’92 the happy-go-lucky musician
severed his connection with Melbourne and went to
Westralia under the patronage of Gov. Bill Robinson.
There he was in clover for a time, but the time didn’t
last. The vice-regal troubadour went back to England
and died, and Herz, returning to Melb., found himself
out in the cold. His old Liedertafel billet had passed
into the keeping of Ernest Wood. He resumed practice
as a music-teacher, a smaller man than of yore, and was
beginning to be almost forgotten when his name appeared
among the obituary notices in the newspapers. The Met.
Liedertafelers talk of raising a subscription for widow
Herz, nee Miss Freyberger, a* lady who, prior to marriage,
gave promise of achieving distinction as a singer.
Florence Sohmidt went away to make her fortune in
Europe with over £lOOO in her pocket. Her three-months'
farewell tour in Queensland panned out a clear profit of
£940, and her Sydney ben. must have netted her at least
£2OO.
Since Madame Phillippini left for England, danseuse
Minnie Everitt has been running the ballet for the Firm.
She contrived the pretty little country dance which fol-
lows Bert Gilbert’s song‘in “ Harbor Lights."
George Darrell, the indefatigable, has contrived to stage
“ The Sunny South ”in London once again. And at the
Surrey Theatre, too. The Surrey can’t boast of much
‘I class,” but its audiences are not disposed to swallow
“ tripe ” unless it is pretty well done. George’s greatest
drammer, when you come to remember it, is not a bad
dish of tripe.
Dear Bulletin. —“ H.” is wrong in saying that the
expose of the Davenport Brothers’ “ spiritualistic ” busi-
ness occurred at Liverpool. It was brought off at the
Trades Hall, Manchester, by Henry Irving, in conjunction
with our very old friends, Fred. Maccabe and Phil. Day.
The affair caused so much excitement that the three
actors gave a special “ stance ” for the benefit of Man-
chester charities, and their exposure of the precious
Davenport show was so complete that the brothers never
boomed again.—N.
Fred. B. Norton writes from Melbourne : “As an old
friend of Billy Ashcroft, and an ex-manager of the
Alhambra Music-hall, Belfast, whereof Billy is now pro-
prietor, I would like to remark that the Scarecrow Dance
of Johnny Coleman was new to ‘ yours truly ’ in Aus-
tralia. I have seen Harry Sefton do some scarecrow
business, but jt was not nearly as humorous as Coleman's
dance. As for the statement that Billy Ashcroft per-
formed the same dance in England 20 years ago, I can
give it an unquestionable contradiction. Between '74
and ’BB I fulfilled many engagements—more than a dozen
perhaps—at various halls where Billy was a popular
favorite, and it so happened that I generally had to fol-
low his ‘ turn,’ which meant standing at the wing and
watching him knock the audience. I’ve played under his
management at the Alhambra aforesaid, and when I
visited the town with My (large M, if you please) drama-
tic co. I have seen his turn from the front of the house.
Ashcroft was the ‘Solid Man’ and ‘Mr. John M'Gee’
(two different songs) all this time, but he never danced
the ‘ Scarecrow.’ Neither was the short, thick-set Irish
comedian said to have done anything so unlike himself in
America, from which country he had departed long be-
fore Coleman’s time. Coleman was a boy in the days I’m
speaking of. Now lam but an ‘ advertising expert,’ but
then I was a comique, and I think I remember patting
little Johnny Coleman’s head.
“T.D.” : In bygone days, at Rockhampton, departed
Florence Schmidt was known as the “ singing girl,” and
was always in great request for a song or so at picnics and
such diversions. I went to Steffarii’s concert t'other
night with a Northerner (latterly of W.A.). When Miss
Schmidt made her bow, he startled everybody near with
the loud ejaculation: “ The singing girl ! Well, I’m
darned 1 ”
Cecil Ward stays on in Adelaide fora “Silver King”
and “Harbor Lights” season with W. and M., while
Pattie Brown takes the “ Little Minister ” through Maori-
land.
Horace Poussard, violinist, a notable figure in Sydney
musical circles for the last 12 years, died suddenly from
brain-hemorrhage on Sept. 12, aged 71 vears. He was
teaching almost to the last hour of his life. Born
in France, he early became a brilliant musician,
toured the world, and visited Australia in the sixties
with Douay, the ’celloist. In ’B2 he settled in
Sydney ; and his spare figure, eager face, and flowing
locks have been earnest of a musical treat on many a
platform since. As a teacher, he made his mark on many
clever young Australians. Australia loses a true artist in
old Horace Poussard.
Carlyle Smythe, the Much-Travelled’s son,
has given up the editorship of the English paper
in Brussels, and is now in Paris. He is expected
back in Melbourne at the end of the year. C.S.
has written an account of Mark Twain, as he
knew him privately, and the yarn is to appear in
next Pall Mall Magazine.

The popular notion that a stomach is
necessary to the physical economy has been upset
by a Swiss professor, who months ago, in order to
save a woman’s life, removed her stomach. She
has since got along all right without it, has gained
flesh, has an excellent appetite, and doesn’t seem
to miss anything in particular. The daring opera-
tor merely ‘ joined the intestines to the digestive
tube.”
This quarter’s N.S.W. “ Postal Guide ” :
Queen bees, and live but harmless entomological speci-
mens, will be transmitted by post within the Australasian
colonies or to any other part of the world where admitted
bv local regulations.
All live specimens must be secured in strong, safe, and
properly-ventilated boxes or cages of wood or metal.
Specimens enclosed in cardbox or other fragile material
will not be forwarded.
“Strong, safe cages of wood or metal.” The idea
of a scatter-bug or a “ forty-legs ” escaping from
his cage and ferociously attacking one of Varney
Parkes’ officials is too horrible to contemplate.
This writer once accompanied N.S.W,
Premier Cowper and some others on an official
visit to Gladesvilie Asylum. W hen the inspection
was over the scribe went down to the launch at
the jetty and there found a decent-looking old
man fishing, and conversing with the water-police
in the launch. His talk was bright and attractive.
He said he lived in the neighborhood and took a
keen interest in the welfare of the poor lunatics—
half of whom, he contended, were infinitely more
sane than those who kept them there. Presently
some of the other visitors came along, arid with
them Dr. Manning. When the old chap saw' the
doctor he gathered up his lines and cried : “ And
that man is the maddest here. Don’t provoke
him. Humor him and get away quietly.” Then
he slunk off himself. He was the champion crank
of the asylum.
A holy and uninviting N.S.W. spinster
thinks she has a youthful local parson hooked ;
but, hearing with horror that he has become en-
gaged to a certain nice-looking girl, who put the
noses of the feminine community out of joint
and had been freely scandalised in consequence,
writes to inquire, and receives following truly
remarkable epistle :
Dear Miss .—Your letter to hand. I summarise
its contents as a request for mv reason, in preferring my
betrothed to yourself. You are doubtless aware that I
am under vow t-> devote my life to the salvation of souls ;
and, even in choosing a life-partner, to make sacrifices
for this object. From your own lips I heard how neces-
sary were my services in the case of the lady about to
take my name, and my few months’ acquaintance with
her have led to the conclusion that I must resort to
extreme measures to bring her to the fold. As I had
made no proposal to you, and believed that our union
would not tend to increase the flock —you being already
a member of it—l besieged the stronghold of Satan in
the person of Miss ; and I feel assured that, after
this explanation, you will rejoice with me at snatching a
brand from the burning.—J.B.
What a fearsome thing logically-carried-out
religion would be!

Is it generally known that in Australia,
under the Imperial Fugitive Offenders Act, a
witness in a criminal case can be dragged from a
province to which lie has levanted to the one in
which he is wanted, by the magisterial endorse-
ment of the subpoena?
Vie. penal statistics show the greatest
number of prisoners as from 20 to 30 years of age,
the next two decades exhibiting considerable de-
crease, and the 60-to 70 period displaying a trifling
increase—probably due to senile weakening of the
precautionary and previsory faculties.
A grim paragraph :
Times are still very tout'll for hundreds of poor devils
in each of the populous suburbs of Melbourne, despite
Treas. Turner’s splendid surplus—which, by the vva.v, is
due entirely to the fact that many wealthy men bave i
been considerate enough to die during the past year, and
which, furthermore, is really a substantial deficit when
lhe Stiiels bonds are accounted for, as they must be.
Have met with two instances of lunacy, induced by out-
of-work terror and wear and tear, in one suburb alone.
The first unfortunate, after a game effort —extending over
six months—to find work,.succumbed intellectually, and
now imagines himself a magnificent and munificent cap-
italist, and goes about bestowing constant billets at £3
a week upon all and sundry. Another man, once a
successful city tradesman, since anything that offered, —
bricklayer’s laborer, bookmaker’s clerk, and bone-gatherer
—after seeking constantly for months the chance to earn
bread for his family, went stuttering from sheer ex-
haustion induced by hunger into the office of a city firm
in answer to an advt., and was told, “ Damn it, man, go
and learn to speak before you come here for work ! ” He
returned home, maundering about this, and is now at.
Yarra-bend Asylum—believes he has built a palace, and it
is his daily duty to carry luxurious food and gorgeous
clothes to his wife and family—whom God help 1 As soon
as news spreads of a fatal accident in a sewer or at a
factory, the first rush is not to help find the dead man,
but to secure his billet. I heard one workman declare,
speaking of a fatality in a sewer tunnel : “ I heard the
explosion, and I ran every step of the way, but two men
had seen the boss before me and there was only one man
killed."

A GREAT 1,000 MILES RACE.
HOW DAVE HIRE WON HIS WAGER.
A ONCE BED RIDDEN CRIPPLE TRIUMPHANTLY TOURS VICTORIA.
RIDING THROUGH SLEET AND HAIL.-NEVER ONCE RUBBED DOWN.—INTER.
VIEW WITH DAVE KIRK.—HOW HE OVERCAME HIS DIFFICULTIES.—AN
ASTOUNDING DISPLAY OF ENDURANCE.
INTERESTING READING FOR CYCLISTS.
A reporter of the Melbourne Advocate, seeing
in the city and provincial press glowing accounts
of an almost incredible feat on the part of one of
the best-known and highly-popular cyclists of
Victoria, Dave Kirk, saw him at his residence,
Aylesbury House, 59 Park-street, East Bruns-
wick, and had a friendly chat with the erstwhile
bed-ridden, but now robust and agile, favorite of
the track. In August of last year a reporter was
favored with a first interview with Kirk regarding
his complete restoration to health, although
several prominent surgeons had not long before
given it as their opinion that Kirk would be for
ever a hopeless cripple. All Melbourne, all Vic-
toria, and even the sister colonies, were marvelling
at his recovery from what was, to all intents and
purposes, his life-long illness. Not without reason
has the present century been called an age
of enlightenment and progress, and justly does
the Westminster Gazette remark that medi-
cine in our day is practically a new science.
But it can be safely said that not only our genera-
tion, but ages to come, will admit that the most
marvellous and fruitful blessing to suffering
humanity in the scientific field of the present cen-
tury is the medical discovery which is ever"where
MR. DAVE KIRK, VICTORIA’S CYCLING HERO.
known as Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People
—an unparalleled remedy for numerous ills to
which poor humanity is heir. True, it does not
require much solid reason to give an impetus to
some alleged discoveries in arts and sciences, but
in the field of medicine the touchstone of success
must absolutely rest.on results, and amongst the
long array of solidly established cures stands out
in bold relief, challenging the medical and the lay
mind alike, the PERMANENT AND COM-
PLETE CURE of the subject of this interview,
Dave Kirk.
Nearly everyone has mastered the particulars of
Kirk’s marvellous case, but, for the benefit of
those who have not, a summary is appended :
KIRK’S TERRIBLE ACCIDENT.
On the Bth October, 1896, Dave Kirk was train-
ing on the Scotch College Grounds, Melbourne,
when he was suddenly hurled with tremendous
force to the ground, through the tire of the leader
breaking. The unfortunate man lay for some
minutes a helpless and wrecked mass of humanity.
His friends, not daring to hope, hurried the appa-
rently lifeless form to the Melbourne Hospital.
The poor fellow was at once admitted to the
casualty ward, where the fearful nature of his
injuries caused the surgeons, who had been hastily
summoned, to shake their heads in significant
silence. Indeed, they held out but slight hopes
of being able to pull the mangled young man
through. For many months the patient lingered
betwixt life and death—a victim of hemorrhage,
spinal disease, pleuisy, dysentery, complete para-
lysis of the body, and last, but by no means least,
locomotor ataxia. This last-named is a fearful
malady, which deprives the victim of all power
over the operations of nature, which take
action irrespective of the will. Kirk’s left
heel had to be removed, and thirty-two stitches
were inserted in that part of his foot, 18 being
placed in the right leg. The kneecap was smashed,
and had to be lifted. Altogether, seven surgeons
had to do with the sufferer, who was reduced to a
very weak and almost comatose state, after nine
operations had been performed on him. Mortifi-
cation set in in parts of the lower limbs, and arti-
ficial means had to be employed to draw off the
water. The system was washed internally four
times every day with water and carbolic acid, the
stomach being cleaned out by means of a tube.
Injections were also largely employed. This was
a sad wreck of the man who had been a powerful
and successful cyclist on many a hard-contested
ring. Dave’s friends, at length despairing of ever
seeing their favorite wheelman on his feet again,
had him removed with gentle care from the hos-
pital to his home.
And yet to-day, Kirk, in the best of health and
spirits, has ridden 1042 miles through Victoria, for
a wager, in the short space of 14 days, under the
most adverse circumstances possible.
When Dave’s relatives found the fearful condi-
tion to which he had been reduced, they naturally
looked out for something that would relieve him
of his many sufferings. Doctors and patent medi-
cines had absolutely failed, and, as a last resource,
the newly - discovered medicine, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People, was brought into play.
An almost
IMMEDIATE CHANGE FOR THE
BETTER
was experienced ; but it is more*satisfactory to let
the then sufferer speak for himself :
“ Gradually,” says he, “ the sense of numbness
began to disappear, and the extremities acquired
by degrees their wonted healthy glow and warmth.
During my enforced stay in the hospital the sense
of feeling had almost deserted my members. To
be sure of the absence of the sense of touch, the
doctors would conceal my vision, and then ques-
tion me as to the part they were piercing ; but my
answers clearly proved to them that I was abso-
lutely bereft of all sensibility !
“After a month’s trial of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills I was able to throw aside my crutches. But
one of the most powerful effects of the pills was
the restoration of functional health. Bowel
troubles had been a terrible trial to me ever since
the day I met with the accident. Regularity in
this direction was perfectly set up, and I am now,
after four months’ taking of these pills, in the
enjoyment of perfect health. ”
So remarkable was this cure that it attracted
general notice throughout the Australian colonies.
Doubtless, much of this notoriety was due to the
fame of the victim of that dire accident, which
brought him to within an ace of death. Dave had
won his spurs on many an Australian cycle track,
being placed, as a general rule, on “ scratch ” for
each event. The sporting world was deeply
affected to hear of what was regarded as his life-
long enforced absence from the track. Hence it
can well be imagined with what general satisfac-
tion the public welcomed his absolute cure, due
solely to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. But the prac-
tical age in which we move likes to be thoroughly
satisfied about the genuineness of a statement
before giving it its adhesion. People were say-
ing, “Well, if Dave Kirk is so thoroughly him-
self again, we should like to see some practical evi-
dence of so remarkable a triumph of medicine over
disease and death.”
No sooner said than done ! On the Bth July
last Dave, accompanied his trainer, Wren Mat-
thews, mounted one of the famous “ Dayton ”
racers, and, although his friends, acquaintances,
and the press tried to dissuade him, started upon
a tour of the colony. He undertook to do 1000
miles in 28 days, but in reality he covered 1042
miles in 14 days ! The very fact of the man
starting on such a journey was sufficient to satisfy
the most matter-of-fact and incredulous as to the
genuineness of his restored health and vigor of
mind and body. But this is not all : it is well
here to emphasise the fact that Dave believes he
did 3000 miles, computed against wind and
weather.
The splendid average of seventy-four and a half
miles per day was maintained throughout the trip.
As an example of muscular power and endurance,
it may be mentioned that Dave rode from Ararat
to Beaufort, a. distance of 28 miles, in If hours,
and at the finish of that run did a walk on foot of
13 miles ! Carbine ! Marvellous work, surely,
for an erstwhile cripple ! Yet this is true in every
particular, and vouchedfor on unquestionable testi-
mony. The route taken by Kirk and his trainer
was through Sunbury, Kynetown, Castlemaine,
Harcourt, and Bendigo. Wherever Dave went
he was literally lionised by the cycling fraternity,
and wherever a club was stationed he was heartily
welcomed, and escorted for some distance on the
next stage. The steep gradients between Castle-
main and Bendigo, notably those experienced
after passing Harcourt, famous for its granite
boulders, are well-known to cyclists. Kirk, how-
ever, mounted these with the greatest possible
ease, and out-distanced some half-dozen “wheels ”
who attempted to keep up with him. His friends
gave him 28 days in which to negotiate 1000 miles,
but he did more than that distance in half the
time. An important point, which greatly strength-
ens the case, is the
TERRIBLY SEVERE WEATHER
which was encountered during the whole fortnight
Kirk was on tour. It rained almost incessantly ;
snow and hail were met with, and a gale of ex-
traordinary violence raged, making the journey a
heavy labor for the stoutest heart. The roads in
places were almost impassible owing to the con-
tinuous downpour. Nevertheless, Kirk rode on

with a i ight heart, happy in the possession of “ a
sound m f nd in a sound body.’ He was filled with
gratitude to the greatest benefactor of the age.

H is arrival in the various centres of population

fche credifc to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and he
advised those suffering from dysentery, hemorr-

fatigue beyond that which was to be expected,
Dave laughingly replied—“ Not the least.’’ His
next line was through Elmore and Rochester to
Echuca. Coming back to Bendigo, he made
Maryborough, where he found the fame of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills had preceded him. Indeed,
a well-known local chemist, Mr. F. Ogle, in-
formed Kirk that he found no small difficulty in
keeping sufficient stocks of the invaluable pills to
satisfy the wants of his ever-increasing customers
Dunolly and St. Arnaud were touched. Included
in what is destined to be regarded in the annals of
Victorian cycling as a thrice-famous run may be
mentioned Ballarat, Geelong, Camperdown, Mort-
lake, Kyneton, Terang, Warrnambool, Koroit,
Hamilton, etc. Quite an ovation was accorded
the plucky and highly-popular crack cyclist wher-
ever he went. But the most astonishing thing
about it all is that, after having undergone such a
labor as is implied in the track traversed by Kirk,
he returned to the metropolis with an
INCREASE OF A STONE IN
WEIGHT !
Upon his return, Kirk freely entered into a chat
anent his trip. “ Well,” he said, “ you see I have
greatly improved in weight as a consequence of
my trip. During the journey I was almost
ashamed of my appetite. After doing a big day’s
ride, lasting up to midnight, I felt quite comfort-
able, and after a hearty supper fell into a sound
sleep.
“ Look at my limbs,” said he, exposing his
legs ; and, indeed, a more splendid pair of calves
it would be difficult to see. “ You will scarcely
believe it,” said he, “but I never required a rub
down at the finish of a day’s hard riding, and the
trip was one of the severest I have tackled. I had
to push on in the teeth of a head wind, which was
almost strong enough to blow the head off one ! I
did not feel the slightest want of medicine or
stimulant of any kind ” (Dave is a total abstainer),
“but if the necessity had arisen, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills would have been my friend as usual. I
would like a word about the Dayton machine, on
which I did my 1042 miles in 14 days. There
was no occasion even to blow up the tire, nor
did so much as a screw loosen during the run.
You can imagine how greatly surprised everybody
was when I returned to Melbourne in such exube-
rant health and spirits. Congratulations poured
in, and, as was naturally to be expected, the fame
of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills continues to gain in
popular favor. In several places I found people
who did not hesitate to say that they had been
cured of various ailments by a course of those won-
derful pills, thus confirming the now public, verdict
regarding their never-failing efficacy in the treat-
ment of all diseases arising from disorders of the
blood. It is my intention to introduce Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills among my chums of the track,
and to impress on them the fact of their wondrous
healing properties. Wren Matthews accompanied
me as a precautionary proviso. This arrangement,
however, proved altogether unnecessary, for,
although I experienced no fewer than eight falls,
my strength and strong
MERVE NEVER FAILED ME
during the 1042 miles. So strong did I feel, and I
was in such splendid fettle, that the trip could
have been accomplished in 12 days! However,
you will admit that all who tried to dissuade me
from the undertaking have been quite out in their
reckoning. The challenge of those who felt some
lingering doubts as to the absolute thoroughness
and permanency of my cure ought to be more
than satisfied. As a further proof of my strength
and muscle, and a test of staying-power and en-
durance, I am ready, at a moment’s notice, to ride
from Melbourne to Sydney, 580 miles, at almost
twice the speed of the Victorian tour.”

Chats From the Papers.
Whilst Kirk was on tour the various provincial
newspapers evinced the liveliest interest in his pro-
gress, and one and all were astounded at his mag-
nificent condition, many of them expecting him to
show, in a more or less degree, signs of his former
illness. We reprint a few of their pars., which
are sure to prove of interest.
The Australian Cyclist, 28th July, says
“ Dave Kirk, the much-shattered racing crack, is
pegging away over the 1000-mile course which he
has to cover ere he wins his £5O wager. He has
been now a little over two weeks away, and has
visited Kyneton, Castlemaine, Bendigo, Creswick,
Ararat, and Stawell, and speaks highly of the
kindness of the various country clubmen, who
have helped him in every way. Kirk is over the
worst part of the road, having ridden 560 miles.
Should he not have any accidents, he hopes to
complete the journey by the 29th of this month.
During the trip Kirk has had seven falls, but, so
far, he has been able to keep going, and is at
present 60 miles ahead of his schedule distance.”
The Cycling News:— “We hear that ‘Dave’
Kirk, a well-known cyclist of Victoria, famous for
his many misfortunes on the wheel, has made a
wager to ride 1000 miles in three weeks. After
Kirk’s last accident he remained in the Melbourne
Hospital for months suffering from partial
paralysis, the result of his injuries, and was ulti-
mately discharged as incurable. Upon Kirk’s
discharge from the hospital he was the beneficiary
of a cycling meeting held at St. Kilda, and was
set up in business. Such have been his recupera-
tive powers that had anyone 12 months ago sug-
gested that Kirk would ever ride a bicycle again
he would have been laughed at; yet we are now
staggered with the news that he is going to make
an attempt to ride 1000 miles on a bicycle. Kirk’s
recovery has been a marvellous one, but we think
that his ride is ill-advised, considering the terrible
injuries he has suffered from.”
The Bendigo Advertiser Dave Kirk and
Wren Matthews, two well-known cyclists, arrived
in Bendigo yesterday from Melbourne. Kirk is
passing through Bendigo on a long ride of 1000
miles to comply with the terms of a wager made
with him. If he succeeds in riding that distance
on his bicvele ho is to receive ,£5O. Captain T.
Hunter, °f the Bendigo Bicycle Club, who went
out to Harcourt to meet Kirk and his companion,
says that he was surprised to see Kirk propel his
machine up the steep gradients between Har-
court and Bendigo. Kirk and his companion leave
here this morning for Maryborough and Ballarat,
whence they will proceed to the Western dis-
trict. Kirk some time back met with severe
m]unes, the result of an accident on the race
path, which laid him up for some months, and it
was generally understood that he would never
nde again. He is mounted on a Dayton machine,
and prior to leaving here was congratulated on his
successful ride from Melbourne.”
Ckeswick Advertiser “ Mr. Kirk is now
doing his first ride for eighteen months, and is
going to ride 1000 miles for a wager of >£so.
Already he has come from Melbourne, passing
through Bendigo and Maryborough, and will con-
tinue his tour to Warrnambool, via Ballarat, Ara-
rat, and Stawell, returning thence to the metro-
polis again. On the completion of this round,
half the stake will be paid to him. Then,
to gain the balance, he must ride to Sydney
and compete in the Austral Race. To realise
what this means, it must be understood that the
Cyclist gave a portrait of Kirk on crutches, a
long time ago, with the title, ‘A crack for ever
off the track ; ’ the doctors gave him up ; he has
had injuries to his head, spine, legs, arms, and
r? • ’ as over 80 stitches in various parts
of his body, and once struck the ground with his
head with such force that, when picked up, many
yards from the place he first touched, the soles of
his feet were found to be burst open—a pheno-
menon that has defied the reasoning of the ablest
medical men. And still he rides ! ”
Clunes Guardian and Gazette : —“My readers
all know Dave Kirk, the cyclist, by name, if
not by person, through having read of him in
the Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills advertisement.
Some considerable time ago he was literally
smashed, up in a bicycle race, and was taken into
the hospital almost a lifeless corpse. He pulled
through by the aid (as his testimonial showed) of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and is once more able to
ride the bicycle. He was in Clunes on Tuesday
last, in company with another well-known cyclist,
Matthews. He is completing a journey of 1000
miles for a wager of ,£5O. He is riding a Dayton
machine, the same frame that Walne rode so
many races with, fitted with Dunlop tyres. His
mate is riding a Dayton, fitted likewise. This is
Kirk’s first ride since he came out of the hospital.
He looks well, and one could hardly realise that
he was the same Dave Kirk who was smashed up
on the Scotch College track.”
Numbers of others print similar pars.
It should be mentioned that Dave Kirk intends
entering for all forthcoming wheel events, includ-
ing, of course, the Austral, the A.N.A., &c. One
can safely predict for Dave some brilliant feats
on the track. No man can command success, but
Dave certainly deserves it.
Here is a case that absolutely defied the best
medical skill of Melbourne. Here was a promis-
ing athlete reduced in a moment to a helpless mass
of quivering flesh, a victim to a combination of
diseases any one of which was enough to render
life intolerable. Look at him now, and behold
him not only raised up from a seeming death-bed,
but completely restored to his wonted remarkable
athletic powers—able to take the sporting field
once more, and fairly astound the public by per-
forming a cycle journey of 1042 miles, in the teeth
of hail, snow, rain, and wind of an unprecedented
severity. Not only so, but he gains a stone in
weight, and further strengthens every member of
his once-diseased body ! To conclude, what the
medical faculty and patent medicines utterly
failed to accomplish has been brought about
by a course of a remedy which, it can be
safely predicted, is destined to revolutionise
medicine, viz., Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People ! Is it any wonder, then, that the practic-
able thought of our up-to-date colonists has
grasped the intrinsic value of this medicine, and
that the manufacturer is experiencing the greatest
difficulty in keeping up with the heavy orders
pouring in daily from the wholesale houses in the
various Australasian colonies ? If, as the saying
goes, the proof of the pudding is in the eating, it
can be said that the popular demand for this, the
greatest remedy of the nineteenth century, is the
best guarantee of its worth as a cure for most of
the ills that flesh is heir to.

Castro’s Last Sacrament.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The scenario of Byers’ stories embraced four con-
tinents and bits of Polynesia. His geography was
correct, and his pathos had a finish that smacked
of art. Byers was a pilot—late of the Hoogli ser-
vice—and on the slightest provocation he would
pack you away in a deep-sea tramp, and send her
clanking like a boiler-yard over the horizon. Then
he would lean forward to hit you convincingly on
the breast-bone, and, presto ! you found yourself
marooned with Johnny, the cook, on a sand-bar,
outside Little Sheol in Malaysia. Marooning was
the leading feature in Byers’ stories, and his prin-
cipal characters were the Johnny aforesaid and a
wicked mining agent named Battle. Upon one
occasion he omitted both, thus :
The three jesters of the Orient Hotel were Cas-
tro, myself, and the big German, Schloss ; the
other man was Gilbert Herrick, a long-limbed
kiddy that the Torres Pearl Fishing Co. had
recruited from the Indian Survey Department.
The co. paid no divs. during Herrick’s regime ; he
was too easy, and used expensive gear, and
studied lives. There was a fleet in the Straits,
and 200 Japs to be controlled, and Herrick’s inex-
perience was pathetic. He could give you the
Himalayas’ area, or Indus soundings from Hyder-
abad to Lahore. As a boss pearl-raker, he wasn’t
sure whether an oyster walked upstairs or used
the lift. For being the newest chum you ever
struck, Herrick took all certificates.
• * * *
Castro’s Orient Hotel was under the bluff where
you signal ships from the Gulf side of the Straits ;
a lonesome place, with a lot of palm-jungle in the
back-yard, and a league or two of surf roaring
under the front window. There was a tiger-like
playfulness about. publican Castro, and if you’d
let him he’d maul and slaver over you like—like
Paolo Macari. No man with a nose like Castro’s
ever made friends. It was a hook between two
blinking eyes, and it was bone-white —perhaps it
lived in constant fear of being hit, and before you
knew him ten minutes you wanted to do it an
evil with both hands. There was another man, a
beach-combing pariah named Smith ; sometimes
he cleaned Castro’s windows, but he preferred to
cadge from the Jap divers. The personality of
Smith wouldn’t improve any story, and the less
said of Smith the better.
Schloss and I were very miserable at Castro’s
until the Torres Pearl Fishing Co. sent Herrick,
and the newness of him blew away the monotony.
The card-hunger vanished from the German’s
eyes ; he rolled walrus-like across the wide veran-
dah, making petty excuses for shaking hands. He
even forebore his daily yarn that connected a dog
and a sapper named Kolbs with the German siege-
lines around Paris. •
“ Der gots haf send us someding do blay mid,”
he chuckled. We had drinks. Herrick was in.
the bar, and publican Castro was talking. On the
counter was a cheese-knife, and Herrick was feel-
ing it gingerly with his thumb. Said Castro :
“ Thirty feelers and seven heads he had, and
that damned octopus was sparring for a hold. I
don’t mind sharks, a kiss of the hand sools ’em
off. Whales are regular gentlemen : you could
take your girl down and introduce her to ’em.
Now that damned octapuus ! ” —— ■
Schloss borrowed the knife to cut some tobacco.
“Whales are always worth looking at, aren’t
they?” said Herrick simply. Castro shook his
head.
“ We wouldn’t go outside to see ’em, would we,
Schloss ? ”
Schloss inferred that a school of thrashing bull-
whales was a dreary spectacle indeed.
* 0 * * *
Mrs. Castro permitted Schloss to address her
from a distance. Mrs. Castro was 17 and a Nea-
politan. She hated Schloss and his silly Dutch
talk, but, as Gilbert Herrick was smoking under
the palms, she stayed, with her back half-turned
to prove her utter indifference to Schloss. Now,
with her back to the Teuton she could see Her-
rick much better, and Herrick, she felt sure, was
looking at her in a manner that wasn’t strictly
proper. Schloss was saying things—in the first
place, he argued that her mother had robbed the
heavens to provide her with a pair of eyes. He
appealed to Herrick. Julia Castro frowned and
spoke Italian to Schloss. Schloss called her “the
immediate jewel of his soul.” Julia Castro didn’t
go away, she prowled amongst adjacent ferns and
shook a tiny white fist from afar. There was a
cross-shaped pearl on her second finger; Schloss
told me under his breath that years ago Castro
had knifed Yamshai Janneker for it, when Jan-
neker and he were working under Yanysuthen’s
reef—knifed him in 60 fathoms of water ! Julia
Castro didn’t know that. We watched her half-
an-hour later swimming in the surf.
* • * *
The thing came out of the jungle, and danced
from shadow to shadow. Schloss said it was a
man, because it was brown, and striped, and had
a tail. It frisked towards us and stopped.
“ Big Babuan jief,” said Schloss, turning to
Herrick, and, when Schloss pressed his heel on my
toe, I looked for Castro’s nose on the Papuan
chief’s face, and it was there, my friend. Castro’s
impersonation was a mixture of harlequin and
barn-fowl: he was a fleering bedevilled nightmare,
edged with ochre. There were turkey’s feathers
between his toes ; he halted in the strip of moon-
light, and slavered with the butt end of his tongue
between his teeth.
“ Good God ! ” I said.
“Ging of Babua,” droned Schloss. “He haf
galled to zee Herrig. He haf a hundred war
ganoes in der sthraids. Herrig, he haf galled on
you; id is dergusdom.” Schloss waved a hand
invitingly to the offensive object, and the object
pounded the earth with a waddy.
“I—er —I’m deeply grateful,” said Herrick,
going forward, “and I look upon the king’s visit
as an honor indeed ! ”
Herrick spoke earnestly, because he couldn’t
tell an Orang Kaya from a Maori pundit. He
was chockful of Stanleyism, was Herrick, and
considered the moment a favorable one for tighten-
ing that silken hawser about a loose and unregene-
rate king. Castro capered appropriately, and
moulted. He was real dirty, and you could whiff
the goosefat of last Xmas in his hair.
Herrick bowed, like Stanley.
“ Glad to meet you, king,” he said. The
alleged king preened himself and slavered.
Nothing that walks on two legs ever looked like
Castro ; the man had been down to Gehenna to
fill in his idea of a devil, and—barring the 101 l of
the head and the slaver there was a sinewy
grace about his masquerade that was convincing.
Talk about Dan Barry ; it was high art !
“ I’m only a 150 a-year man,” said Herrick,
anxiously ; “ but I suppose the beer-bin ordinaire
will fill the bill ? ”
The king frisked judiciously, moulted, and set-
tled on one leg.
“ What does that signify? ” whispered Herrick.
“ Id signify dot your remark haf hees abbroval,”
said Schloss. The king waved his waddy in a
favorable manner at Herrick. Herrick raised his
pith-helmet —like Stanley.
“’Ow are yer, Mister ’lckenbottom?” said
Smith, strolling near. “ Glad ter see you’ve come
up without yer horns. Take a seat, Mister ’lcken-
bottom ! ” There was a lot of crude dialogue about
Smith.
It was fitting that there should be an orgie. and
Smith sang ; no one even hinted that Smith
should sing, and when he commenced his noisy
melody on top of the rain-barrel we found it
necessary to remove him with stones. Herrick
conducted the king back to the jungle. Herrick
was impervious to beer, and the king was anxious
about his reputation.
“So long, king! ” Herrick touched his hand with
due magnificence.
“Ac—hie —Acheron,” breathed the man from
Papua. Herrick looked hard at Schloss.
“ Observe,” he said, “ how the aboriginal mind
jumps with Dante’s ! ”
The king fell into the jungle.
m * * *
A wisp of moon hung like a silver boomerang
across the straits. There was a full tide, too, and
you could hear it talking through the palms, and
on the reef. Schloss pointed seaward; and oh !
man, there was a liner tramping south-rooting it
white over her black muzzle, and snoring.
“Rangoon, China borts and Brisbane. She’s
8.1.,” grunted Schloss.
“She’s Tyne built,” said I, “and she has beam
without length, after the Quetta’s lines. She’s not
8.1. for all that, Schloss. Anyhow, she’ll be in-
side the Barrier before she loses the Cross. Give
us a song, Herrick ! ”
Herrick wouldn’t sing; but he stood out in the
open and recited —words to a woman that filled
the air with a halo of jewel, and scented dusk,
and although he only seemed to whisper, each
word was distinct above the clamor of the surf. I
could see that Julia Castro had come out to listen,
with her head flung back and her hands clenching
a screen of parasites. The crucifix-pearl glowed
on her second finger—the pearl that Castro had
knifed Yamshai Janneker for under Yanysuthen’s
reef. When Herrick stopped, she came out.
“What do you call those words ? ” Strangely
enough she addressed Schloss, but her eyes were
on the ground.
Schloss thought for a moment. She came nearer,
and the silk of her dress whistled like birds; man,
she was decked out fine that night!
“ The words ? ” she asked.
“ Herrig’s nide Biece to Yulia.”
“ Thank you, Herr Schloss ! ” And, without
looking at Herrick, she swept into her room.
“ Every ’Errick ’as ’is Julia!” said Smith,
feelingly. “ Yorter seen the little tart I struck at
Clontarf onct; Spud Maloney was onter it, too.
‘Spud,’ I sez, ‘if yer lookin’ fer a poke in the
eye, say so. Spud
The suppression of Smith was immediate.
Schloss kept both feet on him. And Schloss
talked and carried us through a wilderness of
words, to Gravelotte and Sedan, and from Sedan
to the sullen-faced embrasures of Maison Guyot,
until the crystal streaks lined the humps of barrier
in the east. Then came the king out of the jungle,
and we saw that nothing remained except sour
beer and Castro. He staggered to nis wife’s room
—Julia’s—and we overlooked the little circum-
stance until Herrick sprang up and said “ Oh,
Christ! ”
“Der ging haf gone in to Julia ! ” chuckled
Schloss.
• * • *
I felt the painful squeeze of his fist, and sat
silent. Here was a new turn to a dead joke !
Herrick leaped across the open space, and by the
writhing of his sinewy torso we relied upon
strange things happening to the king. Herrick
sailed into the room like a young steer, and the
next three minutes were filled with a series of sharp
grunts. Then Herrick spun out with his left arm
lifted and dropped to the ground.
“S-say, Byers!” he gasped, “ w-was that a
p-put-up job ? He’s given it me under the ribs,
old man, and I’m cooked.. . . . He’s half
white, and the paint’s coming off. Youdidnt
put it up for me—you or Schloss ? ”
Schloss swore. There was an ugly gash under
his left arm. We carried him into his room.
“ Mein Gott! ” wailed Schloss, “we haf killed
der boy. By-and-by, I tink we shall go into
Gastro ; I haf a sabre dot belong to my fader at
Marengo.”
We bandaged Herrick, and Julia Castro came
in- .
“ Oh, you too ! ” she said. “ Why do you play
with people who are quick ? ” There were tears in
her scowling, half-turned face. She stooped over
the bed and kissed Herrick between the eyes.
“ One of you shall pay for this ... or Castro the
butcher.’’ The last word, “ butcher,” stayed over
long between her pretty teeth. We found Castro
amongst the sheets, whimpering like a painted
gorilla. In the scrap Herrick had turned Castro’s
wrist, and sent the knife back up to the haft. He
doubled up when Julia came in, and whispered
something to me that I didn’t catch. She
smoothed his pillow, and spoke soothing words in
the lingua franca of the Levant. He watched her
like a cornered rat. She wept—he would not take a
little brandy ; he wouldn’t take anything from her
hands. She brought wine from the cellar, and of-
fered it with kisses and “ Caromio —caro inio.” A
little strength came back to Castro; he flung aside
the tumbler and screamed —Julia’s eyes filled with
tears of reproach—he quaked like a frightened
beast, until the painted sweat ran over the sheets-
Man, it was queer !
* * * *
Then the race-instinct came back—he wanted a
priest. We sent Smith. Smith didn’t want to go,
and argued :
“ Ickenbottom goes in fer ’igh-class comedy and
chivs ’Errick ; ’igh-class comedy’s good enough
fer me ; we’ll stick to it. ’E wants a priest ; now
you lend’s a razer, Mister Byers, an’ off goes me
goatee ; then gimme a sheet, an’, s’welp me Gawd,
I’ll ’ear ’is sins ! ” Schloss rolled forward half-a-
pace. I didn’t think Schloss would plug any
man. Smith went for the priest. And the surf-
manes were flying white over Vanysuthen’s reef
when Father Dominick Lynch rode up; he was
carrying something under his vestments. He re-
coiled from the painted effigy in the bed ; Schloss
explained. Then a dour look sent us to the
verandah, until Castro should have jabbered out
his sins. Julia stole from Herrick’s room, and I
saw by the droop of her head and the hand catch-
ing at her breast that Herrick had crossed the bar.
She stood by the window watching the priest in-
side. I spoke a few words ; she eyed me threaten-
ingly, and her hand, snatching at me across the
window-light, brought . with it the fear of a
poignard ; Schloss paled, he understood her breed
better than I.
The priest coughed twice —a signal that Castro’s
sins were out. Julia slid into the room, and I
o .

saw the crucifix-pearl gleam amongst the tapers'
Her hand made a lightning movement over the
communion chalice—the priest was praying beside
Castro—then she knelt and prayed also.
“May the blood of our Lord Jesus Christ!”
began the priest as Castro raised his head to
receive—pilot Byers quoted it in the Latin, but
that’s beyond me—“ preserve thy soul to ever-
lasting life. Amen ! ”
I never saw a man wriggle so after receiving
the Holy Eucharist; it seemed to fill him up.
He just caught himself by the throat and coughed
up the ghost. Man, it was queer !
“ Der vas brussig acid in dot sagrament ! ”
drawled Sehloss. Julia’s maiden name should
have been Borgia.
ALBA DORION.
M‘Culloch’s Chump.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas on a farm, a farm whose charm
Lay in the fact that binders
Got decent pay, six bob a day,
And no midnight reminders !
That is to say, we worked all day;
Worked hard, there’s no disguising;
But heard no. shout to rouse us out
Because the moon was rising !
’Twas in the days of simpler ways,
’Fore strippers struck Australia ;
When goose-neck bands and human hands
Beat all the paraphernalia !
There was a lout, a slack-built lout,
Was binding at M'Culloch’s,
Weighed sixteen stone, all brawn and bone,
Arid calves as big as bullocks !
This raw-boned dolt could break a colt,
And, if it came to fighting,
Gould take his part; but lacked the art
Of reading and of writing.
Said he to me one night at tea :
“ I hope my girl is better,
D’yer think yer’d find—that is— d’yer mind—
Er—would yer write a letter ? ”
The “things” were found. All hands sat round,
The raw-boned chap dictating.
“ Tell her,” said he, “ I hopes that she
Won’t get too tired o’ waiting.
“ You’d better tell her I’m quite well
And hope at time o’ startin’
That she’s the same. Yes, Meg’s her name,
I told yer Marg’ret Martin !
“ You got that down ? Well, say Jack Brown
Is chirpy as a plover ;
And, tell her—why—that—is—that I
You know—just say I love her !
“You might just say I ain’t too gay,
But anxious to be oyer.
Got that all right ? Well, now you might
Say just once more I love her !
“ What else ? Say chaff is three ’n’ a half
And season’s right for clover,
And spuds is down to half-a-crown,
And—better say I love her !
“ That’s all. You sign my name, Devine."
“ No more ? ” asked I, inditing.
He scratched his head and calmly said:
“ Put ‘ Please excuse bad writing ! ’ ”
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
The Tiotala.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I’m under obligation to you,” said the king-
fisher, loftily, from his perch in the tall tauneve
tree, which suggests a large-leaved poplar.
I should say he was. I had just rescued him,
by dint of censure and cajolery, from the atten-
tions of some juvenile natives, to whom he acted
as “ cup-and-ball,” with a string attached to his
leg. His beak was still battling with the remnan
of the string. I thought he need not have been
quite so patronising.
“Don’t let yourself be caught again,” I re-
marked, wishing to be impressive; “I might not
be there to ■rescue you. ”
That is true, and I repeat, I’m much obliged,”
he rasped—his voice always is like a rheumatic
rattle “ but these young Christians are very ob-
jectionable. There is no dodging them, some
days.”
“ well, you understand, I hope, that that is
the consequence, not of their being young
Christians, but the descendants of old heathens.”
The bird stopped fiddling at his small leg with
his big bill, and looked down at me knowingly.
If he had been his first-cousin, the Australian
jackass, he would have laughed outright.
“ White people do amuse me,” was what he
said.
“ White people are uncommonly useful to you,
anyway! Where would you be, pray, if there
were no white tourists to take an interest in you?
no white missionaries to teach the natives that
they must be kind to you? no white traders to
influence ” I stopped short. You see, I
wasn’t sure of my ground with the traders.
That cheeky bird began to plume his shiny blue
feathers, as if I were not speaking at all. Then
he tried to flirt his ridiculous, squat tail. Finally,
he shook himself, and looked at me again. lam
positive that he winked.
Evidently I had rescued a wicked old bird.
Only wicked old birds wink. I turned away.
“ Won’t you listen? ” whirred his Wickedness.
“ Not if you wink,” I retorted. My principles
are occasionally strong.”
I won’t wink; I’ll talk. What white people
call straight talk. Oh, rot this string ! ”
Where did you get your nice language,
pray ? ”
“From the whites, of course, like all other good
things ! Now listen : Christianity is a bad thing
for natives. Missionaries are bad things for
natives. They do a lot of harm. I know 1 ”
“ Oh, oh, oh ! ”
I was quite sorry I had rescued him. Evi-
dently he was an Evil Influence.
■ “ A good heathen is better than a bad Christian.
The missionaries make bad Christians. I know ! ”
“ How dare you,” I burst out, “ I won’t listen
to you any more. The Samoans make splendid
converts ! They go to church regularly, the more
services the better for them. They have family
prayers, Sunday-schools, native teachers, native
preachers; they sing hymns, shout prayers, hurl
sermons with all their lungs ”
“ And play with birds, and spear fish, and break
off crabs’ legs and strangle pigs and ”
“ That’s not the fault of the missionaries.”
“Yes it is.”
“ Not at all. They can’t help the people being
cruel. They tell them it’s wrong.”
“ The old heathen priests could help the people
being cruel. They told them not to dare.”
I sat straight down where I was, crushing a
whole clump of red coleus and almost annihilating
a colony of ants. The ants did not notice their
danger—and mine.
“ Perhaps,” I said in desperation, “ you’ll ex-
plain ? ”
“ With pleasure. I know! Didn’t my ances-
tors settle in Samoa, years ago, on the strength of
our knowledge? Samoa was a lovely place then.
The natives hadn’t seen a dollar, or a Bible, or a
white man—but they believed in the gods.”
“ And in going to war, and in human sacrifices,
and in CANNIBALISM? ”
_ “ It’s very rude to interrupt,” said the king-
fisher, suavely. “ White men have brought big
fighting-ships here. They sometimes kill each
other, and sometimes hang each other, and they
ma,ke other men work hard while alive, which is
quite as disagreeable as being eaten after you’re
dead.”
“ Well, but—those are not missionaries.”
“ No. And missionaries aren’t priests and
don’t teach natives to worship animals. That’s
how we had such good times. The gods took us
under their protection, and that, of course, made
our lives sacred. If you belong to a big god no-
body dares even to think of killing you, and if
you belong only to a little family-god nobody be-
longing to his family dares to kiil you, nor anyone
who does not care to quarrel with that family.
And if a great chief belongs to you and is called
by your name, nobody w'ho is at peace with him
dare kill you. Ah, we could build our nests in
safety then ! ”
“And, of course, you remember that in those
days nobody ever speared a fish, or lured a pigeon,
or cut up a pig alive, or”
“ You need not excite yourself,” observed that
provoking bird ; “ I am only talking for your
benefit, and I certainly don't remember what
didn t occur. Thel-h .were always some people
to eat some animals; and, if it’s done in reason,
I’m not the one to object. I enjoy a fresh fish
as much as anyone, but then I only want a little
fellow now and again, and he’s not missed. The
larger ones are sa to me, because I can’t swallow
them. And a lot of fish used to be sa, because
people dared not swallow them. And if one family
fi • r^)l<^en Mullet, another sea-urchin, and a
third bonito, can’t you see what a difference it
makes?”
‘They don’t eat small birds now,” I put in, in-
consequently.
No; they only kill them by dragging them
round with a string ! Drat ... Oh ! I forgot,
you re particular about language ! Fancy drag-
a s^r ' n o * n the dear old heathen days ?
ihe days when the owl was reverenced in the
grove, and the Devil was in the cuttlefish, and
even a fowl had a chance of being apologised to if
it were baked. As for us kingfishers” The
bird hopped higher and caught the sunrays on
his jewelled feathers, and scratched his head pen-
sively as old folks will when recalling the past.
I hen he raised his harsh voice :
“ Tiotala ! Tiotala ! ”
‘What is that for?” I said, peevishly; “I
don t want any more of your chattering round.”
I was only calling the other tiotalas to see if
they remember about it. Ah, the tine times we
had ! Why, I have known a whole army turn
back just because they spied me sitting on a bough
on a wet day. And I have fluttered ahead of
®uem with the sunbeams flashing on my feathers
and the whole troop capering behind, their skins
glistening and their leaf girdles tossing, as they
brandished their clubs and shouted that I was
leading them on to victory. Dear, dear ! I was
a kind of war-god myself, then. The man who
hurt me would have been killed straight off.”
‘ The missionaries have been very happy and
comfortable here for 60 years, and you remember
things that happened before they came? You
must have singular facilities for living ! ”
You see, I was getting nasty. His remarks put
me out.
‘ I am the old Greek Halcyon,” he answered,
blandly, “and I have the secret of Y outh. You
ought to know that. Yet, how should you ? They
were pagans—healthy, wholesome old pagans, who
understood that God and man and beast are all
lirilvecl together, and acting accordingly. You
Christians— ”
Belong to the right religion,” I interrupted,
firmly.
“ Oh, of course ! But, I put it to yourself: If
one religion held you sacred, and the other held
you by a string, which would you favor? ”
“ I don’t believe you are a genuine bird at all,”
I said, getting up hastily, for the ant colony had
discovered me ; “ I’m really sorry I had anything
to_do with you. I believe you’re just a wretched
old pagan re-incarnation.”
“ H-sh-sh-sh ! ” said the Tiotala. “Who’s
using nice language now? It sounds just like
swe.armg. And if you are going, will you please
undo this knot in the string.”
I did. And left him with a knot in my brain.
The wind was whistling through the ff-trees, and
as they bent it whispered—
“ He prayeth best who loveth best
All creatures, great and small.”
